## Assistant (2026-04-20 20:34:14)

I’m pushing on the branch-label issue now. The question is whether the born branch carries some invariant that the outer rooted branch in the two-odd-zero model cannot share.

_Thought for 9m 6s_

Yes. There is one real step further, and it sharpens the bottleneck again.

I do **not** see an outright contradiction yet, but I think the branch-label issue can be reduced to a more concrete precursor event:

\[
\boxed{\text{in the exactly-two-odd-zero case, any first root must be preceded by a nonrooted critical fold.}}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “maybe the born branch jumps to the outer branch.”

Here is the clean argument.

---

Work in the normalized nonmixed chart and let
\[
t=\tau(a)
\]
denote the analytic continuation of the critical branch born at the first odd zero \(b\). Set
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),
\qquad
H(a):=\mathcal Z(\tau(a))-D_a(\tau(a)).
\]
Then along any simple critical branch,
\[
D_a'(\tau(a))=0,
\qquad
D_a''(\tau(a))=\frac{2}{(\tau(a)-a)^2}H(a).
\]

So the sign of \(D_a''\) is exactly the sign of \(H(a)\).

Now assume the minimal unresolved configuration:

- \(b<c\) are adjacent odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- the born branch continues until a first rooted point \((a_*,\tau(a_*))\),
- and the segment \([a_*,\tau(a_*)]\) contains exactly those two odd zeros \(b,c\).

Let
\[
\sigma:=\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R
\quad\text{on the initial lobe }(a_*,b).
\]
Then the sign pattern on \([a_*,\tau(a_*)]\) is
\[
\sigma,\ -\sigma,\ \sigma.
\]

### 1. Near birth, the born branch has the opposite curvature type

Before the segment reaches \(c\), one is in the one-sign-change chamber. The earlier transport corollary gives
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(a)=\text{sign of the terminal lobe}=-\sigma.
\]
Hence near the seed,
\[
\boxed{\operatorname{sgn}D_a''(\tau(a))=-\sigma.}
\]

So the born branch starts as one critical type: max-type if \(\sigma=+\), min-type if \(\sigma=-\).

### 2. At an exactly-two-odd-zero rooted point, the sign flips

At a rooted point,
\[
G(a_*)=0,
\qquad
D_{a_*}'(\tau(a_*))=0.
\]
By the moving-shear theorem already in the draft, rootedness forces two interior points
\[
r_1<r_2\in(a_*,\tau(a_*))
\]
with
\[
\mathcal Z(r_1)=\mathcal Z(r_2)=\mathcal Z(a_*).
\]

Because there are exactly two odd zeros on the segment, \(\mathcal Z\) is monotone on each of the three lobes. Therefore those two level returns must lie as
\[
r_1\in(b,c),
\qquad
r_2\in(c,\tau(a_*)).
\]
So \(\tau(a_*)\) lies on the terminal lobe, where \(\mathcal Z-\mathcal Z(a_*)\) has sign \(\sigma\). Hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\bigl(\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*)\bigr)=\sigma.
\]
Since rootedness gives \(D_{a_*}(\tau(a_*))=\mathcal Z(a_*)\), this is
\[
H(a_*)=\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{\operatorname{sgn}D_{a_*}''(\tau(a_*))=\sigma.}
\]

Thus the born branch starts with
\[
\operatorname{sgn}D''=-\sigma
\]
but at the first exactly-two-odd-zero root it has
\[
\operatorname{sgn}D''=\sigma.
\]

So a sign change of \(D''\) is unavoidable.

### 3. Therefore there must be an earlier nonrooted fold

By continuity, there exists an earlier anchor \(a_c\) with
\[
a_*<a_c<b
\]
such that along the continued born branch
\[
D_{a_c}'(\tau(a_c))=0,
\qquad
D_{a_c}''(\tau(a_c))=0.
\]
Because \(a_*\) was the **first rooted** point, one still has
\[
D_{a_c}(\tau(a_c))\neq \mathcal Z(a_c),
\]
i.e.
\[
G(a_c)\neq 0.
\]

So the precursor event is a **nonrooted degenerate critical point**.

That is already a sharper theorem target than “branch transfer.”

And one can say more. Let
\[
F(a,t):=D_a'(t).
\]
At such a precursor point \((a_c,t_c)\), with \(t_c:=\tau(a_c)\), one has
\[
F(a_c,t_c)=0,
\qquad
F_t(a_c,t_c)=D_{a_c}''(t_c)=0,
\]
but
\[
F_a(a_c,t_c)
=
-\frac{2}{(t_c-a_c)^2}\bigl(D_{a_c}(t_c)-\mathcal Z(a_c)\bigr)
=
-\frac{2G(a_c)}{(t_c-a_c)^2}\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
F_{tt}(a_c,t_c)
=
D_{a_c}'''(t_c)
=
\frac{2}{(t_c-a_c)^2}\mathcal R(t_c).
\]
So generically
\[
F_{tt}(a_c,t_c)\neq 0.
\]

Therefore the critical-locus equation
\[
D_a'(t)=0
\]
has a standard fold there:
\[
a-a_c
=
\frac{\mathcal R(t_c)}{2G(a_c)}(t-t_c)^2
+o\!\left((t-t_c)^2\right).
\]

So the precise remaining mechanism is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{born branch } \longrightarrow \text{ nonrooted critical fold } \longrightarrow \text{ outer rooted branch.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest continuation of the attack I can justify.

---

So the bottleneck has narrowed again.

It is no longer just “adjacent odd-zero connection,” and not just “first post-birth fold.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{nonrooted critical-fold exclusion in the exactly-two-odd-zero chamber.}
}
\]

In plain terms: prove that the born simple critical branch cannot hit a fold
\[
D_a'(\tau(a))=0,\qquad D_a''(\tau(a))=0,\qquad D_a(\tau(a))\neq \mathcal Z(a)
\]
before any rooting occurs, while the segment contains exactly two odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

That feels like the real next theorem.

The reason I think this is a meaningful advance is that it replaces the vague “maybe the branch switches” picture by a very rigid local model: any switch must occur through a codimension-one fold of the critical locus with explicit formulas for its orientation.

If you want, I’ll turn this into exact LaTeX insertions for the current draft: one proposition for the precursor-fold necessity, one proposition for the local fold normal form of \(D_a'(t)=0\), and a summary replacement updating the remaining burden accordingly.
## User (2026-04-20 20:45:34)

ok continue the attack

## Assistant (2026-04-20 20:45:36)

I’m pushing on the new precursor event now. The question is whether a nonrooted critical fold is itself impossible in the two-odd-zero chamber, or whether it remains a formally realizable local model.

_Thought for 5m 11s_

Yes — I think this one actually goes through.

The new point is that the precursor fold you identified is not just necessary; in the **exactly-two-odd-zero chamber** it has the **wrong orientation** to lie on the born branch. That excludes the two-odd-zero first-root scenario.

Here is the clean argument.

---

Work on the analytic critical branch born at the first odd zero \(b\), and write
\[
t=\tau(a),\qquad
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
H(a):=\mathcal Z(\tau(a))-D_a(\tau(a)).
\]
Along the branch,
\[
D_a'(\tau(a))=0,\qquad
D_a''(\tau(a))=\frac{2}{(\tau(a)-a)^2}H(a).
\]

Let \(c>b\) be the next odd zero of \(\mathcal R\), and assume for contradiction that the born branch reaches a **first rooted point**
\[
(a_*,\tau(a_*))
\]
such that the open segment \((a_*,\tau(a_*))\) contains exactly the two odd zeros \(b,c\).

Let
\[
\sigma:=\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R
\quad\text{on the initial lobe }(a_*,b).
\]
Then the sign pattern of \(\mathcal R\) on \((a_*,\tau(a_*))\) is
\[
\sigma,\ -\sigma,\ \sigma.
\]

### 1. The born branch starts with \(H=-\sigma\)

Before the segment reaches \(c\), one is in the one-sign-change chamber. The earlier transport corollary gives
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(a)= -\sigma
\]
there. So near birth,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}D_a''(\tau(a))=-\sigma.
\]

### 2. At the rooted point one has \(H=+\sigma\)

At the rooted point,
\[
G(a_*)=0,
\qquad
D_{a_*}'(\tau(a_*))=0.
\]
The moving-shear rooted theorem forces two interior points
\[
r_1<r_2\in(a_*,\tau(a_*))
\]
with
\[
\mathcal Z(r_1)=\mathcal Z(r_2)=\mathcal Z(a_*).
\]
Because there are exactly two odd zeros and \(\mathcal Z\) is monotone on each of the three lobes, those two returns must lie one in the middle lobe and one in the terminal lobe. Hence \(\tau(a_*)\) lies beyond the terminal return, so
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\bigl(\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*)\bigr)=\sigma.
\]
Since rootedness gives \(D_{a_*}(\tau(a_*))=\mathcal Z(a_*)\),
\[
H(a_*)=\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*) ,
\]
hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(a_*)=\sigma.
\]

So \(H\) changes sign from \(-\sigma\) near birth to \(+\sigma\) at the first rooted point.

### 3. Therefore there is an earlier nonrooted fold

By continuity, there exists
\[
a_c\in(a_*,b)
\]
such that
\[
H(a_c)=0.
\]
Let \(t_c:=\tau(a_c)\). Since \(a_*\) was the first rooted point, one still has
\[
G(a_c)\neq 0.
\]
Thus
\[
D_{a_c}'(t_c)=0,\qquad D_{a_c}''(t_c)=0,\qquad D_{a_c}(t_c)\neq \mathcal Z(a_c),
\]
so \((a_c,t_c)\) is a **nonrooted critical fold**.

The local fold normal form for the critical locus \(D_a'(t)=0\) is
\[
a-a_c=
\frac{\mathcal R(t_c)}{2G(a_c)}(t-t_c)^2+o\!\left((t-t_c)^2\right).
\]

So the whole issue is the sign of
\[
\frac{\mathcal R(t_c)}{G(a_c)}.
\]

### 4. In the exactly-two-odd-zero chamber that coefficient is positive

Because \(H(a_c)=0\),
\[
D_{a_c}(t_c)=\mathcal Z(t_c).
\]
So with the frozen level \(\gamma=\mathcal Z(t_c)\), the pair \((a_c,t_c)\) is a rooted sheared point. The sheared theorem therefore forces two interior points
\[
u_1<u_2\in(a_c,t_c)
\]
with
\[
\mathcal Z(u_1)=\mathcal Z(u_2)=\mathcal Z(t_c).
\]

Since there are exactly two odd zeros \(b<c\) in \((a_c,t_c)\), and \(\mathcal Z\) is monotone on each lobe, these two interior returns can only lie:

- one on the **initial** lobe \((a_c,b)\),
- one on the **middle** lobe \((b,c)\),

and none on the terminal lobe before \(t_c\), because the terminal lobe is monotone and already ends at the level point \(t_c\).

Hence on the initial lobe, the level \(\mathcal Z(t_c)\) is crossed once after the anchor. Therefore
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\bigl(\mathcal Z(t_c)-\mathcal Z(a_c)\bigr)=\sigma.
\]
That is,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G(a_c)=\sigma.
\]
Also \(t_c\) lies on the terminal lobe, so
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(t_c)=\sigma.
\]
Therefore
\[
\frac{\mathcal R(t_c)}{G(a_c)}>0.
\]

So the fold normal form is
\[
a-a_c = C (t-t_c)^2+o((t-t_c)^2)
\qquad\text{with } C>0.
\]

This means the critical locus near \((a_c,t_c)\) exists only for
\[
a\ge a_c,
\]
not for \(a<a_c\).

### 5. Contradiction

But the born branch is being followed by decreasing the anchor from \(b\) down toward \(a_*\), and we had
\[
a_*<a_c<b.
\]
So to reach the supposed rooted point at \(a_*\), the born branch would have to continue through the fold to anchors \(a<a_c\). The local fold normal form shows that this is impossible: the critical locus ends at \(a_c\) on that side.

Contradiction.

---

## Conclusion

So the exactly-two-odd-zero scenario is excluded:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the born repeated-source critical branch cannot reach its first rooted point
while the segment contains exactly two odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

Equivalently, the branch cannot acquire its first genuine moving-shear root when the source segment contains exactly two odd zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\).

That is a real advance. It sharpens the remaining burden from

\[
\text{“exclude adjacent odd-zero connection”}
\]

to

\[
\boxed{
\text{“the first rooted point, if it exists, must occur only after the segment has acquired at least three odd zeros.”}
}
\]

And it also explains why the earlier cubic model was not a counterexample: it showed an outer rooted branch with exactly two odd zeros, but not a rooted point reached by the **born** branch. The obstruction is exactly the fold orientation above.

If you want, I’ll turn this into exact LaTeX insertions for the current draft.

can you attack this further

## Assistant (2026-04-20 20:52:26)

I’m checking the exact statements around the shear-transfer and rooted-point propositions so I don’t overreach past what the draft actually proves.

_Thought for 6m 0s_

Yes — one step further, but this time the result is a **ceiling theorem** rather than a new exclusion.

Assuming the exact-two-odd-zero first-fold exclusion is right, the next thing you can prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first locally compatible rooting chamber is the exactly-three-odd-zero chamber.}
}
\]

That is useful because it tells you where the current branch really stops.

Let the born branch be
\[
t=\tau(a),
\]
with
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
H(a):=\mathcal Z(\tau(a))-D_a(\tau(a)).
\]
Recall:
\[
D_a'(\tau(a))=0,\qquad
D_a''(\tau(a))=\frac{2}{(\tau(a)-a)^2}H(a),
\]
and at a rooted point,
\[
G(a_*)=0,\qquad
G'(a_*)=-\mathcal R(a_*),\qquad
\tau'(a_*)=0,\qquad
\tau''(a_*)=-\frac{\mathcal R(a_*)}{\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*)}.
\]

Now write
\[
\sigma:=\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R
\]
on the initial lobe \((a,b)\), where \(b\) is the first odd zero.

Near birth, in the one-sign-change chamber, the earlier transport theorem gives
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G=\sigma,
\qquad
\operatorname{sgn}H=-\sigma.
\]

That is the starting orientation of the born branch.

Now suppose a first rooted point occurs when the segment contains exactly **three** odd zeros. Then the lobe signs are
\[
\sigma,\ -\sigma,\ \sigma,\ -\sigma.
\]
At such a rooted point, the terminal lobe has sign \(-\sigma\), so
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\bigl(\mathcal Z(\tau(a_*))-\mathcal Z(a_*)\bigr)=-\sigma,
\]
hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(a_*)=-\sigma.
\]

So unlike the exactly-two case, there is **no sign flip** in \(H\). The born branch starts with
\[
H=-\sigma
\]
and reaches the rooted point still with
\[
H=-\sigma.
\]

That kills the fold-orientation contradiction you used in the two-odd-zero case.

In fact all the local signs now line up in the **compatible** direction:

\[
G \text{ starts with sign } \sigma,\qquad G(a_*)=0,\qquad G'(a_*)=-\sigma,
\]
so \(G\) can simply decrease transversely to zero.

Also
\[
\tau''(a_*)=-\frac{\sigma}{-\sigma}>0,
\]
so the target map can flatten and attain a local minimum at the first root — exactly the correct orientation for a branch being continued by decreasing \(a\).

And for the moving-shear zero set
\[
\mathscr F(a,t)=A_a(t)-\mathcal Z(a)B_a(t)=0,
\]
the fold coefficient is
\[
\alpha''(t_*)=
\frac{2\bigl(\mathcal Z(t_*)-\mathcal Z(a_*)\bigr)}
{(t_*-a_*)^2\,\mathcal R(a_*)},
\]
so in the exactly-three-odd-zero case,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\alpha''=-1.
\]
That means the moving-shear fold opens toward
\[
a\le a_*,
\]
which is precisely the side from which the born branch approaches.

So the current local transport/fold package does **not** obstruct the exactly-three-odd-zero first root. It actually predicts that this is the first chamber where the local geometry becomes compatible.

That gives a clean meta-conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{after the exact-two exclusion, the present scalar branch is exhausted.}
}
\]

The remaining obstruction is no longer local sign geometry. It has to be something genuinely more global or more source-coupled.

So the next live theorem is not another parity/fold sign theorem. It is something like:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{post-boundary-exit three-odd-zero exclusion for the source-pinned moving shear }f_a.
}
\]

In concrete terms, I think there are only two serious ways to continue:

First, a **branch-label invariant**. You need something that distinguishes the born critical branch from the outer rooted branch even when the local fold geometry is compatible. This would be some source-level orientation index or determinant sign that is preserved under continuation and cannot match the rooted branch.

Second, a **boundary-tracking theorem for the frozen shear** \(\gamma=\mathcal Z(a)\). The draft already says the first rooted point must occur beyond the maximal fixed-level slope-overlap chamber. So the next move is to track the chamber boundary as \(a\) moves and show that the born branch cannot outrun it into the three-odd-zero rooted region.

That is the real next direction. Not more kernel moments.

So the honest answer is: yes, one step further — enough to identify the **exactly-three-odd-zero chamber** as the first locally admissible one — but beyond that, this line needs a new invariant.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 00:05:58)

_Thought for 1m 7s_

I think this is a real upgrade. The new proposition is clean, and the proof looks right: once you have
\[
G'(a)=\frac{2}{\tau(a)-a}G(a)-\mathcal R(a),
\]
the “largest root” barrier argument is exactly the right move. It replaces the earlier geometric “outer-lobe” phrasing with the intrinsic condition that the anchor-side value \(\mathcal R(a)\) keeps a fixed sign, which is much better mathematically and conceptually. In that sense, this is not just a cosmetic edit; it genuinely strengthens the route-2 reduction. 

The corollary is also the right downstream use of it. At the chamber-entry point \(a_0\), the segment \([a_0,q]\) still sees only the earlier odd zero \(b\), so the one-sign-change theorem gives the initial sign of \(G(a_0)\), and then the new proposition propagates that sign through the whole continuation interval as long as \(\mathcal R(a)\) does not change sign. That really does kill the standard adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber itself, not just the entry event. So the updated remark that route 2 now reduces to an **anchor-sign-change crossing problem** feels accurate to me. 

I would keep one caution in the wording: the result is strong, but it is still conditional on having a real-analytic continuation interval \(I\) of the born branch on which the segment contains exactly those two sign changes and \(\mathcal R(a)\) keeps a fixed sign. So I would avoid any sentence that sounds like “the two-odd-zero chamber is solved globally” without that qualifier. What you *can* say confidently is: on any such continuation interval, no rooted crossing can occur. That is exactly the right level of claim. 

As drafting taste, I think this update supersedes the earlier “global no-crossing” caveat and makes the residual bottleneck much sharper: the first remaining unresolved event is when the anchor itself reaches an odd zero of \(\mathcal R\) or the branch exits/collapses before then. That is a much better handoff point for the next attack. So overall: yes, I think this update is good, correct, and worth merging.

## User (2026-04-21 01:10:46)

ok whats the next attack?

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:10:48)

I think the draft has reduced the nonmixed repeated-source lane to a very specific moment now: what happens when the continued born branch reaches an interval where the anchor itself crosses an odd zero of \(\mathcal R\). I’m going to frame the next attack as a theorem target, not just a vague route.

_Thought for 2m 10s_

The next attack is now very sharp:

**prove an odd-anchor passage theorem.**

At this point, every interval where the anchor-side value \(\mathcal R(a)\) keeps a fixed sign is sterile for rooting, by the new anchor-sign proposition. So the only live place a rooted crossing can still occur is when the continued born branch reaches an anchor \(q\) that is itself an odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

That is the next bottleneck.

Write the continued born branch as
\[
t=\tau(a),\qquad
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),
\qquad
L(a):=\tau(a)-a.
\]
You already have the exact transport equation
\[
G'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)}\,G(a)-\mathcal R(a).
\]
Equivalently, with integrating factor
\[
A(a):=\int^a \frac{2}{L(s)}\,ds,
\]
this becomes
\[
\bigl(e^{-A(a)}G(a)\bigr)'=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
\]

So near an odd anchor zero \(q\) of \(\mathcal R\), the local behavior is completely explicit:
\[
G(a)=e^{A(a)-A(q)}
\left(
G(q)-\frac{e^{-A(q)}\mathcal R'(q)}{2}(a-q)^2+O((a-q)^3)
\right).
\]

This tells you something important.

If \(G(q)\) is known, then the post-\(q\) behavior is known:

- if \(G(q)\neq 0\), its sign persists locally across \(q\);
- if \(G(q)=0\), then the zero is quadratic and its sign on both sides is controlled by \(-\mathcal R'(q)\).

So the whole route reduces to one scalar datum at each anchor odd zero:

\[
\boxed{G(q)=D_q(\tau(q))-\mathcal Z(q).}
\]

That is the next attack. Not more planar kernel algebra.

The theorem to try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Odd-anchor sign reset theorem.}
}
\]

Let \(q\) be an odd zero of \(\mathcal R\) reached by the continued born branch. Then the value
\[
G(q)
\]
has the sign needed to restart the anchor-sign no-crossing theorem on the next interval of fixed sign of \(\mathcal R\).

There are two versions worth aiming at.

The strong version is
\[
\operatorname{sgn} G(q)
=
\operatorname{sgn}\bigl(\mathcal R(a)\bigr)
\quad\text{on the outgoing side of }q.
\]
That would immediately restart the anchor-sign proposition past \(q\), and then you could iterate from odd zero to odd zero.

The weaker version is
\[
G(q)=0
\quad\text{and}\quad
-\mathcal R'(q)
\]
has the outgoing sign, because then the quadratic expansion above gives the correct sign just past \(q\), which again restarts the anchor-sign proposition.

Why this is the right source-coupled theorem is that \(G(q)\) is not determined by the planar normalized model alone. It is exactly the rooted defect of the source-pinned moving shear at anchor \(q\):
\[
G(q)\sim D_{f_q}(q,h_q),
\qquad
f_q=u_5-\mathcal Z(q)\lambda.
\]
And the draft already contains the local theorem that near an odd zero, the rooted numerator for the fixed anchor shear has a definite nonzero sign on the distinguished opposite-side branch. So the missing bridge is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{identify the RH continuation }(q,\tau(q))
\text{ with that distinguished source-pinned branch.}
}
\]

That is the real next theorem.

In practical terms, I would attack it in this order.

First, prove a **landing lemma**: as the continued born branch reaches an anchor odd zero \(q\), the target \(\tau(q)\) lies on the distinguished opposite-side branch for the frozen shear
\[
f_q=u_5-\mathcal Z(q)\lambda.
\]

Second, combine that with the existing local unrooted-defect theorem at odd zeros to get the sign of
\[
D_{f_q}(q,h_q),
\]
hence the sign of \(G(q)\).

Third, feed that sign into the expansion of \(G\) across \(q\), and then reapply the anchor-sign proposition on the next fixed-sign interval.

If that works, route 2 closes by induction over anchor odd zeros on the opened nonmixed arc.

So the next attack is not “find another contradiction in the two-odd-zero chamber.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{source-pinned odd-anchor passage / sign-reset theorem.}
}
\]

That is the theorem most likely to finish this branch.
## User (2026-04-21 01:14:30)

attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:14:32)

I’m going straight at the odd-anchor passage step: either the continued RH branch can be shown to land on the distinguished source-pinned local branch at the next odd anchor zero, or we’ll see exactly where that identification still fails.

_Thought for 3m 55s_

I attacked the proposed **odd-anchor sign-reset theorem**, and I think the honest result is:

\[
\boxed{\text{the sign does \emph{not} reset at an odd anchor zero.}}
\]

What is true is the opposite:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{odd-anchor passage preserves the incoming sign of }G
\text{ and excludes rooting at the anchor zero itself.}}
\]

That is a useful theorem, but it means the next obstruction is more global than the hoped “sign reset.”

---

Let
\[
t=\tau(a)
\]
be the continued born repeated-source critical branch, and set
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad L(a):=\tau(a)-a.
\]
You already have
\[
G'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)}\,G(a)-\mathcal R(a).
\]
Introduce the integrating factor
\[
A(a):=\int^a \frac{2}{L(s)}\,ds,
\qquad
H(a):=e^{-A(a)}G(a).
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
H'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
}
\]

Now let \(q\) be an isolated odd-order zero of \(\mathcal R\), say
\[
\mathcal R(a)=c(a-q)^m+O((a-q)^{m+1}),
\qquad m\ge 1 \text{ odd},\quad c\neq 0.
\]

Assume you come into \(q\) along an interval on which \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign \(\varepsilon\in\{\pm1\}\), and by the anchor-sign proposition already in the draft,
\[
\varepsilon\,G(a)>0
\]
on that incoming interval.

Then:

### 1. \(G(q)\) cannot vanish

If \(G(q)=0\), then \(H(q)=0\), and integrating from \(q\) gives
\[
H(a)
=
-\int_q^a e^{-A(s)}\mathcal R(s)\,ds.
\]
Hence
\[
H(a)
=
-\frac{e^{-A(q)}c}{m+1}(a-q)^{m+1}
+o\!\left(|a-q|^{m+1}\right).
\]
Because \(m+1\) is even, the sign of this leading term is
\[
-\operatorname{sgn}(c),
\]
the same on both sides of \(q\).

But on the incoming side,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(a)=\operatorname{sgn}(c)=\varepsilon,
\]
while \(H\) has the same sign as \(G\), so the anchor-sign theorem says
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(a)=\varepsilon.
\]
Contradiction.

So
\[
\boxed{G(q)\neq 0.}
\]

### 2. In fact \(G(q)\) keeps the incoming sign

Since \(G\) has fixed sign \(\varepsilon\) on the incoming side and is continuous,
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}G(q)=\varepsilon.
}
\]

### 3. Therefore there is no immediate rooted crossing after the anchor odd zero

On the outgoing side of \(q\), the sign of \(\mathcal R\) flips, because \(q\) is an odd zero:
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(a)=-\varepsilon
\]
just past \(q\).

But \(G(q)=\varepsilon\neq 0\), so by continuity,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G(a)=\varepsilon
\]
on a small outgoing neighborhood of \(q\).

Thus just after the anchor odd zero,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G(a)=-\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(a),
\]
and in particular
\[
\boxed{
G(a)\neq 0
\quad\text{for }a\text{ sufficiently close to }q\text{ on the outgoing side.}
}
\]

So a rooted crossing cannot occur **at** the anchor odd zero, and cannot occur **immediately after** it either.

---

That gives the real theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Odd-anchor no-reset / delayed-crossing theorem.}
}
\]

A good paper-style statement would be:

> Let \(q\) be an isolated odd-order zero of \(\mathcal R\), and let \(t=\tau(a)\) be a real-analytic continuation of the born repeated-source critical branch up to \(a=q\). Assume on one side of \(q\) that \(\mathcal R(a)\) has fixed sign and that \(G(a)\) has the same sign, as provided by the anchor-sign theorem. Then \(G(q)\neq 0\), indeed \(G(q)\) has that same incoming sign. Consequently there is a neighborhood of \(q\) on the opposite side on which \(G\) remains nonzero.

This is a real advance, but it also shows the hoped “odd-anchor sign reset” route is not the right theorem. The defect has **memory**: it carries its incoming sign through the odd anchor zero.

So the residual problem sharpens again. It is no longer:

\[
\text{“what happens at the anchor odd zero?”}
\]

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{delayed post-anchor-crossing no-crossing.}
}
\]

In other words, after the anchor crosses an odd zero, the RH-selected slice does **not** reset into the safe sign configuration; instead it starts on the wrong side and can only cross later, if at all, after enough weighted outgoing \(\mathcal R\)-mass accumulates.

Using the integrating factor, that later crossing is exactly the condition
\[
H(a)=H(q)-\int_q^a e^{-A(s)}\mathcal R(s)\,ds=0.
\]
So the next theorem to try is a genuine **weighted-area domination** statement:

\[
\boxed{
H(q)>\int_a^q e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds
}
\]
through the relevant outgoing interval, or some source-coupled substitute for it.

That feels like the true next attack now. If you want, I’ll turn the odd-anchor no-reset theorem and its corollary into exact LaTeX insertions for the current draft.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:20:13)

I’m pushing on the post-anchor-crossing phase now. The natural target is whether the integrated transport equation can prevent a later zero of the rooted defect, or whether the current scalar data still allow one in principle.

_Thought for 4m 16s_

Yes. I can push it to a very sharp criterion.

I do not think the current scalar machinery gives the contradiction yet, but it reduces the remaining obstruction to one exact weighted-mass inequality.

Write the continued born branch as
\[
t=\tau(a),\qquad
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
L(a):=\tau(a)-a>0,
\]
and introduce the integrating factor
\[
\Xi(a):=e^{-A(a)}G(a),
\qquad
A'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)}.
\]
Then the transport equation becomes
\[
\Xi'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
\]

Now let
\[
q_0>q_1>q_2>\cdots
\]
be the successive odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) encountered by the anchor as \(a\) decreases along the continued branch, and let
\[
I_j:=(q_{j+1},q_j).
\]
On each \(I_j\), \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign; write
\[
\epsilon_j:=\operatorname{sgn}(\mathcal R|_{I_j})\in\{\pm1\}.
\]
Define the weighted lobe-mass
\[
m_j(a):=\int_a^{q_j} e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds,
\qquad
M_j:=m_j(q_{j+1}).
\]
Then on \(I_j\),
\[
\boxed{
\Xi(a)=\Xi(q_j)+\epsilon_j\,m_j(a).
}
\]

That one formula is the real reduction.

It gives three exact consequences.

First, on a “safe” interval where
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Xi(q_j)=\epsilon_j,
\]
one has
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Xi(a)=\epsilon_j
\qquad(a\in I_j),
\]
so no root can occur there.

Second, on a “dangerous” interval where
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Xi(q_j)=-\epsilon_j,
\]
a root occurs on \(I_j\) if and only if
\[
\boxed{
M_j>|\Xi(q_j)|.
}
\]
If equality held, the root would be at the endpoint \(q_{j+1}\), but the odd-anchor no-reset argument rules that out. So interior rooting is exactly strict domination of the incoming defect by the weighted lobe-mass.

Third, at the next odd zero,
\[
\boxed{
\Xi(q_{j+1})=\Xi(q_j)+\epsilon_j M_j.
}
\]
So the whole continuation is a discrete alternating-mass recursion.

This is already enough to explain everything we’ve seen:

- the one-sign-change chamber is safe because the initial sign matches;
- after the first anchor odd zero, the next interval is dangerous;
- the first rooted crossing can happen only if the first dangerous lobe wins the inequality
  \[
  M_1>|\Xi(q_1)|.
  \]

And because
\[
\Xi(q_1)=\Xi(q_0)+\epsilon_0 M_0
\]
with \(\epsilon_0=-\epsilon_1\), that first dangerous root is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
M_1>\Xi(q_0)+M_0
}
\]
after choosing the initial sign convention positively.

So the next live theorem is now very concrete:

> prove that a dangerous lobe cannot dominate the accumulated incoming weighted defect.

That is the exact post-anchor-crossing obstruction.

There is also a useful induction pattern. If no root occurs on a dangerous interval \(I_j\), then
\[
0<|\Xi(q_{j+1})|=|\Xi(q_j)|-M_j.
\]
On the following safe interval \(I_{j+1}\),
\[
|\Xi(q_{j+2})|=|\Xi(q_{j+1})|+M_{j+1}.
\]
So after each dangerous-safe pair,
\[
\boxed{
|\Xi(q_{j+2})|=|\Xi(q_j)|-M_j+M_{j+1}.
}
\]
This means that if one could prove a pairwise dominance
\[
M_{j+1}\ge M_j
\]
for each dangerous-safe pair, the route would close by induction: the defect magnitude would never be exhausted on a dangerous interval.

That, to me, is the clean next attack.

So the branch now reduces to one of two genuinely source-coupled targets.

One is a direct weighted-mass theorem:
\[
M_j\le |\Xi(q_j)|
\quad\text{on every dangerous interval.}
\]

The other is the pairwise version:
\[
M_{j+1}\ge M_j
\quad\text{for each dangerous-safe adjacent pair.}
\]

Either would finish the no-crossing argument.

I do not think you can prove either from the planar normalized model alone. The quantities
\[
e^{-A(a)}\quad\text{and}\quad A'(a)=\frac{2}{\tau(a)-a}
\]
depend on the actual continued RH branch, so this is exactly where source-coupling has to enter. But mathematically, the problem is no longer vague. It is now an alternating weighted-lobe domination problem for the integrated rooted defect.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:25:48)

I’m pushing on the weighted-lobe recursion itself now. The goal is to see whether the dangerous-lobe domination can be reduced to something monotone enough to force no crossing, or whether there is still a formally realizable scalar counter-model.

_Thought for 4m 1s_

Yes — there is a real further push, and it corrects the earlier “three-odd-zero” guess.

I think the right theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Parity-of-chamber theorem for the first root.}
}
\]

If the born repeated-source branch is continued root-free across successive anchor odd zeros, then the entry sign of the rooted defect alternates from chamber to chamber. As a consequence, a first rooted crossing can occur only in a chamber where the active segment contains an **even** number of odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\). Since the \(2\)-odd-zero chamber is already excluded, the first possible rooted crossing is in the **\(4\)-odd-zero chamber**, not the \(3\)-odd-zero chamber.

That is a genuine sharpening.

---

## 1. Set up the phase variable

Let
\[
q_1>q_2>q_3>\cdots
\]
be the successive odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) encountered by the anchor as \(a\) decreases along the continued born branch, and let
\[
I_j:=(q_{j+1},q_j).
\]
On each \(I_j\), \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign; write
\[
\epsilon_j:=\operatorname{sgn}(\mathcal R|_{I_j})\in\{\pm1\}.
\]

Let
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),
\qquad
\Xi(a):=e^{-A(a)}G(a),
\qquad
A'(a)=\frac{2}{\tau(a)-a}.
\]
Then
\[
\Xi'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a),
\]
so on each fixed-sign interval \(I_j\), \(\Xi\) is monotone.

Define the **phase**
\[
\phi_j:=\operatorname{sgn}\Xi(q_j)\,\epsilon_j\in\{\pm1\}.
\]
So:

- \(\phi_j=+1\) means the sign of \(\Xi\) agrees with the sign of \(\mathcal R\) on \(I_j\); this is a **safe** chamber,
- \(\phi_j=-1\) means the signs disagree; this is a **dangerous** chamber.

By the anchor-sign proposition already in the draft:

\[
\boxed{
\phi_j=+1 \Longrightarrow \text{no root can occur anywhere on }I_j.
}
\]

---

## 2. The phase recursion before the first root

Now assume there is **no rooted crossing** on the earlier intervals \(I_1,\dots,I_{j-1}\).

At each odd anchor zero \(q_j\), the odd-anchor no-reset theorem says that \(\Xi\) does **not** vanish and keeps its incoming sign across \(q_j\). But \(\epsilon_j\) flips sign at every odd zero. Therefore, if no root occurred on the previous interval, the phase flips:

\[
\boxed{
\phi_{j+1}=-\phi_j
\qquad
\text{as long as no root occurs on }I_j.
}
\]

Now at the first chamber \(I_1\), the outgoing sign after \(q_1\) is opposite to \(\epsilon_1\), so
\[
\phi_1=-1.
\]
Therefore, inductively, before the first root:
\[
\boxed{
\phi_j=(-1)^j.
}
\]

So:

- \(I_1,I_3,I_5,\dots\) are dangerous;
- \(I_2,I_4,I_6,\dots\) are safe.

---

## 3. Count odd zeros in each chamber

The active segment on \(I_j\) contains:

- the original seeding odd zero \(b\),
- plus the anchor odd zeros \(q_1,\dots,q_j\).

So it contains exactly
\[
j+1
\]
odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

Since the dangerous chambers are exactly the odd \(j\), we get:

\[
\boxed{
\text{before the first root, dangerous chambers are exactly those with an even number of active odd zeros.}
}
\]

And because the safe chambers cannot contain a root at all, the first rooted crossing — if it exists — must occur in one of those dangerous/even chambers.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a first rooted crossing can occur only when the active segment contains an even number of odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

Since the \(2\)-odd-zero chamber is already excluded by the anchor-sign theorem, the first possible chamber is the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber.

---

## 4. What this means

This improves the current bottleneck from:

\[
\text{“maybe the first root occurs after anchor-sign change”}
\]

to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{“if a first root occurs at all, it must occur in the first dangerous chamber not already excluded, namely the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber.”}
}
\]

So the scalar/discriminant route now rules out:

- all \(1\)-odd-zero chambers,
- all \(2\)-odd-zero chambers,
- and, by parity, all \(3\)-odd-zero chambers as candidates for a **first** root.

That is a meaningful advance.

---

## 5. The next attack after this

The next natural theorem target is now very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Four-odd-zero chamber exclusion.}
}
\]

At that point the weighted-lobe recursion becomes
\[
\Xi(q_4)=\Xi(q_1)+\epsilon_1(M_1-M_2+M_3),
\]
and the first root on \(I_3\) is equivalent to domination of the alternating weighted mass
\[
M_3 > \Xi(q_1)+M_1-M_2
\]
after fixing signs.

So the next real problem is no longer generic global crossing. It is a **three-lobe weighted domination problem** on the first dangerous chamber that survives the parity filter.

If you want, I’ll turn this into exact LaTeX insertions: a proposition for the phase-alternation theorem and a corollary stating “the first rooted crossing, if any, must occur in a chamber with at least four odd zeros.”

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:30:49)

I’m checking whether the new phase-alternation picture gives an actual exclusion in the 4-odd-zero chamber, or whether it only tells us that 4 is the first parity-compatible place a root could happen.

_Thought for 2m 16s_

Yes. I can push it to an exact discrete criterion.

Let the anchor odd zeros encountered by the continued born branch be
\[
q_1>q_2>q_3>\cdots,
\qquad
I_j:=(q_{j+1},q_j),
\]
and let
\[
\epsilon_j:=\operatorname{sgn}(\mathcal R|_{I_j})\in\{\pm1\}.
\]
Because the \(q_j\) are odd zeros, the signs alternate:
\[
\epsilon_j=(-1)^{j-1}\epsilon_1.
\]

Along the branch set
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
\Xi(a):=e^{-A(a)}G(a),\qquad
A'(a)=\frac{2}{\tau(a)-a}.
\]
Then
\[
\Xi'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
\]

For each interval \(I_j\), define the weighted lobe mass
\[
M_j:=\int_{q_{j+1}}^{q_j} e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds>0.
\]
Then on \(I_j\),
\[
\Xi(a)=\Xi(q_j)+\epsilon_j\int_a^{q_j}e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds.
\]

Now define the incoming defect magnitude
\[
X:=-\epsilon_1\,\Xi(q_1)>0.
\]
This is positive because the first chamber \(I_1\) is dangerous.

The exact recursion at the odd anchors is
\[
\Xi(q_{j+1})=\Xi(q_j)+\epsilon_j M_j,
\]
so multiplying by \(-\epsilon_{j+1}=\epsilon_j\) gives
\[
-\epsilon_{j+1}\Xi(q_{j+1})
=
-\epsilon_j\Xi(q_j)-M_j.
\]
It is cleaner to package this only on the dangerous chambers.

Define
\[
R_1:=X,
\qquad
R_{m+1}:=R_m-M_{2m-1}+M_{2m}\qquad(m\ge1).
\]
Then one checks inductively that
\[
R_m=-\epsilon_{2m-1}\,\Xi(q_{2m-1})>0
\]
as long as no root has occurred earlier.

This is the exact first-root criterion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The interval }I_{2m-1}\text{ is the first root chamber}
\iff
\begin{cases}
R_m>0,\\
M_{2m-1}>R_m,
\end{cases}
}
\]
and when that happens the root on \(I_{2m-1}\) is unique.

Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\text{No root occurs before }I_{2m-1}
\iff
M_{2k-1}\le R_k
\text{ for }k=1,\dots,m-1.
}
\]

So the whole continued-branch problem has now become a discrete alternating-mass problem.

Unwinding the recursion gives
\[
R_m
=
X-M_1+M_2-M_3+\cdots+M_{2m-2}.
\]
Hence the first possible root on the \(2m\)-odd-zero chamber is exactly the strict domination condition
\[
\boxed{
M_{2m-1}
>
X-M_1+M_2-\cdots+M_{2m-2}.
}
\]

This is the strongest clean reduction I see.

It gives two useful corollaries immediately.

First, the parity statement is exact:
roots can occur only on dangerous chambers \(I_{2m-1}\), so only when the active segment contains
\[
2m
\]
odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

Second, any theorem of the form
\[
M_{2m}\ge M_{2m-1}\qquad\text{for all }m
\]
would close the branch by induction, because then
\[
R_{m+1}=R_m-M_{2m-1}+M_{2m}\ge R_m>0,
\]
and so every dangerous chamber stays subcritical.

That tells you exactly what the next source-coupling target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{pairwise weighted-lobe dominance:}\quad
M_{2m}\ge M_{2m-1}.
}
\]

Or, a little weaker but still enough,
\[
\boxed{
X-M_1+M_2-\cdots+M_{2m}\ge X>0
\quad\text{for all }m.
}
\]

Now for the honest ceiling: I do not think the planar normalized model alone can force those inequalities. In fact the discrete criterion shows why. There are formally admissible positive numbers with
\[
X=10,\qquad M_1=3,\qquad M_2=5,\qquad M_3=13,
\]
for which
\[
R_2=10-3+5=12,
\qquad
M_3=13>12,
\]
so the first root occurs in the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber. That means the scalar recursion itself does not forbid a first root there.

So the attack has now reached a very precise frontier:

the nonmixed repeated-source lane will close if you can prove a source-coupled estimate forcing the alternating weighted sums
\[
X-M_1+M_2-M_3+\cdots
\]
to stay positive.

In other words, the remaining theorem is no longer about parity or local fold shape. It is a weighted comparison theorem for the actual RH branch:
\[
\int_{q_{2m+1}}^{q_{2m}} e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds
\;\ge\;
\int_{q_{2m}}^{q_{2m-1}} e^{-A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds.
\]

That is the cleanest next attack I know.

attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:34:29)

I’m testing whether the weighted-lobe recursion can be pushed into an actual inequality, or whether the current data still allow a formally admissible countermodel even after all the branch-transport structure.

_Thought for 5m 33s_

I pushed it one more step, and I think the clean outcome is this:

the transport recursion is now **completely solved**, and that means the next attack has to use the **critical-point equation** again. The recursion by itself is exhausted.

Here is the exact reduced system along the continued born branch.

Write
\[
t=\tau(a),\qquad L(a):=\tau(a)-a>0,
\]
and
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
\Xi(a):=e^{-A(a)}G(a),\qquad
A'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)}.
\]
Then
\[
\Xi'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
\]

But along the critical branch you also have the defining equation
\[
0=D_a'(\tau(a))
\iff
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,\mathcal R(a+sL(a))\,ds=0.
\]
So the real reduced problem is the coupled system
\[
\boxed{
\Xi'(a)=-e^{-A(a)}\mathcal R(a),
\qquad
A'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)},
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,\mathcal R(a+sL(a))\,ds=0.
}
\]

That last equation is the only place left where real rigidity can enter.

And it has a much cleaner form than it first appears.

If you integrate by parts, the critical-point equation becomes
\[
\boxed{
\int_a^{\tau(a)} \bigl(2x-a-\tau(a)\bigr)\,\bigl(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)\,dx=0.
}
\]
Also, using the critical equation inside the formula for \(G\),
\[
G(a)=L(a)\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\mathcal R(a+sL(a))\,ds
\]
collapses to
\[
\boxed{
L(a)\,G(a)=\int_a^{\tau(a)}\bigl(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)\,dx.
}
\]

So at a rooted point,
\[
G(a)=0,
\qquad
D_a'(\tau(a))=0,
\]
you get the two moment conditions
\[
\int_a^{\tau(a)}\bigl(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)\,dx=0,
\]
and
\[
\int_a^{\tau(a)}\bigl(2x-a-\tau(a)\bigr)\,\bigl(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)\,dx=0.
\]
Equivalently, with midpoint
\[
m(a):=\frac{a+\tau(a)}{2},
\qquad
\phi_a(x):=\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a),
\]
the rooted condition is exactly
\[
\boxed{
\int_a^{\tau(a)} \phi_a(x)\,dx=0,
\qquad
\int_a^{\tau(a)} (x-m(a))\,\phi_a(x)\,dx=0.
}
\]

That is the real shape of the remaining obstruction:

> on the actual RH branch, can the anchor-level profile \(\phi_a(x)=\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\) have both zero total mass and zero first centered moment over the critical interval \([a,\tau(a)]\)?

This is better than the earlier weighted-lobe language, because it shows exactly what extra theorem is needed.

The transport recursion only tracks the first quantity
\[
\int_a^{\tau(a)} \phi_a(x)\,dx,
\]
through the integrating factor. It does **not** control the centered moment
\[
\int_a^{\tau(a)} (x-m(a))\,\phi_a(x)\,dx.
\]
That centered moment is precisely what the critical equation contributes.

So here is the honest no-go refinement:

- the phase recursion and alternating masses tell you **where** a first root could occur;
- but they do not decide it, because the real branch must also satisfy the centered-moment constraint above.

In other words, the weighted-mass recursion is not the endgame. It is only the first half.

That suggests the next actual theorem to attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{centered-moment obstruction on dangerous chambers.}
}
\]

A concrete form would be:

> On a dangerous chamber \(I_{2m-1}\), if
> \[
> \int_a^{\tau(a)}\bigl(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)\,dx=0,
> \]
> then the critical-branch condition
> \[
> \int_a^{\tau(a)} (x-m(a))(\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a))\,dx=0
> \]
> cannot simultaneously hold.

That is now the right place to look for contradiction.

There is also a clean reason this has to be genuinely source-coupled. At the level of the transport ODE alone, you can make the first root happen in any dangerous chamber by choosing masses \(M_j\) appropriately. So no theorem excluding the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber, or any later dangerous chamber, can come from the \(\Xi\)-recursion by itself. The only remaining rigidity is in the way \(L(a)=\tau(a)-a\) is selected by the critical equation.

So the next attack is not another sign-counting argument. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{exploit the critical equation as a centered-moment identity.}
}
\]

If I were pushing this next, I would try two subroutes.

The first is a **lobe decomposition** of
\[
\phi_a(x)=\mathcal Z(x)-\mathcal Z(a)
\]
on the dangerous chamber, tracking the signs of each lobe and the sign of the midpoint weight \(x-m(a)\). If one can show those signed lobe contributions cannot cancel in the centered moment once the zeroth moment already cancels, the branch closes.

The second is a **source-pinned reformulation** of that centered moment identity. Since \(\tau(a)\) is not arbitrary but comes from \(\mathfrak D(a,h)=0\), there may be a determinant-level interpretation of the centered moment that is invisible in the normalized planar picture but has a definite sign on the actual RH branch.

So the push got one real step further:

the remaining math is no longer “weighted-lobe domination” in the abstract. It is a **two-moment incompatibility problem** for the anchor-level profile on the actual critical interval.
## User (2026-04-21 01:45:19)

continue the math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:45:22)

I’m trying to squeeze the centered-moment identity itself now, not just the transport ODE. The hope is that on the actual critical interval, the zeroth-moment cancellation and midpoint-moment cancellation are too rigid to coexist once you track the lobe geometry carefully.

_Thought for 6m 28s_

Yes. The next honest push is a **no-go theorem for the local two-moment route**.

I think this is important, because it tells you exactly what is left.

At a rooted critical point on the continued branch, after normalizing the active interval to \(s\in[0,1]\) by
\[
x=a+sL,\qquad L=\tau(a)-a,
\]
the profile
\[
R_a(s):=\mathcal R(a+sL)
\]
satisfies the two equations
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\,R_a(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,R_a(s)\,ds=0.
\]
These are exactly the root condition \(G(a)=0\) and the critical-branch condition \(D_a'(\tau(a))=0\).

Equivalently,
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)\,R_a(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,R_a(s)\,ds=0,
\]
and also
\[
\int_0^1 R_a(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s\,R_a(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s^2\,R_a(s)\,ds.
\]

That looks rigid, but it is still **not enough** to kill the first dangerous chamber.

Here is an explicit formal model with exactly three sign changes, i.e. the \(4\)-odd-zero pattern.

Define on \([0,1]\)
\[
R_*(s)=
\begin{cases}
1, & 0<s<\frac14,\\[4pt]
-3, & \frac14<s<\frac12,\\[4pt]
3, & \frac12<s<\frac34,\\[4pt]
-1, & \frac34<s<1.
\end{cases}
\]

Then a direct calculation gives
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2 R_*(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s) R_*(s)\,ds=0.
\]

So \(R_*\) has exactly the sign pattern needed for the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber,
\[
+,\ -,\ +,\ -,
\]
and yet it satisfies both rooted-point moment identities.

After smoothing the jumps slightly, one gets a real-analytic profile with the same three sign changes and the same two moment conditions to arbitrary precision, and in fact by a small finite-dimensional correction one can make them exact again.

So this proves:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the local intervalwise moment conditions alone do not exclude a first root in the \(4\)-odd-zero chamber.}
}
\]

That is a real mathematical conclusion.

It means the current scalar attack has reached a hard ceiling:

- the transport recursion tells you **which chambers are even eligible**;
- the critical equation adds the centered-moment constraint;
- but the pair of intervalwise moment constraints is still formally realizable in the first dangerous chamber that survives the parity filter.

So the remaining theorem cannot be about the shape of the normalized profile \(R_a(s)\) on a single interval alone.

It has to use something that this piecewise model does not know about: namely that \(R_a(s)\) comes from the **actual continued RH branch**, i.e. from the coupled source-level equations
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h)=0,
\qquad
\Psi_a(h)=\Gamma_{x(a)}(x(h)),
\qquad
f_a=u_5-\mathcal Z(a)\lambda.
\]

So I think the correct next target is now very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{profile-realizability exclusion:}
\text{ the first dangerous-chamber profile allowed by the scalar moments cannot be realized by the actual source-coupled RH branch.}
}
\]

In other words, the next theorem has to rule out the formal four-lobe profile not by more intervalwise sign algebra, but by a **source-coupled distortion law** for the actual family \(R_a(s)=\mathcal R(a+sL(a))\).

The cleanest ways I see to attack that are:

First, try to prove a **shape monotonicity theorem** for the normalized profile \(R_a(s)\) coming from the determinant equation \(\mathfrak D(a,h)=0\). For example, any relation forcing the middle negative lobe and outer positive lobe not to satisfy the \(1:-3:3:-1\)-type weighted balance would close the first dangerous chamber.

Second, try to derive a **source-level identity for the centered moment**
\[
\int_0^1 s(1-s)R_a(s)\,ds
\]
or for the rooted defect
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2R_a(s)\,ds,
\]
showing that on the actual branch these two moments cannot vanish simultaneously once the chamber has only four odd zeros.

Third, look for a **total-positivity / variation-diminishing statement for the moving-shear family**
\[
f_a=u_5-\mathcal Z(a)\lambda
\]
that would forbid the first four-lobe balanced profile from arising at all.

So the math attack does continue, but with a clearer lesson now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next missing ingredient is not another scalar moment identity; it is a source-coupled profile-exclusion theorem.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the real frontier now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 01:53:33)

I’m going after the “profile-realizability” gap now. The key question is whether the determinant equation forces some shape constraint on the normalized four-lobe profile that the scalar moment model misses, or whether the right next result is actually a no-go theorem saying the current source data still permit that profile formally.

_Thought for 8m 8s_

Yes. I think the next honest push is a **strong no-go theorem for any purely local scalar attack in the first dangerous chamber**.

It goes further than the earlier piecewise example, because it shows there is actually a whole family of formal \(4\)-lobe rooted profiles that satisfy not only the two vanishing moment conditions, but also either sign of the nondegenerate fold data.

Here is the clean model.

Normalize the rooted interval to \(s\in[0,1]\), and consider a profile with sign pattern
\[
+,-,+,-
\]
on the four quarter-intervals:
\[
R_{A,B}(s):=
\begin{cases}
A, & 0<s<\frac14,\\[4pt]
-B, & \frac14<s<\frac12,\\[4pt]
C, & \frac12<s<\frac34,\\[4pt]
-D, & \frac34<s<1,
\end{cases}
\]
with
\[
C=\frac{-15A+7B}{2},
\qquad
D=\frac{-31A+11B}{2}.
\]

A direct calculation shows that for every \(A,B\),
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds=0.
\]

So this entire \(2\)-parameter family satisfies the rooted-point equations
\[
G=0,
\qquad
D_a'(\tau(a))=0
\]
in normalized form.

Now compute the common zeroth/first/second moment:
\[
\int_0^1 R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s^2\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\frac34(3A-B).
\]

That is important because
\[
H=\mathcal Z(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a)
\]
is proportional to
\[
\int_0^1 s^2 R_a(s)\,ds,
\]
so in this model the nondegeneracy and fold orientation are governed by \(3A-B\).

Also
\[
R_{A,B}(0^+)=A,
\]
so the transversality sign
\[
G'(a_*)=-\mathcal R(a_*)
\]
is governed by \(-A\).

Now impose the actual four-lobe sign pattern by requiring
\[
A>0,\qquad B>0,\qquad C>0,\qquad D>0.
\]
This is equivalent to
\[
B>\frac{31}{11}A.
\]

Then:

- if
  \[
  \frac{31}{11}A<B<3A,
  \]
  the profile is four-lobed and nondegenerate with
  \[
  \int_0^1 s^2R_{A,B}(s)\,ds>0;
  \]

- if
  \[
  B>3A,
  \]
  the profile is four-lobed and nondegenerate with
  \[
  \int_0^1 s^2R_{A,B}(s)\,ds<0.
  \]

So the first dangerous chamber admits formal rooted profiles with either sign of \(H\), while \(R(0)\) can be chosen with either sign by flipping the whole profile.

A concrete example is
\[
A=10,\qquad B=29,
\qquad C=\frac{53}{2},\qquad D=\frac92.
\]
Then
\[
R(s)=
\begin{cases}
10,&0<s<\frac14,\\[4pt]
-29,&\frac14<s<\frac12,\\[4pt]
\frac{53}{2},&\frac12<s<\frac34,\\[4pt]
-\frac92,&\frac34<s<1,
\end{cases}
\]
has exactly the sign pattern \(+,-,+,-\) and satisfies
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2R(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)R(s)\,ds=0,
\]
while
\[
\int_0^1 R(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 sR(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s^2R(s)\,ds
=
\frac34>0.
\]

So this model has:

- rootedness,
- the critical-branch condition,
- \(R(0)>0\), hence \(G'(a_*)<0\),
- and \(H>0\), hence a nondegenerate rooted fold.

That is enough to show something quite strong:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any argument using only the local normalized rooted profile, the two moment conditions,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and the local transversality/fold data at the first dangerous chamber cannot close the branch.}
}
\]

This is stronger than the earlier no-go, because it says the obstruction is not just that one special piecewise profile exists. There is a whole open family of formal \(4\)-lobe rooted-fold models.

So the math has now reached a very sharp ceiling:

- parity reduces the first possible root chamber to \(4\) odd zeros;
- the local rooted-point equations plus fold/transversality data still allow a robust family of \(4\)-lobe models;
- therefore the missing theorem cannot live in any purely local scalar normalization.

It has to use something that this model does not encode: namely that the profile comes from the **actual source-coupled branch**
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h)=0,
\qquad
\Psi_a(h)=\Gamma_{x(a)}(x(h)),
\qquad
f_a=u_5-\mathcal Z(a)\lambda.
\]

So the next viable attack is now even narrower than “profile realizability exclusion.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{source-coupled distortion theorem for the normalized four-lobe profile.}
}
\]

In practical terms, I would attack one of these two statements next.

One is a **shape inequality**: along the actual RH branch, the four quarter-lobe amplitudes cannot satisfy the affine relations
\[
C=\frac{-15A+7B}{2},\qquad D=\frac{-31A+11B}{2}
\]
even asymptotically, because the determinant equation forces a different distortion pattern.

The other is a **global continuation invariant**: the born branch cannot evolve into any local rooted model from this open \(4\)-lobe family before source-level exit/collapse, because some determinant sign or orientation index is preserved and disagrees with the local rooted family.

So the current attack does continue, but what it has just shown is that **all finite local scalar rooted data are spent**. The only live next theorem is genuinely source-coupled.

```latex
\begin{proposition}[Odd-anchor passage has no sign reset]
\label{prop:odd-anchor-passage-no-reset}
Let $J$ be an opened nonmixed arc, and work in a local normalized nonmixed
chart on which
\[
S(t)=t^2,
\qquad
\Gamma_a=D_a.
\]
Let
\[
q\in J
\]
be an isolated odd-order zero of $\mathcal R$, and let
\[
\tau:(q-\delta,q+\delta)\to J
\]
be a real-analytic continuation of the repeated-source critical branch such that
\[
\tau(a)>a,
\qquad
D_a'(\tau(a))=0
\qquad (a\in(q-\delta,q+\delta)).
\]
Set
\[
G(a):=D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a),
\qquad
L(a):=\tau(a)-a,
\]
and define
\[
\mathcal A'(a):=\frac{2}{L(a)},
\qquad
\mathscr G(a):=e^{-\mathcal A(a)}G(a).
\]
Assume that there exists $\epsilon\in\{\pm1\}$ such that
\[
\epsilon\,\mathcal R(a)>0,
\qquad
\epsilon\,G(a)>0
\qquad\text{for every }a\in(q,q+\delta).
\]
Then
\[
\epsilon\,G(q)>0.
\]
In particular, $G(q)\neq 0$. Hence, after possibly shrinking $\delta$,
\[
\epsilon\,G(a)>0
\qquad\text{for every }a\in(q-\delta,q+\delta).
\]
Since $q$ is an odd-order zero of $\mathcal R$, the sign of $\mathcal R$ flips
across $q$; therefore on the outgoing side
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G(a)=-\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(a)
\qquad (a\in(q-\delta,q)).
\]
So a rooted crossing cannot occur at the anchor odd zero itself, nor
immediately after passage across it.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By
Proposition~\ref{prop:rooted-defect-transport-stationary-first-root},
\[
G'(a)=\frac{2}{L(a)}\,G(a)-\mathcal R(a),
\]
hence
\[
\mathscr G'(a)=-e^{-\mathcal A(a)}\mathcal R(a).
\]
Assume for contradiction that $G(q)=0$. Then $\mathscr G(q)=0$, and for
$a\in(q,q+\delta)$,
\[
\mathscr G(a)
=
\mathscr G(q)+\int_q^a \mathscr G'(s)\,ds
=
-\int_q^a e^{-\mathcal A(s)}\mathcal R(s)\,ds.
\]
Since $e^{-\mathcal A(s)}>0$ and $\epsilon\,\mathcal R(s)>0$ on $(q,q+\delta)$,
this gives
\[
\epsilon\,\mathscr G(a)<0
\qquad (a\in(q,q+\delta)),
\]
hence
\[
\epsilon\,G(a)<0
\qquad (a\in(q,q+\delta)),
\]
contradicting the hypothesis. Therefore $G(q)\neq 0$. Since
\[
\epsilon\,G(a)>0
\qquad (a\in(q,q+\delta)),
\]
continuity yields
\[
\epsilon\,G(q)\ge 0,
\]
and the nonvanishing just proved forces
\[
\epsilon\,G(q)>0.
\]
A second continuity argument then gives
\[
\epsilon\,G(a)>0
\qquad (a\in(q-\delta,q+\delta))
\]
after shrinking $\delta$ if necessary. Finally, because $q$ is an odd-order zero
of $\mathcal R$, its sign flips across $q$, so on $(q-\delta,q)$ one has
\[
\operatorname{sgn}G(a)=-\operatorname{sgn}\mathcal R(a).
\]
In particular $G$ cannot vanish there.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Weighted-defect recursion on post-anchor chambers]
\label{prop:weighted-defect-recursion-post-anchor-chambers}
Assume the setting of
Proposition~\ref{prop:odd-anchor-passage-no-reset}. Let
\[
q_1>q_2>\cdots>q_N
\]
be successive odd-order zeros of $\mathcal R$ crossed by the anchor along the
continued branch after the adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber, and set
\[
I_j:=(q_{j+1},q_j)
\qquad (1\le j\le N-1).
\]
Assume that the continued branch exists real-analytically on
\[
\bigcup_{j=1}^{N-1} I_j.
\]
For each $j$, let
\[
\epsilon_j:=\operatorname{sgn}(\mathcal R|_{I_j})\in\{\pm1\},
\qquad
m_j(a):=\int_a^{q_j} e^{-\mathcal A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds
\qquad (a\in I_j),
\]
and
\[
M_j:=m_j(q_{j+1})>0.
\]
Then on each $I_j$,
\[
\mathscr G(a)=\mathscr G(q_j)+\epsilon_j\,m_j(a).
\]
Consequently:
\begin{enumerate}
\item if
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j)=\epsilon_j,
\]
then $\mathscr G$ has no zero on $I_j$;

\item if
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j)=-\epsilon_j,
\]
then $\mathscr G$ has a zero on $I_j$ if and only if
\[
M_j>|\mathscr G(q_j)|,
\]
and in that case the zero is unique;

\item if $\mathscr G$ has no zero on $I_j$, then
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_{j+1})
=
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j).
\]
\end{enumerate}
Since $\epsilon_{j+1}=-\epsilon_j$, the chamber type
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j)\ \text{ versus }\ \epsilon_j
\]
alternates from one post-anchor chamber to the next as long as no rooted
crossing has yet occurred.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
On $I_j$ one has
\[
\mathscr G'(a)=-e^{-\mathcal A(a)}\mathcal R(a)
=-\epsilon_j e^{-\mathcal A(a)}|\mathcal R(a)|.
\]
Hence for $a\in I_j$,
\[
\mathscr G(a)
=
\mathscr G(q_j)+\int_{q_j}^a \mathscr G'(s)\,ds
=
\mathscr G(q_j)+\epsilon_j\int_a^{q_j}e^{-\mathcal A(s)}|\mathcal R(s)|\,ds,
\]
which is the stated formula.

Now $m_j(a)$ is strictly increasing as $a$ decreases from $q_j$ to $q_{j+1}$,
with
\[
m_j(q_j)=0,
\qquad
m_j(q_{j+1})=M_j.
\]
So if
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j)=\epsilon_j,
\]
then
\[
\mathscr G(a)=\epsilon_j\bigl(|\mathscr G(q_j)|+m_j(a)\bigr)
\]
has fixed sign on $I_j$ and no zero. If instead
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j)=-\epsilon_j,
\]
then
\[
\mathscr G(a)=\epsilon_j\bigl(-|\mathscr G(q_j)|+m_j(a)\bigr),
\]
so a zero occurs precisely when $m_j(a)=|\mathscr G(q_j)|$, which happens if
and only if
\[
M_j>|\mathscr G(q_j)|.
\]
Because $m_j$ is strictly monotone, that zero is unique.

Finally, if no zero occurs on $I_j$, then $\mathscr G$ keeps one sign
throughout the interval, so in particular
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_{j+1})
=
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_j).
\]
Since the $\epsilon_j$ alternate at successive odd zeros, the relative chamber
type alternates as claimed.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[The first post-anchor chamber is dangerous]
\label{cor:first-post-anchor-chamber-dangerous}
Let $q_1$ be the first odd-order zero of $\mathcal R$ crossed by the anchor
after the adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber, and let
\[
I_1:=(q_2,q_1)
\]
be the first post-anchor chamber. Assume the continued born branch extends
real-analytically across $q_1$ and on $I_1$. Then:
\begin{enumerate}
\item no rooted crossing occurs at $q_1$ or immediately after crossing it;

\item on $I_1$ one has
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_1)=-\epsilon_1,
\]
so $I_1$ is the first \emph{dangerous} post-anchor chamber;

\item as long as no rooted crossing has yet occurred, the post-anchor chambers
alternate dangerous/safe/dangerous/safe\ldots\ .
\end{enumerate}
In particular, the first chamber in which a rooted crossing is even eligible
after the anchor-sign-preservation interval is the first post-anchor chamber,
namely the chamber in which the active segment carries three odd zeros of
$\mathcal R$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber is root-free by
Corollary~\ref{cor:no-crossing-adjacent-two-odd-zero-under-anchor-sign-preservation}.
At its left endpoint $q_1$, the anchor crosses an odd-order zero of
$\mathcal R$. Proposition~\ref{prop:odd-anchor-passage-no-reset} shows that
$\mathscr G$ keeps its incoming sign across $q_1$, while $\mathcal R$ changes
sign there. Thus on the outgoing chamber $I_1$ the signs are opposite:
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\mathscr G(q_1)=-\epsilon_1.
\]
The no-root statement at and immediately after $q_1$ is also exactly
Proposition~\ref{prop:odd-anchor-passage-no-reset}. The alternation of chamber
types then follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:weighted-defect-recursion-post-anchor-chambers}(3).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[No-go for purely local scalar exclusion in the first dangerous chamber]
\label{rem:no-go-local-scalar-first-dangerous-chamber}
At a rooted critical point, after normalizing the active interval to
\[
x=a+sL,
\qquad
0\le s\le 1,
\qquad
L=\tau(a)-a,
\]
the profile
\[
R_a(s):=\mathcal R(a+sL)
\]
satisfies the two local scalar moment identities
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\,R_a(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,R_a(s)\,ds=0.
\]
In the first dangerous post-anchor chamber, the sign pattern of $R_a$ on
$[0,1]$ has four lobes:
\[
+,-,+,-
\]
or its global negative. At the purely local scalar level, these conditions are
still formally too weak to force contradiction.

Indeed, for parameters $A,B\in\mathbf R$, define the quarter-piece profile
\[
R_{A,B}(s):=
\begin{cases}
A, & 0<s<\frac14,\\[4pt]
-B, & \frac14<s<\frac12,\\[4pt]
\frac{-15A+7B}{2}, & \frac12<s<\frac34,\\[4pt]
-\frac{-31A+11B}{2}, & \frac34<s<1.
\end{cases}
\]
A direct calculation gives
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds=0
\]
for every $A,B$.

Moreover, if
\[
B>\frac{31}{11}A>0,
\]
then the four quarter-lobes have signs
\[
+,-,+,-.
\]
For this family one also has
\[
\int_0^1 R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\int_0^1 s^2\,R_{A,B}(s)\,ds
=
\frac34(3A-B),
\]
so both signs of the nondegenerate fold data occur already inside this formal
four-lobe family.

Thus no argument using only the local normalized rooted profile, the two
vanishing scalar moments, and the local transversality/fold signs can exclude
rooting in the first dangerous post-anchor chamber. Any successful next step
must use genuinely source-coupled structure invisible in the planar normalized
$D_a$ model.
\end{remark}
```

```latex
\label{rem:route-two-reduces-to-global-no-crossing}
...
\end{remark}
```

with:

```latex
\begin{remark}[What route 2 now reduces to]
\label{rem:route-two-reduces-to-global-no-crossing}
The discriminant analysis first showed that a rooted point is not born
\emph{at the entry} into the adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber. The stronger
anchor-sign argument showed more: on any continuation interval on which the
anchor-side value
\[
\mathcal R(a)
\]
keeps a fixed sign, the RH-selected slice
\[
\gamma=\mathcal Z(a)
\]
cannot cross the discriminant curve at all.

The new odd-anchor analysis sharpens this again. When the anchor itself passes
through an odd-order zero of $\mathcal R$, the integrating-factor defect
\[
\mathscr G(a)=e^{-\mathcal A(a)}\bigl(D_a(\tau(a))-\mathcal Z(a)\bigr)
\]
keeps its incoming sign across the odd anchor zero, so a rooted crossing cannot
occur at the anchor zero itself, nor immediately after passage. The subsequent
post-anchor chambers then alternate dangerous and safe, beginning with a
dangerous first post-anchor chamber in which the active segment carries three
odd zeros of $\mathcal R$
(Corollary~\ref{cor:first-post-anchor-chamber-dangerous}).

However, Remark~\ref{rem:no-go-local-scalar-first-dangerous-chamber} shows that
already in that first dangerous chamber the local normalized rooted equations
and fold data are formally realizable in the planar scalar model. So route 2 no
longer reduces merely to an anchor-sign-change crossing problem in the abstract.
It reduces to a genuinely source-coupled \emph{delayed no-crossing /
profile-realizability problem} on dangerous post-anchor chambers.
\end{remark}
```

## Assistant (2026-04-21 02:15:23)

I think the local scalar/discriminant branch has reached its ceiling. The next live move is to get something genuinely source-coupled that constrains the *actual* normalized profile on dangerous post-anchor chambers, rather than another intervalwise moment identity.

_Thought for 2m 56s_

My next attack would be this:

**try to extract a third, genuinely source-level constraint at a hypothetical first dangerous-chamber root, and use it to kill the formal four-lobe family.**

That is the most natural next move because we’ve now learned three things.

First, the scalar route is exhausted.  
The one-sign chamber is sterile, the adjacent-two-odd-zero chamber is sterile under anchor-sign preservation, odd-anchor passage has no reset, and the first dangerous post-anchor chamber is the first place a root is even eligible.

Second, the local scalar rooted equations are still too weak there.  
The normalized four-lobe profile
\[
+,-,+,-
\]
can satisfy
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2R(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)R(s)\,ds=0,
\]
and even the local fold/transversality signs. So no argument that only uses the planar normalized profile on one interval is going to close it.

Third, the actual RH branch carries more structure than that model.  
The profile is not arbitrary. It comes from the source-level branch
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h)=0,
\qquad
\Psi_a(h)=\frac{D_{u_5}(a,h)}{D_\lambda(a,h)},
\qquad
f_a=u_5-\mathcal Z(a)\lambda.
\]

So the next theorem should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{source-level third-constraint theorem at first dangerous-chamber root.}
}
\]

In practical terms, I would attack it like this.

Take a hypothetical first dangerous-chamber root \((a_*,h_*)\) on the actual continued branch. Normalize the source interval to
\[
u=a_*+s(h_*-a_*),\qquad s\in[0,1],
\]
and write the source profile
\[
W_*(s):=\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(a_*+s(h_*-a_*)).
\]

From the current scalar theory, \(W_*\) already satisfies the two rooted moment conditions in normalized form:
\[
\int_0^1 (1-s)^2W_*(s)\,ds=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 s(1-s)W_*(s)\,ds=0.
\]

The attack is to show that the **actual source branch** imposes one more independent condition at a first root.

The cleanest place to look is the fact that at a rooted point the target map is stationary:
\[
h'(a_*)=0
\]
after passing to source coordinates. Since the actual branch satisfies
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h(a))=0,
\]
differentiating once and using \(h'(a_*)=0\) gives
\[
\partial_a \mathfrak D(a_*,h_*)=0.
\]
That is source-level information, not just planar information. The right next step is to rewrite
\[
\partial_a \mathfrak D(a_*,h_*)
\]
in normalized source coordinates and see whether it becomes a third linear functional on \(W_*\):
\[
\int_0^1 Q_*(s)\,W_*(s)\,ds=0
\]
for some kernel \(Q_*\) not in the span of
\[
(1-s)^2,\qquad s(1-s).
\]

That is the key test.

If such a third condition appears, then we immediately compare it against the explicit two-parameter four-lobe family. If the family cannot satisfy it, the first dangerous chamber is killed. If the family can still satisfy it, then we have learned something equally important: even the local source-linearized data are still too free, and the next attack must be global rather than local.

So I would do this in two phases.

The first phase is purely symbolic:
rewrite \(\partial_a\mathfrak D\), and if needed \(\partial_{aa}\mathfrak D\), at a rooted stationary point in normalized source coordinates.

The second phase is model-testing:
plug the formal four-lobe family into the resulting third condition and check whether it is compatible.

That is the next attack I’d choose because it is the first one that really uses the source determinant beyond what has already been collapsed into the planar \(D_a\) picture.

If that fails, then I think the next branch after that is clear: abandon local profile exclusion and go fully global, trying to prove a continuation invariant for the discriminant curve that the formal four-lobe models do not see.
## User (2026-04-21 02:19:00)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 02:19:03)

I’m checking the exact source-determinant formulas in the draft before pushing the “third constraint from \(\partial_a\mathfrak D\)” idea, because that attack only works if the determinant derivative really adds information beyond the already-collapsed planar moments.

_Thought for 4m 18s_

I tried the \(\partial_a\mathfrak D\) route carefully, and I think the honest outcome is:

\[
\boxed{\text{it does \emph{not} produce a third independent local constraint.}}
\]

It collapses exactly to the stationary-target / fold data you already have.

Work in the normalized nonmixed chart and write, for free variables \(a<t\),
\[
F(a,t):=D_a'(t),\qquad
G(a,t):=D_a(t)-\mathcal Z(a),\qquad
H(a,t):=\mathcal Z(t)-D_a(t),\qquad
L:=t-a.
\]
Then
\[
F(a,t)=2\int_0^1 s(1-s)\,\mathcal R(a+sL)\,ds,
\]
\[
G(a,t)=L\int_0^1 (1-s)^2\,\mathcal R(a+sL)\,ds,
\]
\[
H(a,t)=L\int_0^1 s^2\,\mathcal R(a+sL)\,ds.
\]

From these, differentiating at fixed \(t\), one gets the exact identities
\[
\boxed{
F_a=\frac{2}{L}F-\frac{2}{L^2}G,
\qquad
F_t=\frac{2}{L^2}H.
}
\]

Now take a rooted point \((a_*,t_*)\), so
\[
F(a_*,t_*)=0,
\qquad
G(a_*,t_*)=0.
\]
Then immediately
\[
\boxed{F_a(a_*,t_*)=0.}
\]

That is the key fact. The hoped-for “third condition” from differentiating the critical determinant in the anchor direction is automatic.

At source level this is exactly the same statement. Since on the nonmixed patch
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h)=\kappa(a,h)\,F(x(a),x(h))
\]
for a nonvanishing analytic prefactor \(\kappa\), at a rooted point one has
\[
\boxed{\partial_a\mathfrak D(a_*,h_*)=0}
\]
for free, simply because \(F=G=0\) there. So \(\partial_a\mathfrak D=0\) is not a new local source constraint at all.

If you push one derivative further, you still do not get anything new. Since
\[
G_a=\frac{2}{L}G-\mathcal R(a),
\]
at a rooted point
\[
G_a(a_*,t_*)=-\mathcal R(a_*).
\]
Hence
\[
F_{aa}(a_*,t_*)
=
-\frac{2}{L_*^2}G_a(a_*,t_*)
=
\frac{2}{L_*^2}\mathcal R(a_*),
\]
while
\[
F_t(a_*,t_*)=\frac{2}{L_*^2}H(a_*,t_*).
\]
So along the critical curve \(F(a,\tau(a))=0\),
\[
\tau'(a_*)=-\frac{F_a}{F_t}=0,
\qquad
\tau''(a_*)=-\frac{F_{aa}}{F_t}
=
-\frac{\mathcal R(a_*)}{H(a_*,t_*)},
\]
which is exactly the stationary-point / fold formula already in the draft. In other words, the second \(a\)-derivative of \(\mathfrak D\) just reproduces the existing transversality/fold data. It does not add a new vanishing relation either.

There is also a useful companion fact for the moving shear. At a rooted point,
\[
D_{f_a}(a,h)=D_\lambda(a,h)\,G(a,h),
\qquad
f_a=u_5-\mathcal Z(a)\lambda.
\]
So
\[
\partial_a D_{f_a}(a_*,h_*)
=
D_\lambda(a_*,h_*)\,G_a(a_*,h_*)
=
-\,D_\lambda(a_*,h_*)\,\mathcal R(a_*),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{\partial_a D_{f_a}(a_*,h_*)\neq 0}
\]
at a nondegenerate rooted point. So the source-pinned moving-shear equation is actually transverse in the anchor direction there. Again: no extra vanishing, no extra local constraint.

So the local source-level picture is now very clear:

- \(\mathfrak D=0\) gives the critical branch;
- \(D_{f_a}=0\) gives the RH-selected slice;
- at a rooted point, \(\partial_a\mathfrak D=0\) automatically;
- \(\partial_a D_{f_a}\neq 0\) automatically;
- and the remaining second-order data are exactly the stationary/fold quantities you already know.

That means the proposed “third local condition from \(\partial_a\mathfrak D\)” is dead.

I think that is a worthwhile structural finding, because it tells you exactly what local source-coupling still cannot do: even after bringing \(\mathfrak D\) back in, the local rooted germ is still fully captured by the existing planar transport/fold package.

So the next viable attack is no longer “differentiate \(\mathfrak D\) more.” It has to involve something genuinely mixed and nonlocal, for example a quantity that couples the two equations
\[
\mathfrak D(a,h)=0,
\qquad
D_{f_a}(a,h)=0
\]
along the continued branch and is not determined by their local jets at a single rooted point.

The cleanest candidate now is to study a ratio or comparison quantity built from both source equations along the critical branch, rather than more local derivatives of either one separately.
## User (2026-04-21 02:24:40)
