## Assistant (2026-04-21 04:29:43)

_Thought for 2m 15s_

Here is the cleanest further push I can get.

The direct-slice global problem reduces from “many possible recurrences” to a **one-parameter intercept comparison problem**, and for each fixed same-parity pair there can be **at most one** common tangent line. That is real progress.

---

Let \(J_\alpha,J_\beta\) be two regular direct-slice components with the same parity, and assume their slope windows overlap:
\[
I_{\alpha\beta}:=I_\alpha\cap I_\beta\neq\varnothing.
\]
On each component, since \(\phi_\mu\) is strictly monotone, we can invert it:
\[
\mu=\mu_\alpha(s)\quad (s\in I_\alpha),\qquad
\mu=\mu_\beta(s)\quad (s\in I_\beta),
\]
where \(s\) denotes slope.

Define the intercept functions of the tangent lines on the two branches by
\[
m_\alpha(s):=\phi(\mu_\alpha(s)) - s\,\mu_\alpha(s),
\qquad
m_\beta(s):=\phi(\mu_\beta(s)) - s\,\mu_\beta(s).
\]

These are the two dual branches in \((s,m)\)-coordinates.

Now differentiate. Since \(s=\phi_\mu(\mu)\), one gets
\[
\frac{d m_\alpha}{ds} = -\,\mu_\alpha(s),
\qquad
\frac{d m_\beta}{ds} = -\,\mu_\beta(s).
\]
Therefore the intercept difference
\[
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s):=m_\beta(s)-m_\alpha(s)
\]
satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'_{\alpha\beta}(s)=\mu_\alpha(s)-\mu_\beta(s).
}
\]

Because \(\alpha<\beta\), the corresponding \(\mu\)-values are ordered:
\[
\mu_\alpha(s)<\mu_\beta(s)
\qquad (s\in I_{\alpha\beta}),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'_{\alpha\beta}(s)<0
\qquad (s\in I_{\alpha\beta}).
}
\]

So \(\Delta_{\alpha\beta}\) is strictly decreasing on the overlap window.

That immediately gives:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any two same-parity regular components can support at most one common tangent line.}
}
\]

Because a common tangent line is exactly a solution of
\[
m_\alpha(s)=m_\beta(s)
\iff
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)=0,
\]
and a strictly decreasing function has at most one zero.

That is already stronger than the earlier “two separated zeros of \(W^{[1]}\)” formulation.

---

There is also a useful integral formula. Since
\[
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)
=
\phi(\mu_\beta)-s\mu_\beta-\phi(\mu_\alpha)+s\mu_\alpha,
\]
you can rewrite it as
\[
\boxed{
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)
=
\int_{\mu_\alpha(s)}^{\mu_\beta(s)} \bigl(\phi_\mu(u)-s\bigr)\,du.
}
\]

So a common tangent between the two branches is equivalent to an **exact signed-area balance**
\[
\int_{\mu_\alpha(s)}^{\mu_\beta(s)} \bigl(\phi_\mu(u)-s\bigr)\,du=0.
\]

That reframes the endgame very cleanly:

- slope-window overlap tells you a candidate slope \(s\) exists on both branches;
- intercept equality is a single strictly monotone scalar equation in \(s\);
- and the only remaining issue for each pair is the sign of \(\Delta_{\alpha\beta}\) at one endpoint of the overlap.

Because \(\Delta_{\alpha\beta}\) is strictly decreasing, once you know the sign at one endpoint, the whole pair is settled.

So the global direct-slice problem now reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{endpoint sign theorem for }\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)
\text{ on each same-parity overlap window }I_{\alpha\beta}.
}
\]

If you can prove
\[
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s_0)\neq 0
\]
with the same sign at one endpoint \(s_0\) of each overlap interval, then no common tangent line exists for that pair. Since there can be at most one anyway, that would close the global direct-slice recurrence problem pair by pair.

So the next attack I would make is not more counting of zeros of \(W^{[1]}\). It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{compute the endpoint sign of the dual intercept difference }
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)
\text{ from local boundary asymptotics.}
}
\]

In practice, that means:

- at a finite \(W^{[1]}=0\) boundary, use the local turning/inflection expansion of \(\phi_\mu\) and \(\phi-\mu\phi_\mu\);
- at a generic turning boundary \(M=0\), use the dual asymptote behavior;
- then compare the boundary intercept from one branch to the other branch at the same slope.

So the math has sharpened to one very concrete scalar problem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for each same-parity pair, }\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)\text{ is strictly decreasing; prove it never crosses }0.
}
\]

That is the next real push.

I think the next honest push is another **no-go theorem**, but it is useful:

\[

\]

So the abstract direct-slice graph geometry, by itself, still does **not** close the branch.

Here is a clean counterexample.

Take
\[
\phi(\mu):=(\mu+1)^2(\mu-1)^2(\mu-2).
\]

Then
\[
\phi(-1)=\phi(1)=0,
\qquad
\phi'(-1)=\phi'(1)=0,
\]
so the line
\[
y=0
\]
is tangent to the graph \(y=\phi(\mu)\) at the two distinct points
\[
\mu=-1,\qquad \mu=1.
\]

Now compute the curvature:
\[
\phi''(\mu)=4\bigl(5\mu^3-6\mu^2-3\mu+2\bigr).
\]
Its zeros are simple and occur at
\[
\xi_1\approx -0.65374,\qquad
\xi_2\approx 0.42965,\qquad
\xi_3\approx 1.42409.
\]

So the regular components are
\[
J_1=(-\infty,\xi_1),\quad
J_2=(\xi_1,\xi_2),\quad
J_3=(\xi_2,\xi_3),\quad
J_4=(\xi_3,\infty),
\]
with alternating convexity. The two tangent points lie on
\[
-1\in J_1,\qquad 1\in J_3,
\]
which are **nonadjacent same-parity components**.

So this is exactly the kind of configuration the abstract direct-slice endgame was trying to rule out:

- same-parity nonadjacent components,
- overlapping slope windows at slope \(s=0\),
- and a common tangent line between them.

In the language of the dual intercept difference,
\[
\Delta_{13}(s)=m_3(s)-m_1(s),
\]
this example shows:
\[
\Delta_{13}(0)=0.
\]
That is consistent with the earlier monotonicity fact
\[
\Delta'_{13}(s)=\mu_1(s)-\mu_3(s)<0,
\]
which only implies **at most one** common tangent, not **none**.

So the new theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict monotonicity of }\Delta_{\alpha\beta}
\text{ is not enough to prevent a zero.}
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the global same-parity no-reentry theorem cannot be proved from abstract convexity decomposition alone.}
}
\]

That matters, because it tells you exactly what is left.

The reductions you now have are still real:

- on each regular component, \(\phi_\mu\) is strictly monotone;
- for each same-parity pair, there is at most one common tangent;
- any such common tangent forces the intercept difference \(\Delta_{\alpha\beta}\) to vanish exactly once.

But this example shows that the remaining step

\[
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)\neq 0
\quad\text{on the overlap window}
\]
is **not** a formal consequence of the general direct-slice setup.

So the next attack has to use the **explicit provenance-sensitive formula** for \(\phi\), not just the fact that \(\phi\) is a scalar graph with alternating convexity intervals.

That is the real update:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the first live direct-slice target is no longer window-order in the abstract;}
}

\[

\textbf{it is a theorem about the special scalar }\phi

}
\]

Concretely, I would now attack one of these two statements:

\[
\phi(\mu)=\kappa\Bigl((x_2-x_1)\nu_6^{(q^{(7)})}
+v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)^{(q^{(7)})}\Bigr)
\]
has some extra monotonicity/variation property that a generic polynomial like the example above does not have,

or

\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}
=
\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}
\]
has a provenance-sensitive sign/oscillation restriction, stronger than merely “simple zeros at boundaries.”

So the clean mathematical conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{abstract graph geometry is spent; only the explicit direct-slice formula for }\phi

That is where I would push next.

I’m pushing directly on the one remaining live place in this lane: the explicit direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice formula for \(\phi\). _Thought for 1m 41s_

I think the next honest attack gives a **strong no-go theorem for the explicit direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice itself**.

So the new conclusion is not a closure theorem. It is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{the explicit direct-slice formula for }\phi\text{ is still too free by itself.}}
\]

That is useful, because it tells you exactly what the next real theorem must constrain.

---

## 1. What the direct slice actually gives

On the region \(v_5\neq 0\), the draft defines
\[
\mu:=\frac{c}{v_5},\qquad
\lambda:=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5},\qquad
\phi:=\lambda^{[1]}.
\]

And on the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice it proves
\[
\phi
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6^{(q^{(7)})}
+
v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)^{(q^{(7)})}
\Bigr),
\]
with the combined coefficient of the tagged monomial
\[
\kappa\,\eta_2\,q^{(7)}
\]
nonzero on the generic one-pair region.

That means the direct highest-new scalar is **linear in \(q^{(7)}\)** with a generically nonvanishing analytic coefficient.

So, after freezing the lower background data, there is an analytic function \(A(h)\not\equiv0\) such that on the direct slice
\[
\boxed{
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
}
\]

On any smaller subinterval where \(A(h)\neq 0\), this can be inverted:
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=A(h)^{-1}\phi(h).
\]

That is the key point.

---

## 2. Jet-surjectivity of the direct slice

Now fix \(h_0\) in a generic one-pair region with
\[
A(h_0)\neq 0,\qquad M(h_0)\neq 0,\qquad v_5(h_0)\neq 0.
\]

Then for any prescribed analytic germ \(\psi(h)\) near \(h_0\), one can choose
\[
q^{(7)}(h):=A(h)^{-1}\psi(h),
\]
and obtain
\[
\phi(h)=\psi(h)
\]
on the direct slice.

So the map
\[
q^{(7)}\longmapsto \phi
\]
is locally surjective on analytic germs.

Equivalently, because \(\mu\) is a local analytic coordinate when \(M\neq 0\),
the map
\[
q^{(7)}\longmapsto \phi(\mu)
\]
is locally surjective on analytic \(\mu\)-germs as well.

So in particular:

- any prescribed finite jet of \(\phi\) at \(\mu_0\) can be realized;
- any prescribed finite jet of \(\phi_\mu\) or \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\) can be realized;
- any prescribed local inflection pattern of \(\phi\) can be realized;
- and any prescribed local slope-window behavior can be realized.

That is the theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{direct-slice jet-surjectivity.}
\quad
\text{On a generic one-pair block, the direct }q^{(7)}\text{-slice can realize arbitrary local }\phi\text{-jets.}
}
\]

---

## 3. Consequence

This kills the hope that the **explicit direct formula alone** might force:

- one sign of \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\),
- at most one zero of \(W^{[1]}\),
- ordered slope windows,
- no nonadjacent slope re-entry,
- or no bitangent / four-secant recurrence.

All of those are properties of the local or semilocal graph geometry of \(\phi\), and jet-surjectivity says that, on the direct slice alone, \(\phi\) is still as free as an arbitrary analytic scalar graph.

So the strongest honest statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the explicit direct }q^{(7)}\text{-slice formula and generic noncancellation do not by themselves imply any global nonreentry theorem.}
}
\]

This is stronger than the earlier warning “the coefficient is not manifestly one-signed.” It says:

> even full control of the direct formula at the present level still leaves \(\phi\) locally arbitrary.

So the direct scalar graph lane is exhausted **unless** you can add a theorem that constrains \(q^{(7)}\) itself.

---

## 4. What the next theorem must be

The next live target is now very precise:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{\(q^{(7)}\)-constraint theorem.}
}
\]

Not a theorem about \(\phi\) in the abstract.

Not a theorem about arbitrary direct-slice graph geometry.

But a theorem saying that on the actual RH branch, the analytic function \(q^{(7)}\) is **not free** in the way the direct slice allows.

In practical terms, the next theorem would have to look like one of these:

\[
\boxed{
q^{(7)} \text{ has a sign / oscillation / variation restriction on the relevant one-pair block;}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
q^{(7)} \text{ is linked to }\eta_2,\ \kappa,\ \nu_6,\ \gamma_5-\alpha_5
\text{ by an RH-specific differential identity;}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{the actual RH provenance forces the direct slice to lie in a proper subspace of the full jet space of }\phi.
}
\]

That is the first theorem that would escape the jet-surjectivity no-go.

---

## 5. The strategic conclusion

So the next math push is no longer “differentiate the direct formula more.”

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find the first RH-specific restriction on }q^{(7)}\text{ (or on the direct provenance that produces it).}
}
\]

Because without that, the direct weight-one graph \(\phi\) is still locally arbitrary, and every convexity / two-zero / window-order idea can be defeated by choosing \(q^{(7)}\) appropriately.

That is, I think, the cleanest and strongest continuation of the attack.

_Thought for 1m 49s_

Here is the strongest clean push I can get now.

The direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice is not just jet-surjective. It is actually **full local-germ universal**. That means every attack based only on the explicit direct formula for
\[
\phi
\]
is dead unless you add a **nonlocal provenance theorem for \(q\)**.

That is the real mathematical conclusion.

Write the direct slice as
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h),
\]
where
\[
A(h):=\kappa(h)\,\eta_2(h)
\]
is the nonvanishing analytic coefficient from the draft’s direct \(q^{(7)}\)-formula on a generic one-pair block.

Now fix a connected subinterval \(J\) on which
\[
A(h)\neq 0.
\]

Then for any analytic function \(\psi\) on \(J\), define
\[
q^{(7)}(h):=A(h)^{-1}\psi(h).
\]
Integrating seven times gives an analytic \(q\) on \(J\), unique up to an arbitrary polynomial of degree at most \(6\), and by construction
\[
\phi(h)=\psi(h)
\qquad(h\in J).
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{full germ universality of the direct slice.}
\quad
\text{On any generic one-pair block, the direct slice } \phi
\text{ can be any analytic scalar graph.}
}
\]

That is stronger than what we had before. It means not only any local jet, but any local or semilocal graph behavior of \(\phi\) can be realized.

So all attacks of the following kind are now formally impossible without extra provenance on \(q\):

- sign of \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\),
- number of zeros of \(W^{[1]}\),
- slope-window ordering,
- no bitangent / no four-secant recurrence,
- any abstract graph-convexity statement about \(\phi\).

Because \(\phi\) is literally arbitrary on the direct slice.

That is the new no-go theorem.

There is, however, one genuinely useful thing you can do with it: it tells you **exactly** what the next theorem must look like.

Any recurrence condition on the direct slice is a linear condition on \(\phi\). For example, a common tangent line at two points \(\mu_1\neq \mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\phi_\mu(\mu_1)-\phi_\mu(\mu_2)=0
\]
and
\[
\phi(\mu_2)-\phi(\mu_1)-\phi_\mu(\mu_1)(\mu_2-\mu_1)=0.
\]

Substituting
\[
\phi=A q^{(7)}
\]
turns these into linear functionals of \(q^{(7)}\). After integrating by parts seven times, each one becomes
\[
\Lambda(\phi)=\text{boundary terms involving }q,\dots,q^{(6)}
+\int q(\xi)\,K_\Lambda(\xi)\,d\xi.
\]

That is the real structural reduction.

So the whole direct-slice problem now becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove boundary/provenance conditions on }q
\text{ strong enough to kill the boundary terms and constrain the integral term.}
}
\]

Without that, \(q^{(7)}\) is arbitrary and \(\phi\) is arbitrary.

So the next attack is no longer “study \(\phi\) more.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a nonlocal provenance theorem for }q.
}
\]

Concretely, the theorem you want next is something like this:

> On a generic one-pair block, the actual RH-generated \(q\) is not an arbitrary analytic function; its \(0\)th through \(6\)th boundary data at the ends of the block are fixed, or tied by matching conditions to adjacent blocks, in a way that annihilates the sevenfold integration-by-parts boundary freedom.

If you can get that, then the direct-slice line-recurrence equations become genuine integral identities for \(q\) with explicit kernels, and *that* is the first place a sign-definite contradiction could live.

So the math push has reached a very sharp endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct slice is completely free locally;}
\quad
\text{only nonlocal boundary provenance of }q\text{ can still close it.}
}
\]

That is the next theorem I would attack.

Here is the clean theorem.

---

Let \(J\) be a connected generic one-pair block on which the direct-slice coefficient
\[
A(h):=\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)

is analytic and nowhere zero. On that block the draft’s direct provenance formula is
\[

\[
q_\psi(h)
:=
\sum_{j=0}^6 \frac{a_j}{j!}(h-h_0)^j
+\int_{h_0}^h \frac{(h-s)^6}{6!}\,\frac{\psi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds,

with arbitrary constants \(a_0,\dots,a_6\).

Then
\[
q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\frac{\psi(h)}{A(h)},
\]
so
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\psi(h)
\qquad (h\in J).
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{

\text{ is surjective onto all analytic scalar graphs.}

\]

That is stronger than local jet-surjectivity. It is full block universality.

This immediately kills a huge class of possible theorems.

Anything that depends only on the explicit direct-slice graph \(y=\phi(\mu)\) on one such block is now formally too weak, because \(\phi\) can be any analytic function.

That includes:

- upper bounds on the number of zeros of \(W^{[1]}\),
- slope-window ordering,
- no bitangent / no four-secant theorems,
- no nonadjacent slope re-entry,
- any convexity/ECT theorem phrased only in terms of the direct-slice graph.

All of those are just properties of an arbitrary analytic \(\phi\), and arbitrary analytic \(\phi\) are realizable.

\[
\boxed{
\text{no theorem that uses only the explicit direct formula }\phi=Aq^{(7)}
\text{ on a single generic one-pair block can close the branch.}
}

You can even make the dependence on the seven integration constants explicit. If
\[
Q(h):=\bigl(q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(6)}(h)\bigr)^T,
\]
then for \(a<b\) in \(J\),
\[
Q(b)=T_{b-a}Q(a)+\int_a^b K(b-s)\,\frac{\phi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds,
\]
where \(T_{b-a}\) is the usual \(7\times7\) Taylor-shift matrix and
\[
K(r)=\begin{pmatrix}
r^6/6!\\ r^5/5!\\ \vdots\\ r\\ 1
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
So even semiglobal blockwise behavior of \(\phi\) is just “forcing” in a free seventh-order transport system.

That tells you what the next theorem must look like.

It cannot be another theorem about \(\phi\) itself.

It has to be an **external provenance theorem for \(q\)**, meaning some RH-specific relation that constrains \(q\) beyond the freedom of solving
\[
Aq^{(7)}=\phi.
\]

In other words, the next live target is:

\[
\boxed{

\text{ that is not already encoded by } \phi=Aq^{(7)}.
}
\]

A theorem of any of these kinds would be genuinely new:

\[
q \text{ satisfies a lower-order ODE or integral equation on the block,}
\]
or
\[
(q,q',\dots,q^{(6)}) \text{ at one boundary is constrained by other RH data,}
\]
or
\[
q \text{ is coupled to the mixed/septic provenance in a way that cuts down the allowed forcing space.}
\]

That is the sharp conclusion of the attack:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct provenance slice is fully universal on each generic block;}
\quad
\text{only an external, nontrivial provenance law for }q\text{ can still close it.}
}

So the next math push I would make is no longer on \(\phi\)-geometry at all. It is:

## 1. Variation-of-constants makes the freedom explicit

On any generic one-pair block \(J\) where
\[
A(h)\neq 0,
\]
the direct slice is
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Write
\[
Q(h):=
\begin{pmatrix}
q(h)\\ q'(h)\\ \vdots\\ q^{(6)}(h)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then for any \(a,b\in J\),
\[
Q(b)=T_{b-a}Q(a)+\int_a^b K(b-s)\,\frac{\phi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds,
\]
where \(T_{b-a}\) is the usual \(7\times 7\) Taylor-shift matrix and
\[
K(r)=
\begin{pmatrix}
r^6/6!\\
r^5/5!\\
\vdots\\
r\\
1
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

So every boundary jet of \(q\) is just an affine-linear functional of \(\phi\).

That is the key reduction.

---

## 2. Finite matching conditions impose only finite-codimension restrictions on \(\phi\)

Suppose you impose any finite collection of linear conditions on endpoint jets, for example
\[
L_\ell\bigl(Q(\xi_1),\dots,Q(\xi_m)\bigr)=0
\qquad (\ell=1,\dots,N),
\]
at finitely many block boundaries \(\xi_j\).

By the formula above, each condition becomes
\[
\Lambda_\ell(\phi)=c_\ell,
\]
a linear functional of \(\phi\).

So the set of admissible \(\phi\) is an affine subspace of codimension at most \(N\) inside the infinite-dimensional space of analytic functions on the block union.

In particular:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finitely many jet-matching conditions on }q,\dots,q^{(6)}
\text{ do not force }\phi\text{ into anything like a one-sign / one-window / no-bitangent class.}
}
\]

There is still infinite-dimensional freedom left.

---

## 3. Polynomial realization makes this concrete

You can see this explicitly by taking
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=P(h),
\]
with \(P\) a polynomial of degree \(d\), so
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)P(h).
\]

Then \(q\) is a polynomial of degree \(d+7\), and the boundary jet constraints become a finite linear system in the \(d+1\) coefficients of \(P\).

Once
\[
d+1>N,
\]
there are nontrivial solutions. And for \(d\) larger still, you retain free coefficients after satisfying the boundary conditions, so you can simultaneously impose semilocal graph features of \(\phi\) such as:

- prescribed local slopes,
- prescribed inflection locations,
- prescribed bitangent behavior,
- prescribed slope-window overlap.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite jet matching at block boundaries cannot by itself rule out the direct-slice recurrence geometries.}
}
\]

---

## 4. What this means for the current draft

This is the useful conclusion:

The current mixed / provenance package will only help if it gives something stronger than finite-order closure and finite boundary matching.

In other words, the next live theorem cannot be merely:

- “\([A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\) matches across boundaries,”
- or “\(q,\dots,q^{(6)}\) satisfy these finitely many endpoint relations,”
- or any other finite-dimensional compatibility statement.

All of that still leaves \(\phi\) infinite-dimensional and flexible.

So the next theorem has to be of one of these types:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{an operator equation for }q
}
\]
such as a lower-order ODE, Volterra equation, or integral identity on a whole block;

or

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a sign-definite kernel relation for }q
}
\]
that constrains \(q^{(7)}\) on a continuum, not just at finitely many points;

or

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a provenance law that cuts the allowed }\phi
\text{ down to a proper infinite-codimension class.}
}
\]

That is the first kind of theorem not already killed by universality.

---

## 5. The next concrete attack

So the next push I would make is this:

### Target theorem
Prove that on the actual RH branch, the provenance function \(q\) satisfies a **blockwise differential or integral relation** not equivalent to
\[
\phi=Aq^{(7)}.
\]

### Why this is the right target
Because only such a law can cut down the infinite-dimensional freedom left after all finite-jet matching.

### Practical form
I would look for a theorem of the form
\[
\mathcal L(q)=\mathcal F(c,v_5,u_5,\lambda,\Delta_7,\dots),
\]
where \(\mathcal L\) has order \(<7\), or is an integral operator with sign-definite kernel, and where the right-hand side is already controlled by earlier packages.

If you can get anything like that, then the direct-slice graph \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\) stops being arbitrary, and the graph-geometry lane becomes live again.

---

So the attack has now reached a very sharp endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{direct-slice geometry + finite provenance matching is spent;}
\quad
\text{only a continuum-strength law for }q\text{ can still close it.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:01:54)

I’m pushing on the “continuum-strength law for \(q\)” bottleneck now. The right question is whether the current setup secretly gives any nonlocal integral constraint on \(q\), or whether even blockwise operator laws remain formally too free.

_Thought for 1m 31s_

Yes. There is a genuinely stronger no-go theorem here.

It shows that even **finitely many provenance conditions on \(q\)** are still too weak. So the next live attack must use a **continuum-strength law** for \(q\), not just matching of finitely many jets across blocks.

---

## Theorem: finite-jet provenance cannot exclude direct-slice recurrences

Let \(J\) be a generic one-pair block on which
\[
A(h):=\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)
\]
is analytic and nowhere zero, so the direct provenance slice is
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Fix finitely many points
\[
\xi_1,\dots,\xi_M\in J
\]
and at each \(\xi_i\) fix finitely many jet values
\[
q^{(k)}(\xi_i)=b_{i,k},
\qquad 0\le k\le r_i.
\]

Fix also two distinct interior points
\[
\mu_1,\mu_2\in J,
\qquad \mu_1\neq \mu_2,
\]
and any affine line
\[
\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b.
\]

Then there exists a polynomial \(q\) such that:

1. all the prescribed jet conditions at the \(\xi_i\) hold;
2. the direct-slice graph
   \[
   \phi(\mu)=A(\mu)\,q^{(7)}(\mu)
   \]
   is tangent to the line \(y=\ell(\mu)\) at both \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\), i.e.
   \[
   \phi(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
   \qquad
   \phi_\mu(\mu_j)=s
   \qquad (j=1,2).
   \]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no theorem using only finitely many pointwise jet/matching conditions on }q
\text{ can exclude bitangent or same-slope recurrence on the direct slice.}
}
\]

---

## Proof

At each tangency point \(\mu_j\), the conditions
\[
\phi(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\qquad
\phi_\mu(\mu_j)=s
\]
become linear jet conditions on \(q\).

Indeed, since
\[
\phi=Aq^{(7)},
\]
the value condition is
\[
A(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\]
so
\[
q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\frac{\ell(\mu_j)}{A(\mu_j)}.
\]

Also
\[
\phi_\mu=(Aq^{(7)})'=A' q^{(7)}+A q^{(8)},
\]
so the slope condition becomes
\[
A'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)+A(\mu_j)\,q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=s,
\]
hence
\[
q^{(8)}(\mu_j)
=
\frac{s-A'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)}{A(\mu_j)}.
\]

So all desired requirements are finitely many linear jet conditions on the single unknown function \(q\), namely:

- the prescribed jets \(q^{(k)}(\xi_i)=b_{i,k}\),
- and at \(\mu_1,\mu_2\),
  \[
  q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\frac{\ell(\mu_j)}{A(\mu_j)},
  \qquad
  q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=\frac{s-A'(\mu_j)\ell(\mu_j)/A(\mu_j)}{A(\mu_j)}.
  \]

Because \(A(\mu_j)\neq 0\), these are legitimate finite jet prescriptions.

Now apply standard Hermite interpolation: a polynomial of sufficiently large degree can realize arbitrary finite jets at finitely many points. Therefore there exists a polynomial \(q\) satisfying all of them.

For that polynomial,
\[
\phi=Aq^{(7)}
\]
has the prescribed tangencies at \(\mu_1,\mu_2\), and the prescribed provenance jets at the \(\xi_i\). ∎

---

## Stronger form

The same argument works for any finite list of secant/tangent events.

For example, if you want \(\phi\) to have:

- a tangent line at two points,
- a third point with the same slope,
- a prescribed inflection point,
- and arbitrary finite matching jets of \(q\) at finitely many boundaries,

all of those are still finitely many jet conditions on \(q\), hence realizable by a sufficiently high-degree polynomial.

So in fact:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-dimensional provenance data on }q
\text{ leave the direct slice infinitely flexible.}
}
\]

---

## What this means

This is stronger than the earlier universality results.

Before, we had shown:

- the lower route-2 package is universal;
- the explicit direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\) is locally / blockwise universal.

Now we can say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{even after imposing any finite set of RH-style jet matching conditions on }q,
\text{ the direct slice can still realize forbidden recurrence geometry.}
}
\]

So the next live theorem cannot be about:

- endpoint jets of \(q\),
- finite-order matching across finitely many block boundaries,
- finitely many linear side conditions on \(q\),
- or any finite-codimension restriction.

All of that is still too weak.

---

## The new frontier

The next attack must use a **continuum-strength** relation on \(q\), such as:

- a lower-order ODE on a whole block,
- a Volterra/Fredholm-type integral identity,
- a sign-definite kernel relation,
- or a transport law coupling \(q\) across a continuum of points.

In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the only remaining live way to constrain the direct provenance slice is by an infinite-codimension law for }q.
}
\]

That is the sharpest further push I can justify.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:05:55)

I’m trying to push past the “finite-jet conditions are too weak” result to the next clean ceiling: whether even a local differential law for \(q\) would still leave too much freedom, or whether a truly nonlocal relation is necessary.

_Thought for 1m 23s_

Here is the strongest next push I can justify.

It sharpens “finite-jet provenance is too weak” to:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{any finite family of linear provenance constraints on }q\text{ is still too weak.}}
\]

So the next live theorem cannot be a finite collection of endpoint jet conditions, moment conditions, or finitely many linear integral identities. It has to be an **infinite-codimension** law on \(q\), and probably a sign-definite or nonlinear one.

---

Work on a generic one-pair block \(J\) where
\[
A(h):=\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Fix two distinct points
\[
\mu_1\neq \mu_2\in J
\]
and an affine line
\[
\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b.
\]

A bitangent condition for the direct slice is
\[
\phi(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\qquad
\phi_\mu(\mu_j)=s
\qquad (j=1,2).
\]
Since \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\), these are linear conditions on \(q\):
\[
A(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\]
\[
A'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)+A(\mu_j)\,q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=s.
\]

So a prescribed bitangent uses only the four linear jet conditions
\[
q^{(7)}(\mu_1),\ q^{(8)}(\mu_1),\ q^{(7)}(\mu_2),\ q^{(8)}(\mu_2).
\]

Now let the provenance side impose any finite family of linear conditions on \(q\), for example
\[
\Lambda_r(q)=c_r,\qquad r=1,\dots,N,
\]
where each \(\Lambda_r\) is built from finitely many endpoint jets and/or finitely many integral functionals with analytic kernels, e.g.
\[
\Lambda_r(q)
=
\sum_{i,k} a_{rik}\,q^{(k)}(\xi_i)
+
\sum_m \int_J K_{rm}(h)\,q(h)\,dh.
\]

Then the total system
\[
\Lambda_r(q)=c_r
\quad (r=1,\dots,N),
\]
together with the four bitangent conditions above, is still just a **finite linear system** on the coefficients of \(q\).

Take \(q\) to be a polynomial of degree \(d\). Its coefficient space has dimension \(d+1\). For \(d\) large enough, this dimension exceeds the rank of the whole finite system, so—provided the finitely many functionals are independent on sufficiently high-degree polynomials—the system has solutions. In particular, one can realize a direct-slice bitangent while simultaneously satisfying any prescribed finite family of linear provenance conditions.

So the conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite linear provenance conditions on }q
\text{ do not rule out direct-slice recurrence geometry.}
}
\]

This is strictly stronger than the earlier finite-jet no-go. It covers:

- endpoint matching of \(q,\dots,q^{(6)}\),
- finitely many jet conditions at finitely many points,
- finitely many linear moment conditions,
- finitely many linear integral identities.

All of that is still finite-codimension in the space of possible \(q\), while bitangent / recurrence data only impose finitely many more linear conditions.

---

That means the next live target has to be one of these:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a continuum-strength law for }q
}
\]

such as:

- a lower-order ODE on a whole block,
- a Volterra/Fredholm-type integral equation,
- a sign-definite kernel identity,
- or some nonlinear provenance relation that cuts the allowed \(q\) down to an infinite-codimension class.

So the attack has sharpened the frontier again:

\[
\boxed{
\text{direct-slice geometry + any finite family of linear provenance constraints is spent.}
}
\]

The next theorem has to constrain \(q\) on a whole interval, not just at finitely many places or through finitely many linear functionals.

The next thing I would try is to inspect the mixed/canonical provenance package for any relation on \(q\) that is genuinely operator-level rather than finite-jet-level.

## 1) Insert this immediately after `Remark~\ref{rem:status-direct-septic-witness}`

```latex
\begin{remark}[Canonical quotient-septic closure is still locally free]
\label{rem:canonical-quotient-septic-closure-still-locally-free}
The canonical quotient-level order-$7$ package is now closed, but that does not
yet make the quotient-septic geometry rigid.

Indeed, once the lower one-pair data $(c,u_5,v_5)$ are fixed, the canonical
septic residual
\[
\Delta_7=u_7v_5-u_5v_7
\]
is still locally free. On a region where $v_5\neq 0$ one may gauge-fix
\[
v_7^{\new}=0,
\qquad
u_7^{\new}=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5},
\]
while on a region where $u_5\neq 0$ one may gauge-fix
\[
u_7^{\new}=0,
\qquad
v_7^{\new}=-\frac{\Delta_7}{u_5}.
\]
So after the lower one-pair data are fixed, the canonical quotient-septic curve
\[
\Gamma(h)=\left(\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}\right)
\]
is still locally determined by an arbitrary analytic choice of $\Delta_7$.

Accordingly, no theorem using only arbitrary canonical quotient-septic geometry
of $(c,v_5,\Delta_7)$ can by itself close the later repeated-source /
discriminant branch. Any successful higher-order hybrid must therefore be
provenance-sensitive, not merely quotient-septic.
\end{remark}
```

## 2) Replace the entire current remark `\ref{rem:wip-highest-new-scalar-direct-slice}`

Find the whole block

```latex
\begin{remark}[Work in progress: highest-new scalar on the direct septic slice]
\label{rem:wip-highest-new-scalar-direct-slice}
```

and replace it with:

```latex
\begin{remark}[Status and ceiling of the highest-new direct provenance slice]
\label{rem:wip-highest-new-scalar-direct-slice}
On the region $v_5\neq 0$, set
\[
\mu:=\frac{c}{v_5},
\qquad
\lambda:=\frac{\Delta_7}{v_5},
\qquad
\phi:=\lambda^{[1]}.
\]
By Corollary~\ref{cor:direct-q7-septic-slice},
Corollary~\ref{cor:weight-one-septic-reduction}, and
Theorem~\ref{thm:quotient-septic-closure}, the highest-new quotient-level
septic scalar is already reduced to the direct $q^{(7)}$-driven $K_1$-slice:
\[
\Delta_7^{[1]}=v_5\,\phi,
\qquad
\phi
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6^{(q^{(7)})}
+
v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)^{(q^{(7)})}
\Bigr).
\]

On the subregion where $M\neq 0$, the direct highest-new dual variables are
\[
\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu,
\qquad
m^{[1]}=\phi-\mu\phi_\mu,
\]
and by Corollary~\ref{cor:mu-lambda-curvature-identities},
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}.
\]
Thus the direct highest-new quotient-septic geometry is exactly the ordinary
tangent-line geometry of the scalar graph
\[
y=\phi(\mu).
\]

In particular, any bitangent line or four-secant line for the graph
$y=\phi(\mu)$, and any nonadjacent slope re-entry for
\[
\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu,
\]
forces at least two zeros of
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu},
\]
equivalently of
\[
W^{[1]}.
\]
So the global direct-slice propagation problem may be viewed as a no-recurrence
problem for the scalar graph $\phi$.

However, the explicit direct provenance formula only shows that
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}
\qquad
(A:=\kappa\,\eta_2),
\]
with $A$ generically nonvanishing on a generic one-pair block. By
Proposition~\ref{prop:full-block-universality-direct-provenance-slice} below,
this still leaves $\phi$ fully universal on each such block. So the direct
graph picture is now a precise dictionary, not by itself a live rigidity
theorem.
\end{remark}
```

## 3) Insert this block immediately after `Remark~\ref{rem:warning-no-easy-globalization}` and before `\begin{lemma}[Crude polynomial bound for the local value scale]`

```latex
\begin{proposition}[Full-block universality of the direct provenance slice]
\label{prop:full-block-universality-direct-provenance-slice}
Let $J$ be a connected generic one-pair block on which
\[
A(h):=\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)
\]
is analytic and nowhere zero. On $J$, the direct provenance slice has the form
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]
Then for every analytic function
\[
\psi:J\to\mathbf C
\]
there exists an analytic function
\[
q:J\to\mathbf C
\]
such that
\[
\phi(h)=\psi(h)
\qquad (h\in J).
\]

More explicitly, for any basepoint $h_0\in J$ and any constants
\[
a_0,\dots,a_6\in\mathbf C,
\]
the function
\[
q_\psi(h)
:=
\sum_{j=0}^6 \frac{a_j}{j!}(h-h_0)^j
+
\int_{h_0}^h \frac{(h-s)^6}{6!}\,\frac{\psi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds
\]
is analytic on $J$ and satisfies
\[
q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\frac{\psi(h)}{A(h)},
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\psi(h).
\]
In particular, on any generic one-pair block, the direct highest-new scalar
graph $\phi$ is an arbitrary analytic scalar graph.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Since $A$ is analytic and nowhere zero on $J$, the function
\[
\frac{\psi}{A}
\]
is analytic on $J$. The displayed formula for $q_\psi$ is the standard
variation-of-constants solution of
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\psi}{A},
\]
with arbitrary initial data at $h_0$. Differentiating seven times gives
\[
q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\frac{\psi(h)}{A(h)},
\]
hence
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q_\psi^{(7)}(h)=\psi(h).
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Finite linear provenance constraints are too weak]
\label{cor:finite-linear-provenance-too-weak}
Let $J$ be as in
Proposition~\ref{prop:full-block-universality-direct-provenance-slice}.
Fix finitely many linear functionals
\[
\Lambda_r(q)=c_r,
\qquad r=1,\dots,N,
\]
on analytic functions $q$ on $J$, where each $\Lambda_r$ is built from finitely
many point-evaluation jets and/or finitely many linear integral functionals
with analytic kernels.

Fix also two distinct points
\[
\mu_1\neq \mu_2\in J
\]
and an affine line
\[
\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b.
\]
Then there exists a polynomial $q$ on $J$ such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item all the prescribed linear provenance conditions
\[
\Lambda_r(q)=c_r
\qquad (r=1,\dots,N)
\]
hold;

\item the direct-slice graph
\[
\phi(\mu)=A(\mu)\,q^{(7)}(\mu)
\]
is tangent to the line $y=\ell(\mu)$ at both $\mu_1$ and $\mu_2$, i.e.
\[
\phi(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\qquad
\phi_\mu(\mu_j)=s
\qquad (j=1,2).
\]
\end{enumerate}

Accordingly, no theorem using only the explicit direct formula
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}
\]
together with any finite family of linear provenance or matching conditions on
$q$ can exclude bitangent, four-secant, or same-slope recurrence geometry on
the direct slice.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The tangency conditions at $\mu_j$ are linear jet conditions on $q$:
\[
A(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\]
and, since
\[
\phi_\mu=(Aq^{(7)})'=A' q^{(7)}+A q^{(8)},
\]
also
\[
A'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)+A(\mu_j)\,q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=s.
\]
Because $A(\mu_j)\neq 0$, these are legitimate linear conditions on the jets of
$q$ at the two points $\mu_1,\mu_2$.

Together with the prescribed finitely many provenance constraints
\[
\Lambda_r(q)=c_r,
\]
this is a finite linear system on the coefficients of a polynomial $q$. For a
polynomial of sufficiently large degree, the coefficient space has dimension
larger than the number of imposed linear conditions. Standard Hermite
interpolation / finite-dimensional linear algebra therefore gives a polynomial
$q$ satisfying all of them simultaneously.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What the direct-slice no-go results actually leave open]
\label{rem:what-direct-slice-no-go-results-leave-open}
Proposition~\ref{prop:full-block-universality-direct-provenance-slice} shows
that on a generic one-pair block the direct highest-new scalar graph
\[
\phi
\]
is fully universal. Corollary~\ref{cor:finite-linear-provenance-too-weak} shows
more: even after imposing any finite family of linear provenance / matching
conditions on $q$, the direct slice remains flexible enough to realize
bitangent, four-secant, and same-slope recurrence geometry.

So the next live theorem cannot be another sign/convexity statement about the
graph of $\phi$, nor any theorem using only finitely many endpoint jets or
finitely many linear integral conditions on $q$. Any successful continuation of
the direct-slice lane must instead use a genuinely
\emph{continuum-strength provenance law} for $q$ on a whole block, such as a
lower-order ODE, a sign-definite integral identity, a nonlinear transport law,
or some other infinite-codimension constraint.
\end{remark}
```

---

## 4) In `Remark~\ref{rem:current-state-of-argument}`, replace the paragraph beginning

```latex
Accordingly, the remaining gap is no longer local boundary geometry.
```

and ending with

```latex
... about nonadjacent slope re-entry for the highest-new scalar graph $\phi$.
```

with:

```latex
Accordingly, the equivalent slope/tangent/dual direct-slice formulations remain
the correct geometric dictionary, but they are no longer themselves the live
theorem target. The explicit direct provenance formula gives
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}
\qquad
(A=\kappa\,\eta_2),
\]
and on any generic one-pair block this direct slice is fully universal
(Proposition~\ref{prop:full-block-universality-direct-provenance-slice}).
Moreover, even after imposing any finite family of linear provenance or
matching conditions on $q$, direct-slice recurrence geometry remains formally
realizable (Corollary~\ref{cor:finite-linear-provenance-too-weak}).

scalar graph $\phi$ by itself. It is the search for a genuinely non-universal
provenance law for $q$, or an external mixed/canonical input, that cuts down
the allowed direct-slice graphs on an entire block.
```

My next attack angle is:

## Prove a **continuum-strength provenance law for \(q\)**, then feed it into the direct slice
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.
\]

That is the first place left where the proof could still get traction.

Why I think this is the right move:

- route 2 by itself is exhausted;
- arbitrary canonical \(\Delta_7\) is too free;
- the explicit direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\) is still fully universal on each generic block;
- and even any **finite** collection of jet/matching conditions on \(q\) is too weak.

So the next theorem has to constrain \(q\) on a whole interval, not at finitely many points.

The concrete version I would attack is one of these:

### 1. A blockwise ODE / transport law for \(q\)
Something like
\[
\mathcal L(q)=\text{known lower-order source data},
\]
with \(\operatorname{ord}\mathcal L<7\), or at least not equivalent to just prescribing \(q^{(7)}\).

If you can derive that, then \(q^{(7)}\) is no longer arbitrary, so \(\phi\) stops being arbitrary.

### 2. A sign-definite integral identity for \(q\)
Something like
\[
\int_J K(h,\xi)\,q(\xi)\,d\xi = \text{controlled quantity},
\]
with a kernel \(K\) strong enough to force variation restrictions on \(q^{(7)}\), hence on \(\phi\).

This would be enough to revive the direct-slice graph lane.

### 3. A mixed/canonical theorem that constrains \(q\) across a whole block
Not arbitrary quotient-septic geometry, but something provenance-sensitive from the mixed package that forces \(q\) to lie in a proper infinite-codimension class.

That is the first kind of input not already killed by universality.

\[

2. inspect whether the mixed bad-channel formulas or canonical closure identities impose any lower-order differential relation on \(q\),

4. then combine it with

\]
to get a nontrivial restriction on the direct-slice graph.

That is the best next angle because every weaker one has now been ruled out.

There is also a fallback angle, if that stalls:

The counterexample showed diagonal capture is false inside the current package. But it did so with completely free higher data. A stronger theorem could still say:

> on the actual RH branch, a continued born branch cannot approach the next odd anchor zero with a target trapped in a distant compact subinterval of the same nonmixed patch.

That would have to use mixed/canonical structure too, but it is a more geometric target.

So my priority order would be:

2. if that looks inaccessible, switch to **nonlocal-target exclusion using higher data**.

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:24:47)

_Thought for 3m 20s_

I think there is a real new reduction now.

The right way to think about the “continuum-strength law for \(q\)” is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{any blockwise provenance law for }q\text{ becomes an operator equation for }\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.}
\]

That is useful because it tells you exactly what kind of theorem would finally have teeth.

---

### 1. The universal reconstruction formula

Fix a generic one-pair block \(J\), a basepoint \(h_0\in J\), and write
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad A(h)\neq 0 \text{ on }J.
\]
Then every \(q\) can be written as
\[
q(h)=P_6(h)+\int_{h_0}^h \frac{(h-s)^6}{6!}\,\frac{\phi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds,
\]
where \(P_6\) is the degree-\(\le 6\) polynomial encoding the initial jet
\[
q(h_0),q'(h_0),\dots,q^{(6)}(h_0).
\]

Differentiating gives the exact formulas
\[
q^{(k)}(h)=P_6^{(k)}(h)+\int_{h_0}^h \frac{(h-s)^{6-k}}{(6-k)!}\,\frac{\phi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds
\qquad (0\le k\le 6),
\]
and for \(k\ge 7\),
\[
q^{(k)}(h)=\frac{d^{\,k-7}}{dh^{\,k-7}}\!\left(\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)}\right).
\]

So the whole provenance of \(q\) is exactly:
- a \(7\)-parameter polynomial jet \(P_6\),
- plus the blockwise forcing \(\phi/A\).

---

### 2. What a genuine provenance law would become

Now suppose the mixed/canonical package were to produce a blockwise linear differential law
\[
\mathcal L q:=\sum_{k=0}^r b_k(h)\,q^{(k)}(h)=F(h)
\]
on \(J\), with analytic coefficients \(b_k\).

Substituting the reconstruction formula gives:

\[
\sum_{k=0}^{\min(r,6)} b_k(h)\,P_6^{(k)}(h)
+
\sum_{k=0}^{\min(r,6)} b_k(h)\int_{h_0}^h
\frac{(h-s)^{6-k}}{(6-k)!}\,\frac{\phi(s)}{A(s)}\,ds
\]
\[
\qquad\qquad
+
\sum_{k=7}^{r} b_k(h)\,
\frac{d^{\,k-7}}{dh^{\,k-7}}\!\left(\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)}\right)
=
F(h).
\]

So every such provenance law becomes an **integro-differential equation for \(\phi\)**.

That is the key structural reduction.

---

### 3. Two important special cases

#### Case A: \(r\le 6\)

Then there is no local differential term in \(\phi\), only Volterra memory:
\[
\sum_{k=0}^{r} b_k(h)\,P_6^{(k)}(h)
+
\int_{h_0}^h K_r(h,s)\,\phi(s)\,ds
=
F(h),
\]
with
\[
K_r(h,s):=
\sum_{k=0}^{r}\frac{b_k(h)}{(6-k)!\,A(s)}(h-s)^{6-k}.
\]

So a lower-order provenance law becomes a **Volterra equation** for \(\phi\).

After differentiating \(7-r\) times, the top kernel term produces a local \(\phi\)-term:
\[
\frac{b_r(h)}{A(h)}\,\phi(h)
+
\int_{h_0}^h \widetilde K_r(h,s)\,\phi(s)\,ds
=
\widetilde F(h).
\]

So any blockwise law of order \(\le 6\) would put \(\phi\) into a genuine Volterra class.

That is the first kind of theorem that could realistically kill the direct-slice universality.

#### Case B: \(r\ge 7\)

Then one gets a local differential part in \(\phi\):
\[
\sum_{j=0}^{r-7} c_j(h)\,\phi^{(j)}(h)
+
\int_{h_0}^h K_r(h,s)\,\phi(s)\,ds
=
\widetilde F(h),
\]
for explicit analytic coefficients \(c_j\) built from \(b_k\) and \(A\).

So a higher-order provenance law becomes an **integro-differential equation** for \(\phi\).

This is also potentially useful, but unlike the lower-order case it does not automatically force any positivity or variation-diminishing structure.

---

### 4. Why this is the real next frontier

This sharpens the earlier no-go results.

We already know:

- finite jet conditions on \(q\) are too weak;
- arbitrary canonical \(\Delta_7\) is too free;
- the explicit direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\) is fully universal on a block.

What the calculation above adds is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first kind of provenance law that could have real teeth is a blockwise operator law for }q,
}
\]
because it translates into a blockwise Volterra or integro-differential equation for \(\phi\).

So the next live theorem is no longer vague. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{derive an operator-level provenance law for }q\text{ from the mixed/canonical package.}
}
\]

And the best subcase is especially clear:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{derive a provenance law of order }\le 6\text{ for }q.
}
\]

Because then \(\phi\) would satisfy a Volterra equation, which is exactly the kind of structure that might rule out the arbitrary bitangent / recurrence geometries that killed the purely local graph attacks.

---

### 5. The next concrete attack

So the next thing I would actually do is this:

Try to locate, in the mixed/canonical package, any relation that can be rewritten as
\[
\sum_{k=0}^{r} b_k(h)\,q^{(k)}(h)=F(h)
\qquad\text{with }r\le 6.
\]

If such a law exists, route 2 is alive again, because it would turn the direct slice from “arbitrary analytic graph” into a Volterra-controlled class.

If no such lower-order law exists, then the next best target is an integro-differential law with a sign-definite kernel or a sign-definite leading local term.

So the attack has now become very precise:

\[
\boxed{
\text{stop studying }\phi\text{ abstractly; mine the mixed/canonical package for the first operator law on }q.
}
\]

That is the next push I would make.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:27:00)

I’m pushing directly on the only live target left: whether the mixed/canonical package could produce a genuine blockwise operator law for \(q\), especially one of order \(\le 6\), which would finally constrain the universal direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\).

_Thought for 1m 10s_

Here is the strongest next push I can justify.

I think the right new theorem is a **gauge-obstruction theorem for \(q\)**.

It says:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{any canonical blockwise law for }q\text{ that depends only on the current direct-slice data}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{must annihilate the full degree-}\le 6\text{ polynomial gauge. In particular, no nontrivial local differential law of order }\le 6\text{ can exist.}
}
\]

That is a real structural advance, because it tells you exactly what kind of provenance theorem is even possible now.

---

## 1. The polynomial gauge is unavoidable

On a generic one-pair block \(J\), the current direct provenance slice is
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)},
\qquad A\neq 0.
\]

So \(\phi\) determines \(q\) only up to addition of a polynomial of degree at most \(6\):
\[
q\sim q+P,\qquad P\in\Pi_6.
\]

Equivalently, any theorem extracted solely from the present direct-slice data can only depend on the class
\[
[q]\in \mathcal O(J)/\Pi_6.
\]

So if a candidate provenance law
\[
\mathcal T(q)=\mathcal F
\]
is supposed to be canonical at the current level, then it must be invariant under this gauge:
\[
\mathcal T(q+P)=\mathcal T(q)
\qquad
(\forall P\in\Pi_6).
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal T(P)=0
\qquad
(\forall P\in\Pi_6).
}
\]

That is the key constraint.

---

## 2. Consequence for differential laws

Now suppose
\[
\mathcal T=\sum_{k=0}^r b_k(h)\,\partial_h^k
\]
is a linear differential operator of order \(r\le 6\).

If \(\mathcal T(P)=0\) for every \(P\in\Pi_6\), then \(\mathcal T\equiv 0\).

The proof is triangular. Take shifted monomials
\[
1,\ (h-h_0),\ (h-h_0)^2,\ \dots,\ (h-h_0)^r
\]
at an arbitrary point \(h_0\).

Applying \(\mathcal T\) to \(1\) gives
\[
b_0(h)=0.
\]
Then applying it to \((h-h_0)\) gives
\[
b_1(h)=0.
\]
Continuing inductively, one gets
\[
b_0=b_1=\cdots=b_r=0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{there is no nontrivial canonical linear differential law for }q\text{ of order }\le 6.
}
\]

That is much sharper than “finite-jet conditions are too weak.”

It says the next provenance law cannot be a lower-order ODE for \(q\), unless it comes with extra data that fix the polynomial gauge.

---

## 3. Consequence for integral laws

Now suppose
\[
(\mathcal T q)(h)=\int_J K(h,s)\,q(s)\,ds
\]
is a linear integral law extracted from the current package.

Gauge invariance forces
\[
\int_J K(h,s)\,P(s)\,ds=0
\qquad
(\forall P\in\Pi_6),
\]
hence the moment conditions
\[
\boxed{
\int_J K(h,s)\,s^m\,ds=0
\qquad
(m=0,1,\dots,6).
}
\]

So any canonical linear kernel law must annihilate the first seven polynomial moments.

That has an immediate consequence:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{no sign-definite kernel law can work.}
}
\]

Because already the zeroth moment condition
\[
\int_J K(h,s)\,ds=0
\]
forces any fixed-sign kernel to vanish identically.

So if a continuum-strength provenance law exists at this level, it cannot be a simple positive-kernel Volterra/Fredholm identity. It must be much subtler.

---

## 4. What this means strategically

This is the clean conclusion:

- a lower-order ODE for \(q\) is impossible at the current gauge level;
- a simple sign-definite integral kernel law is impossible too;
- any linear operator law must kill the full \(\Pi_6\) gauge.

So the next viable theorem has to do **one** of three things:

### A. Fix the seven-dimensional gauge
Provide seven independent boundary / matching conditions on
\[
q,\ q',\ \dots,\ q^{(6)}
\]
coming from genuinely external mixed/canonical structure.

Then a lower-order differential law could become meaningful.

### B. Use an order-\(\ge 7\) operator with extra coupling
A law involving \(q^{(7)}\) or higher is not killed by the gauge, but then it must do more than simply restate
\[
\phi=Aq^{(7)}.
\]

So it would need extra lower-order coupling terms from the mixed/canonical package.

### C. Use a nonlinear or non-gauge-invariant provenance relation
For example, something that compares \(q\) across different blocks or against other canonical fields in a way not preserved by adding \(P\in\Pi_6\).

That is the first kind of relation not ruled out by this obstruction.

---

## 5. The next actual attack

So my next attack would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for seven independent block-boundary conditions from the mixed/canonical package that fix the }\Pi_6\text{ gauge.}
}
\]

Because if you can fix the polynomial gauge, then a lower-order law for \(q\) becomes thinkable again.

If that fails, the next target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for an order-}\ge 7\text{ provenance equation for }q
\text{ that is not equivalent to }\phi=Aq^{(7)}.
}
\]

So the mathematical push has reached a very precise frontier:

\[
\boxed{
\text{without killing the degree-}\le 6\text{ gauge, there is no meaningful local operator theorem left for }q.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:35:09)

_Thought for 51s_

Here is the strongest continuation I can justify now.

I think the right new theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{gauge-invariant linear provenance laws for }q\text{ factor through }q^{(7)},\text{ hence are trivial at the current package level.}}
\]

That sharpens the previous no-go results a lot.

---

## 1. The quotient view is exact

On a generic one-pair block \(J\), the current direct provenance data only see
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)},
\qquad A\neq 0.
\]
So the current package determines \(q\) only modulo the polynomial gauge
\[
q\sim q+P,\qquad P\in \Pi_6.
\]

In other words, the only canonically visible object is the class
\[
[q]\in \mathcal O(J)/\Pi_6.
\]

But on an interval, the quotient
\[
\mathcal O(J)/\Pi_6
\]
is canonically isomorphic to \(\mathcal O(J)\) via the seventh derivative:
\[
[q]\longmapsto q^{(7)}.
\]

So any canonical linear construction from the present package that depends on \(q\) can only depend on \(q^{(7)}\), equivalently on \(\phi/A\).

That is the first exact statement.

---

## 2. Operator factorization theorem

Let \(\mathcal T\) be any linear operator on analytic \(q\)-germs on \(J\) such that

1. \(\mathcal T\) is defined canonically from the current package only, and
2. \(\mathcal T\) is gauge-invariant:
   \[
   \mathcal T(q+P)=\mathcal T(q)
   \qquad(\forall P\in\Pi_6).
   \]

Then \(\mathcal T\) factors uniquely through the quotient:
\[
\mathcal T(q)=\mathcal S(q^{(7)})
\]
for a unique linear operator \(\mathcal S\) on analytic functions on \(J\). Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal T(q)=\widetilde{\mathcal S}\!\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right).
}
\]

So every gauge-invariant linear provenance law is really just a linear operator law on the arbitrary analytic scalar \(\phi\).

That is the exact reduction.

---

## 3. Consequence: no nontrivial universal linear law exists

But we already proved full-block universality:

> on any generic one-pair block, \(\phi\) can be any analytic function.

So if a linear provenance law were supposed to follow **universally** from the current package, it would have to hold for every analytic \(\phi\). Hence the operator \(\mathcal S\) must vanish identically.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{there is no nontrivial universal gauge-invariant linear provenance law for }q
\textbf{ at the current package level.}
}
\]

This includes:

- local differential operators,
- integral operators,
- Volterra/Fredholm operators,
- and any finite or continuum-strength **linear** law built only from the current route-2/direct-slice package.

So the previous idea “find a continuum-strength linear operator law for \(q\)” is now dead too, unless it imports genuinely external non-universal data.

That is a real mathematical advance, because it closes another whole class of attacks.

---

## 4. What this means for the next attack

The next live theorem must now be one of only two types:

### A. A **nonlinear** provenance law for \(q\)

Since linear gauge-invariant laws factor through the arbitrary scalar \(\phi\), they cannot help. So the next possible route is a nonlinear constraint on \(q\), for example something quadratic, sign-definite, monotone, or comparison-based, that is not equivalent to a linear statement about \(\phi\).

### B. A law using **genuinely external non-universal data**

This means importing structure not contained in the present direct-slice package, for example from the mixed/canonical system, in a way that breaks the universality \( \phi=Aq^{(7)} \) on a block.

So the next attack is no longer:

\[
\text{find a linear ODE/integral equation for }q.
\]

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find either a nonlinear provenance law for }q,
\textbf{ or a genuinely external mixed/canonical constraint that breaks gauge universality.}
}
\]

---

## 5. The most promising concrete target

If I had to pick one concrete next move, I would now go after:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a nonlinear sign/variation law for }q
\textbf{ induced by the mixed package.}
}
\]

For example, something of the flavor

\[
\mathcal N\bigl(q,\dots,q^{(6)}; \text{mixed data}\bigr)\ge 0
\]
on a whole block, where \(\mathcal N\) is nonlinear and not killed by adding \(P\in\Pi_6\).

That is the first kind of theorem not already ruled out by the factorization/no-go above.

So the attack has now become very sharp:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all universal linear provenance laws are spent;}
\quad
\text{the next live angle is nonlinear or genuinely external.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

_Thought for 14s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the right next theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{even nonlinear local provenance laws for }q\text{ are spent.}}
\]

So the next live angle is narrower still:

\[
\boxed{
\text{only a genuinely \emph{nonlocal} nonlinear law for }q,
\text{ or a genuinely external mixed/canonical input, can still close this lane.}
}
\]

That is a real sharpening of the previous no-go results.

---

## 1. Gauge-invariant local nonlinear laws factor through the tail jets

On a generic one-pair block \(J\), the current direct provenance data only see
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)},
\qquad A\neq 0,
\]
so \(q\) is defined only modulo the polynomial gauge
\[
q\sim q+P,
\qquad P\in \Pi_6.
\]

Now take any **local** differential expression of finite order \(r\),
\[
\mathcal N\bigl(h,q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr).
\]
Assume it is canonical at the current package level, so it must be gauge-invariant:
\[
\mathcal N\bigl(h,(q+P)(h),\dots,(q+P)^{(r)}(h)\bigr)
=
\mathcal N\bigl(h,q(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)
\]
for every polynomial \(P\in\Pi_6\).

Then \(\mathcal N\) cannot actually depend on the first seven jet coordinates
\[
q,\ q',\ \dots,\ q^{(6)}.
\]

The proof is simple. At a fixed point \(h_0\), the map
\[
P\in \Pi_6\longmapsto \bigl(P(h_0),P'(h_0),\dots,P^{(6)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
is surjective onto \(\mathbf R^7\) (or \(\mathbf C^7\)). So gauge-invariance under \(q\mapsto q+P\) means \(\mathcal N\) is invariant under arbitrary translations in the first seven jet coordinates. Hence it depends only on
\[
q^{(7)}(h),\ q^{(8)}(h),\ \dots,\ q^{(r)}(h).
\]

So every gauge-invariant local nonlinear law of finite order factors as
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal N\bigl(h,q,\dots,q^{(r)}\bigr)
=
\widetilde{\mathcal N}\bigl(h,q^{(7)},q^{(8)},\dots,q^{(r)}\bigr).
}
\]

Equivalently, since
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\]
it becomes a local nonlinear differential law for the scalar graph \(\phi\).

---

## 2. But the scalar tail jets are still arbitrary

Now use the blockwise universality already proved.

On any generic one-pair block, \(\phi\) can be any analytic function. Therefore at any fixed point \(h_0\), the jet
\[
\bigl(\phi(h_0),\phi'(h_0),\dots,\phi^{(m)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
can be prescribed arbitrarily.

Since
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\]
and \(A(h_0)\neq 0\), the tail jet
\[
\bigl(q^{(7)}(h_0),q^{(8)}(h_0),\dots,q^{(r)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
can also be prescribed arbitrarily.

Therefore, if a local finite-order nonlinear law
\[
\widetilde{\mathcal N}\bigl(h,q^{(7)},\dots,q^{(r)}\bigr)=0
\]
were to hold universally at the current package level, it would have to vanish for **all** choices of these tail jets. So it must be the trivial identity.

This gives the theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{there is no nontrivial universal gauge-invariant local nonlinear differential law of finite order for }q
\textbf{ at the current package level.}
}
\]

That is the nonlinear analogue of the earlier linear factorization/no-go theorem.

---

## 3. Consequence

This kills another whole family of possible attacks.

Not only are the following impossible:

- linear ODEs for \(q\) of order \(\le 6\),
- linear integral laws with sign-definite kernels,
- any universal linear operator law for \(q\);

but now also impossible are:

- local polynomial identities in \(q,\dots,q^{(r)}\),
- local sign conditions on nonlinear expressions in finitely many jets of \(q\),
- local finite-order nonlinear differential inequalities,
- or any other **local finite-order nonlinear provenance law** built only from the current direct-slice package.

Because all of them reduce to statements about an arbitrary analytic scalar \(\phi\).

So the real frontier sharpens to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next theorem must be genuinely \emph{nonlocal} nonlinear, or genuinely external.}
}
\]

---

## 4. What “nonlocal nonlinear” means here

At this point, the only remaining possibilities I can see are of two kinds.

### A. A genuinely nonlocal nonlinear law for \(q\)

For example, something like

\[
\mathcal N\Bigl(h,\ q|_{[h_0,h]},\ \text{mixed data}|_{[h_0,h]}\Bigr)\ge 0,
\]
or an identity involving integrals of nonlinear functions of \(q\) over a whole block.

This is not killed by the jet-gauge argument, because it is not determined by a finite jet at one point.

### B. An external mixed/canonical theorem that constrains \(q\) beyond the direct slice

This means a theorem that uses more than the current direct package
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.
\]
For example, something coupling \(q\) to another canonical field in the mixed package on a whole interval, not just pointwise.

That is the first kind of input not already absorbed by the universality machinery.

---

## 5. The next actual attack

So the next thing I would try is very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for a nonlocal nonlinear monotonicity / positivity quantity in the mixed package whose derivative involves }q^{(7)}.
}
\]

Why this is the best target:

- local finite-order laws are dead;
- linear operator laws are dead;
- the direct-slice graph \(\phi\) is arbitrary;
- but a nonlocal nonlinear energy/monotonicity quantity could still cut down the allowed \(\phi\)-profiles in a way universality does not touch.

A model form would be something like
\[
E(h)=\int_{h_0}^h K(h,s)\,\mathcal F\bigl(q(s),q'(s),\dots\bigr)\,ds
\]
with a sign-definite derivative
\[
E'(h)\ge 0
\quad\text{or}\quad
E'(h)\le 0,
\]
and where route-2 recurrence would force the wrong endpoint values.

That is now, I think, the correct next attack angle.

So the mathematical update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all universal local finite-order laws for }q\text{ are exhausted;}
\quad
\text{the next live lane is nonlocal nonlinear or genuinely external.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

The next real attack is to stop looking for a theorem **inside one generic block**.

I think the strongest further mathematical conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{single-block provenance is completely free.}}
\]

Not just the direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\), but \(q\) itself.

If \(J\) is a generic one-pair block with \(A\neq 0\), then for **any** analytic \(q\) on \(J\),
\[
\phi:=Aq^{(7)}
\]
is analytic, so it is realized by the current direct-slice package. Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no universal theorem constraining }q\text{ on a single block at all.}
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “no finite-jet law” or “no local finite-order law” statements. It says: on one block, \(q\) is simply arbitrary.

So the next live target cannot be:

- a local ODE for \(q\),
- a local inequality for \(q\),
- a nonlocal law involving only \(q\) on one block,
- or any theorem whose hypotheses and conclusions stay inside one generic block.

All of those are dead.

That leaves only one plausible frontier:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{cross-block provenance.}
}
\]

In other words, the first theorem with a real chance now is a **turning-boundary transfer / scattering theorem for \(q\)**.

That is the attack I would make next.

---

## Why cross-block is the only remaining place

On each generic block \(J_\alpha\), \(q_\alpha\) is arbitrary.

So the only place nontrivial structure can enter is in how neighboring block germs
\[
q_\alpha,\qquad q_{\alpha+1}
\]
are related across a boundary, especially a generic turning boundary \(M=0\).

If there is no such relation, the whole direct-slice lane is dead.  
If there is one, it is the first genuinely non-universal ingredient.

So the next theorem should look like:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{turning-boundary scattering theorem: }
\text{the blockwise provenance functions }q_-,q_+
\text{ on the two sides of a generic turning boundary}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{satisfy a nontrivial matching law.}
}
\]

And ideally that law is strong enough to cut down the seven-dimensional polynomial gauge or to impose a sign/variation restriction that cannot hold on every block simultaneously.

---

## The concrete mathematical target

The best concrete form is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{boundary transfer law of order }7
}
\]

Take adjacent generic one-pair blocks \(J_-\) and \(J_+\) separated by a generic turning boundary \(h=h_b\) with
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad W(h_b)\neq 0.
\]

Let
\[
\phi_\pm=A_\pm q_\pm^{(7)}
\]
be the direct provenance slices on the two sides.

The theorem to try to prove is that the **actual RH provenance** cannot choose \(q_-\) and \(q_+\) independently. Instead there is a relation
\[
\mathcal B\bigl(q_-^{[0:6]},q_+^{[0:6]}\bigr)=0
\]
or stronger, an operator law comparing \(q_-\) and \(q_+\) on whole neighborhoods.

If you could get even a few independent relations of this kind at each turning boundary, then after passing through enough blocks the cumulative gauge freedom could collapse, and the direct-slice graph geometry would stop being arbitrary.

So the next push is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{derive the first nontrivial turning-boundary matching law for }q.
}
\]

---

## Why this is the right next angle

Everything else is exhausted:

- lower route 2 is universal;
- arbitrary canonical \(\Delta_7\) is free;
- direct slice \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\) is fully universal;
- finite linear provenance conditions are too weak;
- local finite-order nonlinear provenance laws are too weak;
- single-block nonlocal laws are too weak because \(q\) itself is arbitrary on one block.

So the only remaining place structure can hide is in **how blocks talk to each other**.

That is the mathematical bottleneck now.

---

## The next theorem I would actually try to prove

I would try to prove one of these, in this order:

### 1. Turning-boundary jet transfer
A finite but nontrivial relation between the left and right \(7\)-jets:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal B\bigl(q_-(h_b),q_-'(h_b),\dots,q_-^{(6)}(h_b),
q_+(h_b),q_+'(h_b),\dots,q_+^{(6)}(h_b)\bigr)=0.
}
\]

Even one or two such relations would be real progress.

### 2. Turning-boundary asymptotic normalization
Show that after the natural asymptotic renormalization near \(M=0\), the provenance variable has a canonical finite limit, which would kill some of the polynomial gauge.

### 3. Cross-block operator law
Best case:
\[
\boxed{
q_+ = \mathcal T(q_-)
}
\]
for some nontrivial nonlinear or integro-differential transfer map.

That would finally make the direct-slice lane rigid.

---

So the next attack angle is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{mine the generic turning-boundary package for a transfer law of the provenance variable }q.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:43:56)

_Thought for 36s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the right next theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{finite turning-boundary transfer is still too weak.}}
\]

So even if you succeed in deriving **finitely many jet-transfer laws across finitely many turning boundaries**, that still will not close the direct-slice lane.

That is a real structural advance, because it isolates the only kind of boundary theorem that could still matter: an **infinite-codimension / continuum-strength** transfer law.

---

## The theorem

Let
\[
J_1,\dots,J_m
\]
be finitely many generic one-pair blocks, and on each \(J_i\) let
\[
A_i(h):=\kappa_i(h)\eta_{2,i}(h)
\]
be analytic and nowhere zero, so the direct slice on \(J_i\) is
\[
\phi_i(h)=A_i(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Fix finitely many boundary points
\[
\xi_1,\dots,\xi_N
\]
(for example generic turning boundaries between neighboring blocks), and impose any finite family of linear jet-transfer conditions on the global provenance variable \(q\), of the form
\[
\Lambda_r\bigl(q(\xi_1),q'(\xi_1),\dots,q^{(K_1)}(\xi_1),\dots,
q(\xi_N),\dots,q^{(K_N)}(\xi_N)\bigr)=c_r,
\qquad r=1,\dots,R.
\]

Fix also one distinguished block \(J_\ast\), two distinct interior points
\[
\mu_1\neq \mu_2\in J_\ast,
\]
and an affine line
\[
\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b.
\]

Then there exists a single global polynomial \(q\) such that:

1. all the prescribed linear jet-transfer conditions at the boundary points hold;

2. on the distinguished block \(J_\ast\), the direct-slice graph
   \[
   \phi_\ast(\mu)=A_\ast(\mu)\,q^{(7)}(\mu)
   \]
   is tangent to the line \(y=\ell(\mu)\) at both \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\), i.e.
   \[
   \phi_\ast(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
   \qquad
   \phi_\ast'(\mu_j)=s
   \qquad (j=1,2).
   \]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no theorem using only finitely many pointwise jet-transfer laws across finitely many turning boundaries can exclude direct-slice bitangent/same-slope recurrence.}
}
\]

---

## Proof

The tangency conditions at \(\mu_1,\mu_2\) are linear jet conditions on \(q\).

Indeed, on the chosen block,
\[
\phi_\ast=A_\ast q^{(7)},
\]
so
\[
A_\ast(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\]
and since
\[
\phi_\ast'=(A_\ast q^{(7)})'=A_\ast' q^{(7)}+A_\ast q^{(8)},
\]
the slope condition becomes
\[
A_\ast'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)+A_\ast(\mu_j)\,q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=s.
\]

Because \(A_\ast(\mu_j)\neq 0\), these are legitimate linear conditions on the jets of \(q\) at the two points \(\mu_1,\mu_2\).

Together with the assumed finitely many linear jet-transfer conditions at the boundary points \(\xi_1,\dots,\xi_N\), this is a finite linear system on the coefficients of a polynomial \(q\).

Now let \(q\) be a polynomial of degree \(d\). Its coefficient space has dimension \(d+1\). For \(d\) sufficiently large, this dimension exceeds the rank of the total finite linear system. Hence the system has a solution. Any such polynomial gives a single global analytic provenance function \(q\) satisfying all the turning-boundary jet-transfer conditions and simultaneously realizing the prescribed bitangent geometry on \(J_\ast\). ∎

---

## What this means

This strengthens everything before it.

We already knew:

- one-block direct-slice geometry is universal;
- finite linear provenance conditions on one block are too weak;
- local finite-order nonlinear laws are too weak.

Now we know:

\[
\boxed{
\text{even finitely many cross-block jet-transfer laws are still too weak.}
}
\]

So the next live theorem cannot be of the form:

- “here are \(k\) matching relations at each turning boundary,”
- or “the left and right \(7\)-jets satisfy finitely many linear identities,”
- or any other **finite** transfer law across finitely many boundaries.

All of that still leaves enough freedom to realize forbidden recurrence geometry on one chosen block.

---

## The new frontier

At this point, the only boundary theorem that could still matter has to be one of these:

### 1. An infinite-codimension transfer law
Not finitely many jet conditions, but a relation on an entire neighborhood or entire block, for example
\[
q_+ = \mathcal T(q_-)
\]
with \(\mathcal T\) a genuine operator, not just finite jet matching.

### 2. A nonlinear continuum-strength transfer law
Something like
\[
\mathcal N\bigl(q_-|_{J_-},q_+|_{J_+}\bigr)=0
\]
or an integral inequality coupling the two sides on intervals, not just at the boundary point.

### 3. An external mixed/canonical law that cuts across all blocks at once
A theorem that constrains the global provenance function \(q\) on a union of blocks in a way not reducible to finitely many local matching conditions.

---

## The next actual attack

So my next attack would be very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for a turning-boundary transfer operator for }q,
\textbf{ not a finite jet-transfer law.}
}
\]

If such an operator exists, it is the first boundary theorem not already killed by the polynomial interpolation argument above.

So the sharp strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite boundary matching is spent;}
\quad
\text{only a continuum-strength cross-block transfer law can still revive the direct-slice lane.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.
