I’m pushing on the last boundary-theorem lane now: whether even a genuine cross-block transfer operator could still be too weak unless it cuts the solution space down by infinite codimension.

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the right new theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{finite-codimension cross-block transfer is still too weak.}}
\]

This strictly strengthens the earlier “finite jet-transfer is too weak” result.

It says: even if you replace finitely many boundary jet conditions by a much more abstract theorem saying that the global provenance \(q\) must lie in some fixed **finite-codimension linear subspace** of analytic functions across a finite union of blocks, that still does not exclude direct-slice recurrence on a chosen block.

So the next live theorem cannot just be “some linear transfer operator.” It has to be something that cuts the allowed provenance space down by **infinite codimension**, or else something genuinely nonlinear.

---

## 1. Setup

Let
\[
U=\bigcup_{\alpha=1}^m J_\alpha
\]
be a finite union of generic one-pair blocks. On each block \(J_\alpha\),
\[
\phi_\alpha(h)=A_\alpha(h)\,q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad
A_\alpha(h)\neq 0.
\]

Pick one distinguished block \(J_\ast\subset U\), two distinct interior points
\[
\mu_1\neq \mu_2\in J_\ast,
\]
and an affine line
\[
\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b.
\]

As before, tangency of the direct-slice graph on \(J_\ast\) at those two points is the finite system
\[
A_\ast(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\]
\[
A_\ast'(\mu_j)\,q^{(7)}(\mu_j)+A_\ast(\mu_j)\,q^{(8)}(\mu_j)=s,
\qquad j=1,2.
\]

So a forbidden bitangent/same-slope recurrence still imposes only **four linear jet conditions** on the global provenance function \(q\).

---

## 2. Finite-codimension linear transfer still cannot kill it

Now suppose some future theorem were to give a linear global transfer law of the form

\[
q\in \mathcal V\subset \mathcal O(U),
\]
where \(\mathcal V\) is a linear subspace of finite codimension:
\[
\operatorname{codim}\mathcal V<\infty.
\]

This includes, for example:

- finitely many cross-block integral identities,
- finitely many linear boundary-transfer laws,
- finitely many linear matching constraints on whole blocks,
- or any linear operator theorem whose solution space has finite codimension.

Then the tangency conditions above cut out a further finite-codimension affine subspace inside \(\mathcal O(U)\).

The intersection of two finite-codimension affine subspaces in an infinite-dimensional space is generically still infinite-dimensional, and in particular nonempty unless one has some special incompatibility. Concretely, polynomials of sufficiently high degree already give an infinite-dimensional test class, and finite-codimension linear restrictions cannot annihilate all of them.

So one gets:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there exists }q\in\mathcal V
\text{ such that the direct slice on }J_\ast\text{ has the prescribed bitangent.}
}
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{no linear theorem whose allowed provenance space has finite codimension can exclude direct-slice recurrence.}
}
\]

That is the new no-go statement.

---

## 3. Why this matters

This is stronger than everything before it.

We already knew:

- one-block freedom is too large;
- finite jet conditions are too weak;
- any local finite-order law is too weak;
- finitely many turning-boundary jet-transfer laws are too weak.

Now we can say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{even an arbitrary linear cross-block transfer theorem is useless unless it cuts the provenance space down by infinite codimension.}
}
\]

So the next theorem cannot merely be “a linear transfer operator exists.”  
That is still too weak if its solution space remains finite-codimensional.

---

## 4. The only remaining live classes

At this point, there are really only two mathematically live possibilities left.

### A. An infinite-codimension linear law
For example, something like a true ODE / Volterra / Fredholm equation whose solution space is a proper infinite-codimension subset of \(\mathcal O(U)\), not just finitely many linear constraints.

This would be a real continuum-strength transfer law.

### B. A genuinely nonlinear law
For example, a sign-definite monotonicity principle, an energy inequality, or a nonlinear transfer map across turning boundaries.

This escapes the finite-codimension interpolation no-go.

So the frontier is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next useful theorem must be either infinite-codimension linear, or genuinely nonlinear.}
}
\]

---

## 5. The best next attack

If I were to push next, I would now focus only on this question:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{does the mixed/canonical package give a nonlinear monotonicity or positivity law for the global provenance }q?
}
\]

Not a finite matching law.  
Not a finite set of operator identities.  
Not abstract boundary transfer.

A theorem of the form
\[
\mathcal E[q](h)\ge 0
\quad\text{or}\quad
\frac{d}{dh}\mathcal E[q](h)\ge 0
\]
on a whole block or across a turning boundary would now be the first kind of statement not already killed by interpolation/universality.

So the strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all finite-codimension linear transfer laws are spent;}
\quad
\text{the next live lane is infinite-codimension linear or genuinely nonlinear.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

I think the right new theorem is:

This extends the earlier no-go from linear jet conditions to **arbitrary finite systems of analytic pointwise equations/inequalities** in the jets of \(q\).

So the next live theorem cannot be:

- finitely many endpoint matching equations,
- finitely many nonlinear boundary relations,
- finitely many local sign constraints at selected points,
- or any other finite collection of pointwise provenance conditions.

All of that is still too weak to kill direct-slice recurrence.

---

## 1. General finite pointwise provenance constraints

Fix a finite set of points
\[
\xi_1,\dots,\xi_M\in U
\]
on a finite union of generic one-pair blocks \(U\), and at each point let
\[
J_i(q):=\bigl(q(\xi_i),q'(\xi_i),\dots,q^{(r_i)}(\xi_i)\bigr)
\]
be the finite jet.

Now suppose some future provenance theorem imposes finitely many analytic conditions
\[
F_\ell\bigl(J_1(q),\dots,J_M(q)\bigr)=0,
\qquad \ell=1,\dots,N,
\]
or even mixed equalities/strict inequalities
\[
F_\ell\bigl(J_1(q),\dots,J_M(q)\bigr)\ \#_\ell\ 0,
\qquad \#_\ell\in\{=,>,<\}.
\]

Then these conditions constrain only a finite-dimensional jet vector.

But by Hermite-Borel interpolation, the map
\[
q \longmapsto \bigl(J_1(q),\dots,J_M(q)\bigr)
\]
from analytic functions to that finite-dimensional jet space is surjective. So any compatible finite jet pattern can be realized by a polynomial \(q\).

That is the core fact.

So if you also impose a direct-slice recurrence event on a chosen block — for example a bitangent line at two interior points \(\mu_1\neq \mu_2\) —
\[
\phi(\mu_j)=\ell(\mu_j),
\qquad
\phi'(\mu_j)=s,
\qquad
\phi=Aq^{(7)},
\]
these are just finitely many more pointwise jet conditions on \(q\), namely on
\[
q^{(7)}(\mu_j),\ q^{(8)}(\mu_j).
\]

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any compatible finite system of pointwise provenance conditions can be realized simultaneously with a prescribed direct-slice bitangent / same-slope recurrence.}
}
\]

So the no-go theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{no theorem built from finitely many pointwise equations or inequalities on jets of }q
\textbf{ can exclude direct-slice recurrence.}
}
\]

This is strictly stronger than the earlier “finite linear conditions are too weak” result.

---

## 2. Why this matters

This kills another whole class of possible next steps.

Not only are the following too weak:

- finite linear matching conditions,
- finite jet-transfer laws,
- finite-codimension linear constraints,

but also too weak are:

- finitely many nonlinear relations between left/right boundary jets,
- finitely many pointwise positivity constraints,
- finitely many algebraic identities involving \(q,\dots,q^{(r)}\),
- finitely many local barrier conditions at selected points.

All of these reduce to finite-dimensional conditions in jet space, and jet space is still completely flexible.

So the next live theorem cannot be “some clever finite set of nonlinear matching identities across boundaries.” That lane is now spent too.

---

## 3. The new frontier

At this point, the only remaining kinds of provenance theorem that could still matter are genuinely **continuum-strength** ones.

There are really only three mathematically live possibilities left:

### A. A pointwise law on an entire interval
For example,
\[
F\bigl(h,q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad\text{for all }h\in J,
\]
or a sign law
\[
F\bigl(h,q(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)\ge 0
\qquad\text{for all }h\in J.
\]

That is no longer finite-dimensional.

### B. A nonlocal law on an entire interval
For example, a Volterra/Fredholm/integral identity or inequality involving \(q\) over a whole block.

### C. A genuinely external mixed/canonical relation
Something coupling \(q\) to other canonical fields over a continuum of points, not just by finitely many boundary values.

So the sharp new frontier is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next useful provenance theorem must constrain }q\text{ on a whole interval, not at finitely many points.}
}
\]

---

## 4. The best next attack

So the next thing I would now attack is this:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for a pointwise-in-}h\textbf{ mixed/canonical law for }q
\textbf{ on a generic one-pair block.}
}
\]

Concretely, something of the form
\[
F\bigl(h,q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h); \text{other canonical data at }h\bigr)=0
\]
for every \(h\) on the block.

Why this is the right next target:

- finite pointwise conditions are dead;
- local finite-order laws were dead only if they used the current package alone;
- but a pointwise law involving **external canonical fields** could still break the universality, because those other fields are not arbitrary once the actual RH provenance is fixed.

And the very best subcase would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a pointwise law of order }r\le 6
}
\]
because then \(q^{(7)}\) would stop being arbitrary and the direct slice
\[
\phi=Aq^{(7)}
\]
would immediately become rigid.

---

So the strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all finite collections of pointwise provenance conditions are spent;}
\quad
\text{the next live lane is a continuum-strength pointwise or nonlocal law on a whole block.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:48:45)

_Thought for 51s_

Here is the strongest clean continuation I can justify now.

I think the right new theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{no universal pointwise finite-order provenance law exists on a generic block.}}
\]

This is stronger than the earlier “finite sets of pointwise conditions are too weak.” It says that even a **pointwise-in-\(h\)** differential law on a whole block, if it is built only from the current direct-slice package and is gauge-invariant, must be trivial.

So the next live attack really does have to use **external non-universal fields** from the mixed/canonical package, or a genuinely nonlocal law.

---

## 1. Statement

Let \(J\) be a generic one-pair block on which
\[
A(h)=\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Let
\[
B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h)
\]
be any analytic scalar coefficient fields canonically determined by the **current direct-slice package** on \(J\). Think of these as whatever lower/direct coefficient functions you like, as long as they are already contained in the present package and do not introduce extra non-universal provenance.

Now suppose there is a pointwise finite-order analytic law
\[
F\bigl(h,B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h),q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in J),
\]
and suppose it is gauge-invariant under
\[
q\mapsto q+P,
\qquad P\in\Pi_6.
\]

Then that law is trivial: it imposes no nontrivial restriction on the current package.

---

## 2. Why it is true

At a fixed \(h_0\in J\), the map
\[
P\in\Pi_6\longmapsto \bigl(P(h_0),P'(h_0),\dots,P^{(6)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
is surjective onto the first seven jet coordinates. So gauge-invariance forces \(F\) to be independent of
\[
q(h_0),q'(h_0),\dots,q^{(6)}(h_0).
\]

Hence any such law must factor pointwise as
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),q,\dots,q^{(r)}\bigr)
=
\widetilde F\bigl(h,B(h),q^{(7)},q^{(8)},\dots,q^{(r)}\bigr).
\]

But on the current package,
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\]
and \(\phi\) is fully universal on \(J\): for any analytic scalar germ \(\psi\), there is an admissible \(q\) with
\[
\phi=\psi.
\]
Therefore the tail jet
\[
\bigl(q^{(7)}(h_0),q^{(8)}(h_0),\dots,q^{(r)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
can be prescribed arbitrarily at any \(h_0\in J\).

So the pointwise identity
\[
\widetilde F\bigl(h_0,B(h_0),u_7,\dots,u_r\bigr)=0
\]
must hold for **all** values of \((u_7,\dots,u_r)\). Hence \(\widetilde F\) vanishes identically along the actual coefficient field \(B(h)\), and the law is empty.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every universal gauge-invariant pointwise finite-order law on one block is vacuous.}
}
\]

---

## 3. What this kills

This kills another whole class of attacks.

Not only are these too weak:

- finitely many endpoint jet conditions,
- finitely many boundary transfer laws,
- finite-codimension linear constraints,
- local finite-order nonlinear laws at selected points,

but now also too weak are:

- any **pointwise-in-\(h\)** finite-order ODE-type law for \(q\),
- any pointwise differential inequality of finite order,
- any analytic relation
  \[
  F(h,\text{current-package coefficients},q,\dots,q^{(r)})=0
  \]
  on a whole block,
- provided it uses only the current direct-slice package and respects the \(\Pi_6\) gauge.

So the direct-slice lane is now exhausted not just locally and not just finitely — it is exhausted for **all universal pointwise finite-order laws on a block**.

---

## 4. The new frontier

This leaves only two mathematically live possibilities.

### A. A genuinely nonlocal law
Something that is not determined pointwise by finite jets:
\[
\mathcal N\bigl(h, q|_{[h_0,h]}, \text{external data}|_{[h_0,h]}\bigr)=0
\]
or an integral inequality / monotonicity formula.

### B. A pointwise law using **external non-universal fields**
That is, a law involving canonical mixed/provenance fields not contained in the current direct-slice package. Such fields could break the universality because they are not arbitrary once the actual RH provenance is fixed.

This is the sharpest formulation yet:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next useful theorem must be either nonlocal, or pointwise but genuinely external.}
}
\]

---

## 5. The next attack I would make

I would now target exactly this:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a pointwise mixed/canonical law of order }\le 6
\textbf{ for }q\textbf{ involving external fields not in the current direct slice.}
}
\]

Why order \(\le 6\): if such a law exists, it immediately kills the arbitrariness of
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\]
so the direct slice stops being universal.

If no such pointwise external law exists, then the next target is a genuinely nonlocal nonlinear law from the mixed package.

So the attack has now reached a very precise endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all universal pointwise finite-order laws on a generic block are spent;}
\quad
\text{the next live lane is external-pointwise or nonlocal.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:51:15)

_Thought for 27s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the right next theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{order-}\le 6\textbf{ external pointwise laws cannot constrain }q.}
\]

That is stronger than the previous “all universal pointwise finite-order laws are spent” result, because it still holds even if you allow **arbitrary external mixed/canonical fields** to enter pointwise.

So the next live pointwise theorem, if one exists, must have order at least \(7\), and must constrain \(q^{(7)}\) itself.

---

## 1. The theorem

Let \(J\) be a generic one-pair block, and let
\[
B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h)
\]
be any analytic scalar fields on \(J\), thought of as arbitrary external mixed/canonical data.

Consider a pointwise finite-order law
\[
F\bigl(h,B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h),q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in J),
\]
and assume it is gauge-invariant under
\[
q\mapsto q+P,
\qquad P\in\Pi_6.
\]

If
\[
r\le 6,
\]
then the law is independent of \(q\) altogether. Equivalently, it reduces to a relation only among the external coefficient fields:
\[
F\bigl(h,B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h)\bigr)=0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no gauge-invariant pointwise law of order }\le 6
\text{ can ever be the missing provenance constraint on }q.
}
\]

---

## 2. Proof

Fix \(h_0\in J\). The map
\[
P\in\Pi_6\longmapsto \bigl(P(h_0),P'(h_0),\dots,P^{(6)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
is surjective onto \(\mathbf C^7\) (or \(\mathbf R^7\), depending on the setting). So for any prescribed increment
\[
(\delta_0,\dots,\delta_r)\in \mathbf C^{r+1},
\qquad r\le 6,
\]
there exists \(P\in\Pi_6\) such that
\[
P^{(k)}(h_0)=\delta_k
\qquad (0\le k\le r).
\]

Gauge invariance gives
\[
F\bigl(h_0,B(h_0),q(h_0)+\delta_0,\dots,q^{(r)}(h_0)+\delta_r\bigr)
=
F\bigl(h_0,B(h_0),q(h_0),\dots,q^{(r)}(h_0)\bigr)
\]
for all \((\delta_0,\dots,\delta_r)\).

Therefore \(F\) is invariant under arbitrary translations in the \(q\)-jet variables \(q,\dots,q^{(r)}\), hence cannot depend on them at all. So the law reduces to a relation among the external fields only. ∎

---

## 3. Consequence

This is a real narrowing of the search.

It says the first pointwise external law that could possibly matter must satisfy

\[
\boxed{
\text{order }r\ge 7.
}
\]

And that makes sense: order \(7\) is exactly where the polynomial gauge stops acting freely.

So the first viable pointwise external provenance theorem must involve at least one of
\[
q^{(7)},\ q^{(8)},\ \dots
\]
and therefore, through
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)},
\]
must constrain the direct slice itself.

---

## 4. The next refinement

You can push this one step further.

Suppose a pointwise external law has order exactly \(7\):
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),q(h),\dots,q^{(7)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]
Gauge invariance again removes dependence on
\[
q,\dots,q^{(6)},
\]
so the law must reduce to
\[
\widetilde F\bigl(h,B(h),q^{(7)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]

Using
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\]
this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\widetilde F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)}\right)=0.
}
\]

So the first live pointwise theorem is not arbitrary. It must be a **pointwise relation that solves for \(q^{(7)}\)**, equivalently for \(\phi\), in terms of genuinely external fields.

In particular, if such a law is nondegenerate in the \(q^{(7)}\)-variable, it would force
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=H\bigl(h,B(h)\bigr),
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,H\bigl(h,B(h)\bigr).
\]

That would instantly kill the universality of the direct slice.

So the next concrete theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{derive an order-7 pointwise external law that determines }q^{(7)}
\text{ from mixed/canonical data.}
}
\]

That is the first pointwise theorem that is not already ruled out.

---

## 5. Why this is the right next attack

This gives a very clean triage:

- order \(\le 6\) external pointwise laws are impossible as constraints on \(q\);
- order \(7\) pointwise external laws are the **first viable pointwise level**;
- if no such law exists, then only genuinely nonlocal laws remain.

So my next attack would be:

### Try to extract from the mixed/canonical package a pointwise order-7 relation of the form
\[
q^{(7)} = H\bigl(h,\text{external mixed/canonical fields}\bigr)
\]
or at least
\[
F\bigl(h,\text{external fields},q^{(7)}\bigr)=0
\]
with nontrivial dependence on \(q^{(7)}\).

If that exists, then the direct slice
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}
\]
stops being arbitrary immediately, and the graph-geometry lane comes back to life.

If it does not, then the next live angle is indeed a genuinely nonlocal law.

So the mathematical update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first viable pointwise external theorem must live at order }7\text{ or higher.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 05:55:45)

_Thought for 23s_

Here is the strongest clean continuation I can justify.

I think the right next theorem is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{order-}7\textbf{ is the first viable pointwise level, and every higher pointwise law reduces to a local differential law for }\phi.}
\]

That is useful because it turns the search for a provenance law for \(q\) into a much more concrete search for a law for the direct-slice scalar
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.
\]

---

## 1. General reduction of pointwise external laws

Work on a generic one-pair block \(J\), and let
\[
A(h)\neq 0,\qquad \phi(h)=A(h)\,q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Let
\[
B_1(h),\dots,B_m(h)
\]
be arbitrary external mixed/canonical coefficient fields.

Consider a gauge-invariant pointwise law of order \(r\):
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),q(h),q'(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in J).
\]

From the previous step, gauge invariance under
\[
q\mapsto q+P,\qquad P\in\Pi_6
\]
kills dependence on
\[
q,\ q',\ \dots,\ q^{(6)}.
\]
So the law must factor as
\[
\widetilde F\bigl(h,B(h),q^{(7)}(h),q^{(8)}(h),\dots,q^{(r)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]

Now substitute
\[
q^{(7)}=\frac{\phi}{A},
\qquad
q^{(8)}=\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)',
\qquad
\dots,
\qquad
q^{(r)}=\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)^{(r-7)}.
\]

So every gauge-invariant pointwise external law of order \(r\) becomes exactly a local differential law of order \(r-7\) for \(\phi\):
\[
\boxed{
\widehat F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi}{A},\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)',\dots,
\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)^{(r-7)}\right)=0.
}
\]

That is the clean reduction theorem.

---

## 2. Consequences by order

This immediately gives the hierarchy.

### Order \(r\le 6\)

Impossible as a constraint on \(q\). We already proved these laws are vacuous.

### Order \(r=7\)

A pointwise law of order \(7\) reduces to an **algebraic** relation for \(\phi\):
\[
\boxed{
\widehat F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)}\right)=0.
}
\]

So the first viable pointwise law does not give an ODE for \(q\). It simply prescribes \(\phi\) pointwise from external fields.

If this law is nondegenerate in the last variable, then by the implicit function theorem it gives
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=H(h,B(h)),
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)H(h,B(h)).
\]

### Order \(r=8\)

A pointwise law of order \(8\) reduces to a **first-order ODE** for \(\phi\):
\[
\widehat F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi}{A},\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)'\right)=0.
\]

### General order \(r\ge 7\)

It reduces to a local differential law of order \(r-7\) for \(\phi\).

So the whole provenance problem has now been translated into this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first viable pointwise external law for }q
\text{ is exactly the first viable local law for }\phi.
}
\]

That is the new structural reduction.

---

## 3. Why this matters

This is stronger than just saying “order \(7\) is the first viable level.”

It says:

- any pointwise theorem for \(q\) of order \(7\) is really just an algebraic constraint on the scalar graph \(\phi\);
- any order-\(8\) theorem is really just a first-order ODE for \(\phi\);
- more generally, any pointwise theorem of order \(r\) is really a local differential law of order \(r-7\) for \(\phi\).

So the search is no longer vague at all.

If the mixed/canonical package gives a pointwise law, then one of two things happens:

- it prescribes \(\phi\) directly,
- or it gives a low-order ODE for \(\phi\).

Either way, the direct-slice graph lane becomes live again.

---

## 4. The next concrete attack

So the next move I would make is very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for the first external pointwise relation of order }7\textbf{ or }8.
}
\]

Those are the sweet spots.

### Best case: order \(7\)

Derive
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h)),
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
\phi=A\,H(h,B(h)).
\]

Then the direct-slice graph is no longer arbitrary at all, and you can attack its bitangent / slope-window geometry directly from the external fields.

### Next best: order \(8\)

Derive a first-order ODE for \(\phi\), for example
\[
\phi'=\mathcal G(h,B(h),\phi).
\]

A first-order ODE is already dramatically more rigid than arbitrary analytic \(\phi\), and that could realistically kill the direct-slice recurrence geometries.

So my next attack would be:

1. inspect the mixed/canonical package for any pointwise identity involving \(q^{(7)}\) or \(q^{(8)}\);
2. rewrite it immediately as either
   \[
   \phi=A\,H(h,B)
   \quad\text{or}\quad
   \phi'=\mathcal G(h,B,\phi);
   \]
3. then restart the direct-slice no-reentry analysis with that reduced scalar law.

---

## 5. Strategic update

So the attack has now reached a very sharp formulation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next live pointwise theorem is not “some law for }q\text{”; it is either}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{an algebraic law for }\phi\text{ (order 7), or a first-order ODE for }\phi\text{ (order 8).}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:03:15)

I’m trying to squeeze one more rigorous reduction out of the “order \(7/8\) pointwise law” idea, namely: what such a law would actually have to do to the direct-slice graph, and whether that already narrows the search meaningfully.

_Thought for 24s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the next clean reduction is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{order }7\textbf{ or }8\textbf{ pointwise laws are the only viable pointwise ones, but they still split into two very different classes.}}
\]

And only one of those classes looks genuinely promising.

---

## 1. The exact reduction, sharpened

On a generic one-pair block \(J\),
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)},\qquad A\neq 0.
\]

We already reduced any gauge-invariant pointwise law of order \(r\) for \(q\) to a local differential law of order \(r-7\) for \(\phi\).

That means:

- order \(7\) gives an **algebraic pointwise law**
  \[
  \widehat F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)}\right)=0;
  \]
- order \(8\) gives a **first-order ODE**
  \[
  \widehat F\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi(h)}{A(h)},\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)'(h)\right)=0.
  \]

Now here is the new split.

### Class I: tautological laws

If the mixed/canonical external fields \(B(h)\) already contain enough information to encode \(\phi\) or \(\phi'\) directly, then an order \(7\) or \(8\) law is mathematically possible but empty. For example, a law of the schematic form
\[
q^{(7)}=B_1(h)
\quad\text{or}\quad
q^{(8)}=B_2(h)
\]
only helps if \(B_1,B_2\) are themselves constrained independently of \(\phi\).

So a viable order \(7/8\) theorem has to be **non-tautological**: it must determine \(q^{(7)}\) or \(q^{(8)}\) from external fields whose own behavior is already controlled elsewhere in the proof.

That is the first useful filter.

### Class II: genuinely constraining laws

If an order \(7\) law gives
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h)),
\]
then
\[
\phi=A\,H(h,B(h))
\]
is completely prescribed by external fields. That would immediately revive the direct-slice lane.

If an order \(8\) law gives a nondegenerate first-order ODE
\[
\left(\frac{\phi}{A}\right)'=G\!\left(h,B(h),\frac{\phi}{A}\right),
\]
equivalently
\[
\phi'=\widetilde G(h,B(h),\phi),
\]
then \(\phi\) is no longer arbitrary either; it lies in a one-parameter family. That is weaker than order \(7\), but still strong enough that the direct-slice graph geometry might become rigid.

So the next attack is not “find any order \(7\) or \(8\) law.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a non-tautological order }7\textbf{ or }8\textbf{ law whose right-hand side is already controlled by previously proved mixed/canonical structure.}
}
\]

---

## 2. Why order \(7\) is the real prize

An order \(8\) first-order ODE for \(\phi\) still leaves one free constant of integration. That may or may not be enough to allow recurrence geometry; in general it often is.

By contrast, a nondegenerate order \(7\) algebraic law
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h))
\]
kills the freedom completely:
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)H(h,B(h)).
\]

So if there is a pointwise external theorem to be found, the natural thing to hunt is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{an order }7\textbf{ algebraic determination of }q^{(7)}.
}
\]

That is the cleanest possible resurrection of the direct-slice lane.

---

## 3. A useful necessary condition for any viable order \(7\) law

Suppose such a law exists:
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),q^{(7)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]
For it to be useful, it must be solvable nontrivially for \(q^{(7)}\). So necessarily,
\[
\partial_{q^{(7)}}F\not\equiv 0
\]
on the relevant block.

Then by the implicit function theorem, at least locally on the generic block it reduces to
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h)).
\]

So every viable order \(7\) theorem is locally equivalent to an explicit determination of \(q^{(7)}\) from external fields.

That gives a very concrete diagnostic:

> when inspecting the mixed/canonical package, look specifically for identities where \(q^{(7)}\) enters pointwise and nondegenerately.

Anything else at order \(7\) is probably too weak.

---

## 4. The one more reduction I can make

A pointwise order \(7\) law for \(q^{(7)}\) is equivalent to a pointwise law for the direct-slice curvature datum
\[
W^{[1]}.
\]

Indeed,
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]},
\qquad
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.
\]
So if
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h)),
\]
then \(\phi\) is known, hence so is \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\), hence so is \(W^{[1]}\).

Conversely, if one could determine \(W^{[1]}\) pointwise from external fields, then \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\) is known and \(\phi\) is constrained by a second-order ODE, which is also quite strong.

So the next viable pointwise theorem could come in either of two equivalent-looking forms:

\[
\boxed{
q^{(7)}=H(h,B(h))
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
W^{[1]}=K(h,B(h)).
}
\]

The first is cleaner in provenance language; the second may be easier to find inside the quotient-septic / mixed geometry.

That is, I think, the best new tactical insight.

---

## 5. What I would attack next, concretely

I would now stop speaking abstractly about “a law for \(q\)” and instead inspect the mixed/canonical package for either of these two exact targets:

### Target A
A pointwise mixed/canonical identity whose highest-new part contains \(q^{(7)}\) with nonzero coefficient and no \(q^{(8)}\). Then solve it for \(q^{(7)}\).

### Target B
A pointwise mixed/canonical identity whose highest-new part contains \(W^{[1]}\) with nonzero coefficient and no higher direct-slice freedom. Then solve it for \(W^{[1]}\).

If neither exists, then pointwise external laws are probably dead too, and the next live lane really is a genuinely nonlocal nonlinear law.

---

So the strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next live pointwise target is no longer “some law for }q\text{”; it is a non-tautological algebraic determination of }q^{(7)}\text{ or }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:05:57)

_Thought for 1m 7s_

I can make one more clean reduction.

The right next target is no longer “determine \(q^{(7)}\).” It is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{determine }W^{[1]}\textbf{ pointwise.}}
\]

That is the **minimal** pointwise target that could still matter, because the whole direct-slice recurrence problem is **affine-invariant** in \(\phi\).

---

## 1. Affine invariance of the direct-slice geometry

Recall on a regular direct-slice block we use
\[
\phi=\lambda^{[1]},\qquad
\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu,\qquad
m^{[1]}=\phi-\mu\phi_\mu.
\]

Now replace \(\phi\) by
\[
\widetilde\phi(\mu)=\phi(\mu)+a\mu+b.
\]

Then
\[
\widetilde\beta^{[1]}=\widetilde\phi_\mu=\phi_\mu+a=\beta^{[1]}+a,
\]
and
\[
\widetilde m^{[1]}
=
\widetilde\phi-\mu\widetilde\phi_\mu
=
(\phi+a\mu+b)-\mu(\phi_\mu+a)
=
(\phi-\mu\phi_\mu)+b
=
m^{[1]}+b.
\]

So:

- every slope window is translated by the same constant \(a\),
- every dual intercept branch is translated by the same constant \(b\),
- and therefore every overlap condition and every intercept-difference condition is unchanged.

In particular, for two branches,
\[
\Delta_{\alpha\beta}(s)=m_\beta(s)-m_\alpha(s)
\]
is unchanged by \(\phi\mapsto \phi+a\mu+b\).

Likewise a bitangent line to \(y=\phi(\mu)\) stays a bitangent line after adding an affine function, just with the tangent line shifted.

So the entire direct-slice recurrence geometry depends only on the affine class
\[
[\phi]\in \mathcal O(J)/\Pi_1.
\]

That is the first key fact.

---

## 2. \(W^{[1]}\) is exactly the affine-invariant curvature datum

The draft already gives
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}.
\]

Since \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\) is unchanged by adding \(a\mu+b\), it is precisely the affine-invariant content of \(\phi\).

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
W^{[1]}\ \text{determines the affine class }[\phi],
\text{ and }[\phi]\text{ determines }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

Indeed, once \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\) is known on a connected regular block, \(\phi\) is determined up to addition of an affine function \(a\mu+b\). And that affine freedom is exactly invisible to the recurrence geometry.

So among all possible pointwise external laws, a law for \(W^{[1]}\) is the **canonically minimal** useful one.

That is better than targeting \(q^{(7)}\) itself, which is stronger than necessary and polluted by affine gauge.

---

## 3. What this means for the order hierarchy

You already have
\[
\phi=A\,q^{(7)}.
\]

An order-7 pointwise law for \(q\) would determine \(q^{(7)}\), hence determine \(\phi\) itself.

But for the recurrence problem that is overkill. Since recurrence only sees \([\phi]\), the real pointwise goal is just to determine
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu},
\qquad\text{equivalently }W^{[1]}.
\]

So the pointwise search splits like this:

- **too weak:** any order \(\le 6\) law;
- **possibly useful but over-strong:** order-7 law determining \(q^{(7)}\);
- **canonically minimal useful target:** a pointwise law determining \(W^{[1]}\), i.e. determining \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\).

That is, I think, the best structural reduction so far.

---

## 4. Why this is the right next attack

The previous no-go results killed:

- finite-jet conditions on \(q\),
- finite linear provenance conditions,
- local finite-order universal laws for \(q\),
- even pointwise order-\(\le 6\) external laws.

What survives pointwise is exactly the affine-invariant curvature datum.

So the next theorem to hunt is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{pointwise external determination of }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

For example, anything like
\[
W^{[1]}=K(h,B(h))
\]
with \(B(h)\) external mixed/canonical fields already controlled elsewhere, would be enough in principle to revive the direct-slice lane. Because then the affine class of \(\phi\) would be fixed, and all slope-window / bitangent geometry would become a theorem about a specific prescribed affine class rather than an arbitrary analytic graph.

---

## 5. The caution

This does **not** mean that every pointwise law for \(W^{[1]}\) would close the proof.

A prescribed curvature function can still produce a graph with bitangents. For instance,
\[
\phi(\mu)=(\mu^2-1)^2
\]
has
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=12\mu^2-4
\]
and the line \(y=0\) is tangent at \(\mu=\pm1\).

So merely knowing \(W^{[1]}\) pointwise is not automatically enough to kill recurrence.

But it is the **minimal pointwise datum** that could matter. Anything weaker is definitely irrelevant to the direct-slice geometry, and anything stronger is unnecessary unless it comes with real extra structure.

---

## 6. The next concrete attack

So my next attack would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{inspect the mixed/canonical package for a pointwise identity whose highest-new content is }W^{[1]},
}
\]
or equivalently \(\phi_{\mu\mu}\), with no dependence on the affine gauge.

That is the right target because:

- it is not already ruled out by the \(\Pi_6\)-gauge arguments,
- it matches exactly the affine-invariant content of the direct-slice line geometry,
- and it is weaker and cleaner than trying to determine \(q^{(7)}\) itself.

So the sharp update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next live pointwise target is }W^{[1]}\text{, not }q^{(7)}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:08:54)

_Thought for 1m 38s_

Here is the strongest further push I can justify.

I think the right new reduction is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{bitangent / same-slope recurrence is exactly a two-moment vanishing problem for }W^{[1]}.}
\]

That is better than saying “the next live pointwise target is \(W^{[1]}\),” because it tells you exactly what theorem about \(W^{[1]}\) would be enough.

---

## 1. Reduce the direct-slice geometry to the curvature field

On a regular direct-slice block, set
\[
K(\mu):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(\mu)
=
\frac{v_5(\mu)^3}{M(\mu)^3}\,W^{[1]}(\mu).
\]

Then \(\phi\) is determined up to an affine function by \(K\), and the direct-slice recurrence geometry is affine-invariant, so it depends only on \(K\).

Fix two points
\[
\mu_1<\mu_2.
\]
A line tangent to \(y=\phi(\mu)\) at both \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\phi'(\mu_1)=\phi'(\mu_2)
\]
and
\[
\phi(\mu_2)-\phi(\mu_1)=\phi'(\mu_1)(\mu_2-\mu_1).
\]

Now integrate \(\phi''=K\). The slope condition is exactly
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} K(u)\,du=0.
}
\]

And the intercept condition becomes
\[
\phi(\mu_2)-\phi(\mu_1)-\phi'(\mu_1)(\mu_2-\mu_1)
=
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du,
\]
so the second condition is
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du=0.
}
\]

So a bitangent between \(\mu_1,\mu_2\) is equivalent to the two moment conditions
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du=0.
}
\]

After normalizing
\[
u=\mu_1+t(\mu_2-\mu_1),\qquad 0\le t\le 1,
\]
this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\int_0^1 K_{12}(t)\,dt=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 (1-t)\,K_{12}(t)\,dt=0,
}
\]
where
\[
K_{12}(t):=K(\mu_1+t(\mu_2-\mu_1)).
\]

That is the key exact reduction.

---

## 2. Immediate consequence: one sign change is impossible

This is now the same weighted-moment structure we saw earlier in route 2.

If \(K_{12}\) changes sign exactly once on \((0,1)\), then the two moment equations above cannot both hold.

Reason: once
\[
\int_0^1 K_{12}(t)\,dt=0
\]
balances the positive and negative masses, the second weight
\[
1-t
\]
is strictly larger on the left and strictly smaller on the right. So the second weighted integral keeps the sign of the initial lobe and cannot vanish.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a common tangent line forces at least two sign changes of }K
\textbf{ on the interval }[\mu_1,\mu_2].
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a common tangent line forces at least two sign changes of }W^{[1]}
\textbf{ on the interval }[\mu_1,\mu_2].
}
\]

That recovers the earlier “two separated zeros” obstruction, but now in the exact moment form.

---

## 3. What this means for the next theorem

This now tells you precisely what kind of pointwise external law for \(W^{[1]}\) would be enough.

You do **not** need to determine \(\phi\) completely.  
You do **not** need to determine \(q^{(7)}\) completely.

It is enough to prove a continuum-strength theorem implying:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on every candidate same-parity overlap interval, }W^{[1]}\text{ has at most one sign change.}
}
\]

Because then the two-moment system above is impossible, hence no bitangent / same-slope recurrence can occur.

That is the cleanest sharpening so far.

So the next live pointwise target is not just “know \(W^{[1]}\).” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a one-sign-change theorem for }W^{[1]}\textbf{ on candidate recurrence intervals.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Why this is better than the earlier targets

Earlier targets like “determine \(q^{(7)}\)” or “determine \(W^{[1]}\) pointwise” were stronger than necessary.

Now the minimal useful target is clearer:

- recurrence is equivalent to a two-moment vanishing condition on \(K\);
- a single sign change already makes that impossible;
- so a theorem that bounds the sign-change count of \(W^{[1]}\) on the relevant intervals would suffice.

That is a much more realistic theorem target than complete determination of \(\phi\).

---

## 5. The next concrete attack

So my next attack would be very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{mine the external mixed/canonical package for a pointwise law or monotonicity principle forcing }W^{[1]}
\textbf{ to have at most one sign change on each candidate overlap interval.}
}
\]

A theorem of any of these forms would be enough:

- \(W^{[1]}\) has one sign on each relevant interval;
- \(W^{[1]}\) is monotone on each relevant interval;
- \(W^{[1]}\) satisfies a first-order ODE forcing at most one zero;
- \(W^{[1]}\) is a product of controlled-sign factors with only one sign-changing factor.

So the strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct-slice endgame is now an exact two-moment problem for }W^{[1]};
\quad
\text{the next live theorem is a sign-change bound for }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

_Thought for 39s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the right new result is a **no-go refinement**:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{a one-sign-change theorem for }W^{[1]}\textbf{ would be enough, but it is also the most you can hope to get from abstract moment geometry alone.}}
\]

More concretely:

- a common tangent / same-slope recurrence forces the two moment conditions
  \[
  \int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} K(u)\,du=0,
  \qquad
  \int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} (\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du=0,
  \]
  where
  \[
  K=\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]};
  \]
- from those, you can prove recurrence is impossible if \(K\) has only one sign change on \((\mu_1,\mu_2)\);
- but exactly **two** sign changes are already enough to realize both moments.

So the abstract “sign-count” lane stops there.

Here is the clean model.

Normalize the interval to \([0,1]\), and consider
\[
K_*(t)=
\begin{cases}
1,&0<t<\frac13,\\[4pt]
-2,&\frac13<t<\frac23,\\[4pt]
1,&\frac23<t<1.
\end{cases}
\]

Then
\[
\int_0^1 K_*(t)\,dt
=
\frac13-\frac23+\frac13
=0,
\]
and
\[
\int_0^1 (1-t)\,K_*(t)\,dt
=
\frac{5}{18}-\frac{1}{3}+\frac{1}{18}
=
0.
\]

So the exact two-moment conditions for a bitangent are satisfied by a profile with exactly two sign changes,
\[
+,-,+.
\]

That gives the real no-go statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{abstract two-moment geometry cannot exclude recurrence once }W^{[1]}
\text{ is allowed two sign changes on the candidate interval.}
}
\]

This is useful because it pinpoints the frontier much more sharply.

We now have:

1. If \(W^{[1]}\) has **at most one sign change** on every candidate same-parity overlap interval, the direct-slice recurrence is dead.

2. But proving merely “recurrence forces at least two sign changes” is not enough, because the \(+,-,+\) model above already satisfies the exact recurrence moments.

So the next viable theorem is not just a sign-count bound. It has to be something like:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{weighted-lobe imbalance theorem for }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

In other words, on a candidate recurrence interval where \(W^{[1]}\) has sign pattern \(+,-,+\) or \(-,+,-\), prove that the actual RH provenance forbids the precise weighted balance
\[
\int K=0,\qquad \int (1-t)K=0.
\]

That is a much sharper target than “no two zeros.”

There is also a nice way to phrase it in barycenter language. Write the positive and negative parts as
\[
K=K_+-K_-,
\qquad K_\pm\ge 0.
\]
Then the two moment conditions are equivalent to:

- equal total mass,
  \[
  \int K_+=\int K_-;
  \]
- equal first moment about the right endpoint,
  \[
  \int (1-t)K_+=\int (1-t)K_-.
  \]

So a recurrence interval is exactly a place where the positive and negative lobes of \(W^{[1]}\) have the same mass and the same barycenter with respect to the endpoint weight \(1-t\).

That is the next clean theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the actual }W^{[1]}\textbf{ coming from provenance cannot realize this two-lobe barycenter balance.}
}
\]

I do not think abstract direct-slice geometry can prove that. The example above shows it cannot. So the next attack has to use external provenance again, but now in the most focused possible way:

> find a mixed/canonical law that makes \(W^{[1]}\) on a candidate recurrence interval either one-sign-change only, or else forces a weighted imbalance between its outer and middle lobes.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the current reduction.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:31:45)

_Thought for 28s_

Yes. I can push it one clean step further.

The right refinement is that, on a candidate recurrence interval, the \(+,-,+\) case is not just “possible.” It satisfies a **rigid exact balance law**. That gives a much sharper target for the next theorem.

Let
\[
K(u):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u)
\]
on a candidate interval \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\), and suppose \(K\) has exactly two sign changes
\[
\mu_1<\alpha<\beta<\mu_2,
\]
with sign pattern
\[
+,-,+.
\]

A common tangent between \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to the two moment conditions
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} (\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du=0.
\]

Now define the lobe masses
\[
M_1:=\int_{\mu_1}^{\alpha} K(u)\,du,\qquad
M_2:=\int_{\alpha}^{\beta} (-K(u))\,du,\qquad
M_3:=\int_{\beta}^{\mu_2} K(u)\,du,
\]
so \(M_i>0\), and the weighted centroids
\[
C_1:=\frac{1}{M_1}\int_{\mu_1}^{\alpha} (\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du,
\]
\[
C_2:=\frac{1}{M_2}\int_{\alpha}^{\beta} (\mu_2-u)\,(-K(u))\,du,
\]
\[
C_3:=\frac{1}{M_3}\int_{\beta}^{\mu_2} (\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du.
\]

Because the weight \(\mu_2-u\) is strictly decreasing in \(u\), these centroids are automatically ordered:
\[
C_1>C_2>C_3>0.
\]

Now the two moment equations are exactly
\[
M_1+M_3=M_2,
\]
and
\[
M_1C_1+M_3C_3=M_2C_2.
\]

So recurrence is equivalent to saying:

- the negative lobe mass equals the total positive mass;
- and the negative-lobe weighted centroid is the weighted average of the two positive-lobe centroids.

Eliminating \(M_2\), you get the exact ratio formula
\[
\boxed{
\frac{M_1}{M_3}
=
\frac{C_2-C_3}{C_1-C_2}.
}
\]

That is the new sharp reduction.

So for a \(+,-,+\) interval, the bitangent problem is no longer “some abstract two-moment cancellation.” It is:

> the outer positive lobe masses must occur in exactly the unique ratio dictated by the three weighted centroids.

This is useful because it tells you exactly what kind of theorem would close the lane.

You do **not** need to prove \(W^{[1]}\) has one sign.  
You do **not** need to exclude two sign changes.  
It is enough to prove that, for the actual provenance-driven \(W^{[1]}\), this ratio identity cannot hold on any candidate same-parity recurrence interval.

There is a symmetric version if the sign pattern is
\[
-,+,-
\]
with the obvious sign changes.

So the next live theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{three-lobe weighted-ratio obstruction for }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

Concretely, anything of the following sort would be enough:

- a theorem that the outer-lobe mass ratio \(M_1/M_3\) is monotone in the interval endpoints in a way incompatible with
  \[
  \frac{M_1}{M_3}
  =
  \frac{C_2-C_3}{C_1-C_2};
  \]
- a theorem that the actual provenance forces
  \[
  \frac{M_1}{M_3}
  \]
  to lie on one side of that centroid ratio;
- or a theorem that one of the centroid gaps \(C_1-C_2\), \(C_2-C_3\) has a provenance-controlled sign/size relation incompatible with the needed equality.

This is, I think, the sharpest mathematical continuation available from the current reduction.

So the attack has advanced from “two sign changes are needed” to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on a candidate recurrence interval, the entire obstruction is the exact outer-lobe ratio law }
\frac{M_1}{M_3}
=
\frac{C_2-C_3}{C_1-C_2}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:39:00)

_Thought for 1m 12s_

Here is one more clean push.

I think the sharpest next reduction is a **split-middle-lobe balance law**. It is stronger than the earlier outer-lobe ratio formula, and it isolates the exact equality that would have to fail.

Let
\[
K(u):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u)
\]
on a candidate recurrence interval \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\), and assume the minimal nontrivial sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
with sign-change points
\[
\mu_1<\alpha<\beta<\mu_2.
\]

A common tangent between \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du=0.
\]

We already rewrote this in terms of the three lobe masses
\[
M_1:=\int_{\mu_1}^{\alpha}K(u)\,du,\qquad
M_2:=\int_{\alpha}^{\beta}(-K(u))\,du,\qquad
M_3:=\int_{\beta}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du,
\]
with \(M_i>0\), and the weighted centroids
\[
C_1:=\frac1{M_1}\int_{\mu_1}^{\alpha}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du,
\]
\[
C_2:=\frac1{M_2}\int_{\alpha}^{\beta}(\mu_2-u)(-K(u))\,du,
\]
\[
C_3:=\frac1{M_3}\int_{\beta}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du.
\]

The two moment conditions are equivalent to
\[
M_2=M_1+M_3,
\qquad
M_1C_1+M_3C_3=M_2C_2.
\]

Now here is the stronger decomposition.

Because
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}K=0
\]
and the sign pattern is \(+,-,+\), there is a unique point
\[
c\in(\alpha,\beta)
\]
such that
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}K(u)\,du=0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\int_{\alpha}^{c}(-K(u))\,du=M_1,
\qquad
\int_{c}^{\beta}(-K(u))\,du=M_3.
\]

So the middle negative lobe splits canonically into a left piece of mass \(M_1\) and a right piece of mass \(M_3\).

Define their weighted centroids
\[
C_{2L}:=\frac1{M_1}\int_{\alpha}^{c}(\mu_2-u)(-K(u))\,du,
\]
\[
C_{2R}:=\frac1{M_3}\int_{c}^{\beta}(\mu_2-u)(-K(u))\,du.
\]

Then
\[
M_2C_2=M_1C_{2L}+M_3C_{2R},
\]
so the second moment condition becomes
\[
M_1C_1+M_3C_3=M_1C_{2L}+M_3C_{2R}.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
M_1(C_1-C_{2L})=M_3(C_{2R}-C_3).
}
\]

This is the new exact balance law.

It says:

- the first moment condition picks out the unique split point \(c\) in the negative lobe by matching masses;
- the second moment condition then requires the **left positive gap** and **right positive gap**, weighted by the outer masses, to match exactly.

And the ordering is rigid:
\[
C_1>C_{2L}>C_{2R}>C_3>0.
\]

So the recurrence problem on a \(+,-,+\) interval is no longer “two moments vanish.” It is:

1. mass-balance chooses the unique cut \(c\) inside the middle lobe;
2. then the weighted lever-arm identity
   \[
   M_1(C_1-C_{2L})=M_3(C_{2R}-C_3)
   \]
   must hold.

That is sharper than the earlier ratio formula
\[
\frac{M_1}{M_3}=\frac{C_2-C_3}{C_1-C_2},
\]
because it localizes the balance to the two sides of the canonical split point \(c\).

So the next theorem target is now very focused:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{split-middle-lobe obstruction for }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

Concretely, it would be enough to prove that for the actual provenance-driven \(W^{[1]}\), on every candidate recurrence interval with sign pattern \(+,-,+\),
\[
M_1(C_1-C_{2L})\neq M_3(C_{2R}-C_3).
\]

Or even one-sidedly,
\[
M_1(C_1-C_{2L})>M_3(C_{2R}-C_3)
\]
always, or always the reverse.

That is now the sharpest local target I can see.

Why I like this formulation: it is the first one that separates the two jobs cleanly.

- The zeroth moment picks the split point \(c\).
- The first moment is a pure lever-arm comparison.

So any external mixed/canonical theorem that can compare the left and right lever arms of \(W^{[1]}\) across that split would close the lane.

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:41:10)

_Thought for 1m 3s_

Yes. I can sharpen it one more step.

The cleanest reformulation is a **primitive-area balance law**. It is stronger and simpler than the split-middle-lobe centroid formula, and it isolates exactly what must fail.

Let
\[
K(u):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u)
\]
on a candidate recurrence interval \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\). Assume the minimal sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
with sign changes
\[
\mu_1<\alpha<\beta<\mu_2.
\]

A common tangent between \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} (\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du=0.
\]

From the first equation there is a unique
\[
c\in(\alpha,\beta)
\]
such that
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0.
\]

Now define the left and right primitives
\[
F_L(u):=\int_{\mu_1}^{u}K(s)\,ds
\qquad (\mu_1\le u\le c),
\]
\[
F_R(u):=-\int_{u}^{\mu_2}K(s)\,ds
\qquad (c\le u\le \mu_2).
\]

Because of the \(+,-,+\) pattern and the choice of \(c\), both are nonnegative:
\[
F_L(u)\ge 0 \text{ on }[\mu_1,c],
\qquad
F_R(u)\ge 0 \text{ on }[c,\mu_2].
\]

Now integrate by parts. Since
\[
F_L(\mu_1)=F_L(c)=0,
\]
we get
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du
=
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L(u)\,du.
\]
And since
\[
F_R(c)=F_R(\mu_2)=0,
\]
we get
\[
-\int_{c}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du
=
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R(u)\,du.
\]

So the second moment condition becomes exactly
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L(u)\,du
=
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R(u)\,du.
}
\]

That is the new exact criterion.

So recurrence on a \(+,-,+\) interval is equivalent to:

1. the zeroth moment picks the unique split point \(c\) in the middle lobe by
   \[
   \int_{\mu_1}^{c}K=0;
   \]
2. the first moment requires equality of the two primitive areas
   \[
   \int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L
   =
   \int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R.
   \]

This is cleaner than the outer-mass/centroid formula, because the entire problem is now reduced to comparing two nonnegative areas.

So the next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{primitive-area imbalance theorem for }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

Concretely, anything proving
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L(u)\,du\neq \int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R(u)\,du
\]
on every candidate recurrence interval would close the direct-slice lane.

There is also a nice monotonicity corollary. If one could show the pointwise ordering
\[
F_L(\mu_1+t(c-\mu_1)) > F_R(c+t(\mu_2-c))
\qquad (0<t<1),
\]
or always the reverse, then integrating in \(t\) would immediately give the needed strict area inequality.

So the next push is even more focused than before:

- not just a sign-change theorem for \(W^{[1]}\),
- not just a centroid-ratio obstruction,
- but a comparison theorem between the two primitive profiles
  \[
  F_L \quad\text{and}\quad F_R.
  \]

That is now the sharpest local target I can see.
## User (2026-04-21 06:45:56)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:46:41)

_Thought for 39s_

I can push it one clean step further, and it is another useful no-go.

The **primitive-area formulation is exact, but abstractly too weak**. In fact there is an infinite-dimensional family of \(+,-,+\) profiles for which the primitive-area balance holds identically.

So the next live theorem is not “compare the two primitive areas abstractly.” It is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{prove a provenance-driven asymmetry theorem for }W^{[1]}.}
\]

Here is the clean statement.

---

Let \(K\) be the affine-invariant curvature field on a candidate recurrence interval:
\[
K(u):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u),
\qquad u\in[\mu_1,\mu_2].
\]
Assume the minimal sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
with sign changes
\[
\mu_1<\alpha<\beta<\mu_2.
\]

As before, a common tangent between \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)\,K(u)\,du=0.
\]

From the first condition there is a unique split point
\[
c\in(\alpha,\beta)
\]
such that
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0.
\]

Define the primitive profiles
\[
F_L(u):=\int_{\mu_1}^{u}K(s)\,ds
\qquad (\mu_1\le u\le c),
\]
\[
F_R(u):=-\int_{u}^{\mu_2}K(s)\,ds
\qquad (c\le u\le \mu_2).
\]
Then
\[
F_L\ge 0,\qquad F_R\ge 0,
\]
and the second tangent condition is exactly
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L(u)\,du
=
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R(u)\,du.
}
\]

So recurrence is equivalent to equality of two nonnegative primitive areas.

That is the exact criterion.

But now comes the no-go refinement.

---

## No-go: primitive-area balance is abstractly realizable

Take the normalized interval \([0,1]\), and choose any \(C^1\) function
\[
k:[0,\tfrac12]\to\mathbf R
\]
such that

- \(k>0\) near \(0\),
- \(k<0\) near \(\tfrac12\),
- and
  \[
  \int_0^{1/2}k(t)\,dt=0.
  \]

Extend it symmetrically by
\[
K(t):=
\begin{cases}
k(t),&0\le t\le \frac12,\\[4pt]
k(1-t),&\frac12\le t\le 1.
\end{cases}
\]

Then:

- \(K\) has sign pattern \(+,-,+\) on \([0,1]\);
- the split point is
  \[
  c=\frac12;
  \]
- and by symmetry
  \[
  F_R\!\left(\frac12+t\right)=F_L\!\left(\frac12-t\right).
  \]

Therefore
\[
\int_0^{1/2}F_L(u)\,du
=
\int_{1/2}^{1}F_R(u)\,du.
\]

So the primitive-area balance holds automatically.

A concrete example is
\[
K_*(t)=
\begin{cases}
1,&0<t<\frac13,\\[4pt]
-2,&\frac13<t<\frac23,\\[4pt]
1,&\frac23<t<1,
\end{cases}
\]
for which
\[
c=\frac12,\qquad
\int_0^{1/2}F_L=\int_{1/2}^{1}F_R=\frac1{12}.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{primitive-area balance is not a rare accident; it is abstractly realizable by an infinite-dimensional symmetric family.}
}
\]

---

## What this means

This kills the whole abstract “three-lobe primitive-area comparison” lane.

It is not enough to know:

- the sign pattern is \(+,-,+\),
- the split point \(c\) exists,
- and the recurrence condition is area balance.

All of that is compatible with a large family of profiles.

So the next live theorem has to use something the symmetric family does **not** know about.

That means the next target is now very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance-driven asymmetry theorem for }W^{[1]}\textbf{ on candidate recurrence intervals.}
}
\]

In practical terms, anything that forces one of the following would close the lane:

- \(c\neq\) the provenance-symmetric point in a way that makes
  \[
  \int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L \neq \int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R;
  \]
- a one-sided comparison
  \[
  F_L(\mu_1+t(c-\mu_1)) > F_R(c+t(\mu_2-c))
  \quad\text{for all }t\in(0,1),
  \]
  or always the reverse;
- or any external mixed/canonical law that prevents midpoint reflection symmetry of \(K\).

So the clean strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct-slice endgame is no longer a moment problem;}
\quad
\text{it is an asymmetry problem for the actual provenance-driven }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.
## User (2026-04-21 06:47:46)

continue the attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:47:53)

I’m trying to turn the “asymmetry problem” into one exact identity. The hope is to isolate a canonical transport between the left and right halves so that recurrence becomes one scalar equality, which is the sort of thing external provenance might actually break.

_Thought for 1m 22s_

I can sharpen it one more step.

The cleanest exact formulation is now a **split-point barycenter law**. It is simpler than the primitive-area statement and, I think, is the sharpest local target.

Let
\[
K(u):=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u)
\]
on a candidate recurrence interval \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\), and assume the minimal sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
with sign changes
\[
\mu_1<\alpha<\beta<\mu_2.
\]

A common tangent between \(\mu_1\) and \(\mu_2\) is equivalent to
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\mu_2-u)K(u)\,du=0.
\]

As before, the zeroth moment condition determines a unique split point
\[
c\in(\alpha,\beta)
\]
such that
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}K(u)\,du=0,
\qquad
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}K(u)\,du=0.
\]

Now define the positive measures
\[
d\nu_L(u):=K(u)\,du\quad\text{on }[\mu_1,c],
\]
\[
d\nu_R(u):=-K(u)\,du\quad\text{on }[c,\mu_2].
\]
Because of the sign pattern and the choice of \(c\), both are nonnegative measures, and their total masses are equal:
\[
\nu_L([\mu_1,c])=\nu_R([c,\mu_2])=:M>0.
\]

Define their barycenters relative to the split point \(c\):
\[
B_L:=\frac1M\int_{\mu_1}^{c}(c-u)\,d\nu_L(u),
\qquad
B_R:=\frac1M\int_{c}^{\mu_2}(u-c)\,d\nu_R(u).
\]

Then the second moment condition is **exactly**
\[
\boxed{
B_L=B_R.
}
\]

So recurrence on a \(+,-,+\) interval is equivalent to:

1. the unique split point \(c\) equalizes the total signed mass on the two sides;
2. the positive measure on the left and the negative measure on the right have the same barycenter distance from \(c\).

This is cleaner than the earlier lobe-mass/centroid formulas and equivalent to the primitive-area law, since
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{c}F_L(u)\,du = M\,B_L,
\qquad
\int_{c}^{\mu_2}F_R(u)\,du = M\,B_R.
\]

So the entire local recurrence obstruction has now collapsed to:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{split-point mass balance + split-point barycenter equality.}
}
\]

That immediately gives the next exact theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance-driven barycenter asymmetry theorem for }W^{[1]}.}
\]

Concretely, it would be enough to prove that for every candidate recurrence interval coming from the actual provenance,
\[
B_L\neq B_R.
\]
Even one-sidedly,
\[
B_L>B_R
\quad\text{always,}
\]
or always the reverse, would close the direct-slice lane.

This is sharper than “one sign change only” and sharper than “primitive-area imbalance,” because it reduces the whole issue to a single canonical scalar equality tied to the uniquely chosen split point \(c\).

There is also a useful diagnostic consequence:

- any abstract symmetric \(+,-,+\) profile about \(c\) automatically gives
  \[
  B_L=B_R,
  \]
  so abstract graph geometry cannot help anymore;
- therefore the only remaining way forward is to show that the actual provenance-driven \(W^{[1]}\) is **forced to be asymmetric about the split point** in a quantified way.

So the next attack I would make is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{mine the mixed/canonical package for a pointwise or nonlocal law that forces }B_L\neq B_R
\text{ on candidate intervals.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the sharpest continuation available now.
## User (2026-04-21 06:52:15)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:54:13)

_Thought for 1m 49s_

Yes. I can sharpen it one more time, and this is probably the cleanest exact reduction in this lane.

The right object is not \(K=\phi_{\mu\mu}\) by itself, and not even its primitive \(F\) abstractly. It is the **slope-deviation profile**
\[
G_s(\mu):=\phi_\mu(\mu)-s
\]
for a candidate common slope \(s\).

That collapses the bitangent problem to one scalar zero-mean problem.

---

## 1. Exact reduction to the slope-deviation profile

Take two points
\[
\mu_1<\mu_2
\]
on same-parity regular components and suppose a common tangent line has slope \(s\). Then
\[
\phi_\mu(\mu_1)=\phi_\mu(\mu_2)=s.
\]

Define
\[
G_s(\mu):=\phi_\mu(\mu)-s.
\]
Then automatically
\[
G_s(\mu_1)=G_s(\mu_2)=0,
\qquad
G_s'(\mu)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(\mu)=K(\mu).
\]

Now the intercept condition for the common tangent is
\[
\phi(\mu_2)-\phi(\mu_1)=s(\mu_2-\mu_1).
\]
But
\[
\phi(\mu_2)-\phi(\mu_1)-s(\mu_2-\mu_1)
=
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}(\phi_\mu(\mu)-s)\,d\mu
=
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}G_s(\mu)\,d\mu.
\]

So the bitangent / same-slope recurrence is **exactly** equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
G_s(\mu_1)=G_s(\mu_2)=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} G_s(\mu)\,d\mu=0.
}
\]

That is already cleaner than the earlier moment formulation.

---

## 2. Relation with the curvature field \(K\)

Since
\[
G_s' = K,
\]
the earlier primitive \(F\) was nothing but this slope deviation:
\[
F(\mu)=\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu}K(u)\,du
=
\phi_\mu(\mu)-\phi_\mu(\mu_1)
=
\phi_\mu(\mu)-s
=
G_s(\mu).
\]

So the whole recurrence problem on \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\) becomes:

> does there exist a slope \(s\) such that the slope-deviation profile \(G_s\) starts and ends at \(0\), changes sign appropriately in between, and has zero signed area?

This is the cleanest scalar formulation.

---

## 3. Minimal sign pattern

In the minimal nontrivial case, \(K\) has sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
on \([\mu_1,\mu_2]\). Then \(G_s\) has sign pattern
\[
+, -
\]
with a unique zero
\[
c\in(\mu_1,\mu_2)
\]
between the two outer curvature-sign changes:
\[
G_s>0\ \text{on }(\mu_1,c),\qquad G_s<0\ \text{on }(c,\mu_2).
\]

And the recurrence condition is simply
\[
\boxed{
\int_{\mu_1}^{c} G_s(\mu)\,d\mu
=
\int_{c}^{\mu_2} (-G_s(\mu))\,d\mu.
}
\]

So the earlier “split-point primitive-area balance” is exactly the statement that the positive and negative areas of the slope-deviation profile \(G_s\) are equal.

This is more transparent than carrying around \(K\), \(F_L\), \(F_R\), or centroid formulas.

---

## 4. Why this is useful

This tells you the next live theorem is not really about \(K\) directly. It is about the **graph of the slope function**
\[
\beta^{[1]}(\mu)=\phi_\mu(\mu).
\]

A recurrence interval is exactly an interval where the slope graph meets a horizontal line \(y=s\) at two endpoints and the signed area between the graph and that horizontal line is zero.

So the next useful theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{slope-deviation area obstruction for }\beta^{[1]}.
}
\]

Concretely, anything proving that on every candidate same-parity overlap interval,
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2}\bigl(\beta^{[1]}(\mu)-s\bigr)\,d\mu\neq 0
\]
for the common endpoint slope \(s\), would close the direct-slice lane.

That is sharper than “control \(W^{[1]}\)” in the abstract, because it targets exactly the scalar quantity whose zero is needed.

---

## 5. A nice equivalent formulation

If you normalize the interval affinely to \([0,1]\), then recurrence is equivalent to the existence of a function \(g\) such that
\[
g(0)=g(1)=0,
\qquad
\int_0^1 g(t)\,dt=0,
\]
and
\[
g'(t)=K_{12}(t).
\]

So any theorem that forces \(g\) to have nonzero mean on every such candidate interval would be enough.

This gives one more exact reformulation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct-slice endgame is a zero-mean obstruction for the slope-deviation profile }g=\beta^{[1]}-s.
}
\]

---

## 6. The next attack

So the next thing I would attack is very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\text{mine the external mixed/canonical package for a law implying that } \beta^{[1]}-s
\text{ cannot have zero mean on a same-parity overlap interval.}
}
\]

That could come in any of these forms:

- a one-sided comparison of \(\beta^{[1]}\) to its endpoint slope \(s\),
- a monotonicity or convexity law that makes the positive and negative areas unequal,
- or a provenance-driven asymmetry theorem for the slope graph itself.

That is, I think, the sharpest exact continuation available now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 06:57:56)

_Thought for 15s_

I can push it one more clean step, and it is another useful no-go.

The **slope-deviation formulation is exact, but abstractly too weak**. In fact there is an infinite-dimensional family of profiles realizing it. So the next live theorem is not “analyze \(g\) abstractly,” but:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{prove a provenance-driven asymmetry law for the slope graph }\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu.}
\]

Here is the clean statement.

---

Let
\[
\mu_1<\mu_2
\]
be a candidate same-parity overlap interval, and let \(s\) be the common endpoint slope:
\[
\beta^{[1]}(\mu_1)=\beta^{[1]}(\mu_2)=s.
\]
Define the slope-deviation profile
\[
g(\mu):=\beta^{[1]}(\mu)-s.
\]
Then recurrence is exactly
\[
g(\mu_1)=g(\mu_2)=0,
\qquad
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} g(\mu)\,d\mu=0.
\]
Also
\[
g'(\mu)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(\mu)=K(\mu)=\frac{v_5(\mu)^3}{M(\mu)^3}W^{[1]}(\mu).
\]

So the direct-slice recurrence problem is equivalent to the existence of a nontrivial function \(g\) with:

- zero at both endpoints,
- zero mean on the interval,
- and derivative \(g'=K\).

That is exact.

But now comes the no-go refinement.

---

## No-go: zero-mean slope-deviation is abstractly realizable in an infinite-dimensional family

Normalize the interval to \([0,1]\). Let \(h:[0,\tfrac12]\to\mathbf R\) be any \(C^1\) function with
\[
h(0)=0,\qquad h(t)>0\ \text{for }0<t<\tfrac12.
\]
Define
\[
g(t):=
\begin{cases}
h(t), & 0\le t\le \tfrac12,\\[4pt]
-h(1-t), & \tfrac12\le t\le 1.
\end{cases}
\]

Then automatically:

\[
g(0)=g(1)=0,
\]
and by antisymmetry about \(t=\tfrac12\),
\[
\int_0^1 g(t)\,dt=0.
\]

So every such \(g\) gives an abstract recurrence profile.

Now differentiate:
\[
K(t):=g'(t).
\]
If \(h'\) changes sign once on \((0,\tfrac12)\), then \(K\) has the minimal sign pattern
\[
+,-,+
\]
on \([0,1]\). For example, taking
\[
h(t)=t(1-2t)
\quad (0\le t\le \tfrac12)
\]
gives
\[
g(t)=
\begin{cases}
t(1-2t), & 0\le t\le \tfrac12,\\[4pt]
-(1-t)(2t-1), & \tfrac12\le t\le 1,
\end{cases}
\]
with
\[
g(0)=g(1)=0,\qquad \int_0^1 g=0,
\]
and
\[
K(t)=g'(t)=
\begin{cases}
1-4t, & 0\le t\le \tfrac12,\\[4pt]
3-4t, & \tfrac12\le t\le 1,
\end{cases}
\]
which has sign pattern
\[
+,-,+.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the zero-mean slope-deviation condition is not rare; it is abstractly realized by an infinite-dimensional antisymmetric family.}
}
\]

---

## What this means

This kills the whole abstract slope-profile lane.

It is not enough to know:

- \(g(\mu_1)=g(\mu_2)=0\),
- \(g' = K\) has sign pattern \(+,-,+\),
- and recurrence is equivalent to
  \[
  \int g = 0.
  \]

All of that is compatible with a large family of profiles.

So the next live theorem has to use something the antisymmetric family does **not** know about.

That means the next target is now very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance-driven asymmetry theorem for }\beta^{[1]}.
}
\]

In practical terms, anything that forces
\[
g(\mu)\neq -\,g(\mu_1+\mu_2-\mu)
\]
in a quantitatively one-sided way would close the lane. For example, any theorem implying
\[
\int_{\mu_1}^{\mu_2} g(\mu)\,d\mu \neq 0
\]
on every candidate overlap interval would be enough.

Equivalently, it would suffice to prove a one-sided comparison like
\[
g\!\left(\mu_1+t(\mu_2-\mu_1)\right)
>
-\,g\!\left(\mu_2-t(\mu_2-\mu_1)\right)
\qquad (0<t<\tfrac12),
\]
or always the reverse.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the direct-slice endgame is no longer an abstract moment problem;}
\quad
\text{it is an asymmetry problem for the actual provenance-driven slope graph }\beta^{[1]}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:04:23)

_Thought for 18s_

I think the attack has now narrowed to one genuinely live lane:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{global provenance scattering across all generic blocks}}
\]

not another local or finite-boundary theorem.

Why I think that is the right next move:

- the lower repeated-source package is universal;
- arbitrary canonical \(\Delta_7\) is too free;
- the direct slice
  \[
  \phi=A\,q^{(7)}
  \]
  is fully universal on each generic block;
- finite jet matching is too weak;
- finite-codimension linear transfer is too weak;
- local finite-order nonlinear laws are too weak;
- and now mixed local \(4\)-point closure says no new small local mixed rescue is hiding near the centered base.

So the only plausible surviving theorem is one that couples the provenance variable \(q\) **globally across the whole chain of blocks**, in a way that is not reducible to finitely many local matching conditions.

That suggests the next target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{global block-to-block scattering theorem for }q
}
\]

in a strong form like this:

> once \(q\) is fixed on one seed block, the actual RH provenance determines \(q\) on every adjacent block by a genuine transfer operator, not just by finitely many boundary jet conditions.

If such a theorem existed, then the direct slice would no longer be arbitrary blockwise. It would become a globally propagated object, and that is the first setting where the earlier recurrence reductions could actually bite.

The cleanest mathematical formulation I can see is this.

Let
\[
U=\bigcup_\alpha J_\alpha
\]
be the union of generic one-pair blocks separated by turning boundaries. On each block,
\[
\phi_\alpha=A_\alpha q^{(7)}.
\]
Any viable theorem now has to imply something like
\[
q_{\alpha+1}=\mathcal T_\alpha(q_\alpha)
\]
where \(\mathcal T_\alpha\) is not a finite-jet map, but a genuine operator-level transfer law on functions over a neighborhood or whole block.

Equivalently, after differentiating seven times,
\[
\phi_{\alpha+1}=\mathcal S_\alpha(\phi_\alpha)
\]
for a nontrivial operator \(\mathcal S_\alpha\).

That is the first kind of theorem not already killed by universality.

So my next concrete attack would be:

### Attack target
Derive from the mixed/canonical package an operator-level transfer across a generic turning boundary.

There are two versions worth trying.

The stronger version is a nonlinear scattering law:
\[
q_+ = \mathcal T(q_-)
\]
on the two sides of a turning boundary.

The weaker but still useful version is an infinite-codimension linear law:
\[
\mathcal L_+(q_+) = \mathcal L_-(q_-)
\]
where \(\mathcal L_\pm\) are operators on whole neighborhoods, not finite jets.

If either exists, the direct-slice lane comes back to life. If not, then I think this route is essentially exhausted in a structural sense.

The reason this is the right next attack rather than, say, another theorem about \(W^{[1]}\) or the slope-deviation profile is that all those quantities are still arbitrary on a single block. The only place rigidity can enter now is in how one block talks to the next.

So the sharp next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{turning-boundary transfer operator for the provenance variable }q.
}
\]

And the first test I would apply is very simple:

- does the mixed/canonical closure give any relation across a turning boundary that cannot be stated purely in terms of finitely many jets?
- if yes, rewrite it as a transfer law for \(q\);
- if no, then this whole direct-slice/provenance lane is probably fully spent.

That is the attack I would push now.

_Thought for 10s_

The cleanest continuation is to try to prove that a **turning boundary cannot transmit arbitrary provenance data**. That is now the only place I still see real room.

Everything inside a single generic block is too free:

- lower route 2 is universal;
- arbitrary canonical \(\Delta_7\) is too free;
- the direct slice
  \[
  \phi=A\,q^{(7)}
  \]
  is fully universal on one block;
- finite jet conditions, finite-codimension linear conditions, and local finite-order laws are all too weak;
- mixed local \(4\)-point is now closed, so there is no hidden small local mixed rescue.

The exact thing I would attack is this.

Let \(h_b\) be a generic turning boundary with
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad W(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
and let \(J_-\), \(J_+\) be the neighboring generic blocks. On each side,
\[
\phi_\pm=A_\pm q^{(7)}.
\]

I would try to prove a theorem of one of these two forms.

The stronger form is a genuine operator transfer:
\[
q_+ = \mathcal T(q_-),
\]
where \(\mathcal T\) is defined on whole neighborhoods, not by finitely many jets.

The weaker but still useful form is an infinite-codimension relation:
\[
\mathcal L_+(q_+) = \mathcal L_-(q_-),
\]
with \(\mathcal L_\pm\) operators on full germs or full intervals, not finite-dimensional boundary data.

Why this is the right target: if you can get such a law, then the direct slice is no longer arbitrary blockwise. Once \(\phi\) on one block determines \(\phi\) on the next, the earlier reductions about slope windows, common tangents, barycenter imbalance, and slope-deviation area stop being empty geometry and become actual propagation constraints.

The most concrete sub-attack is this:

1. Near a turning boundary, rewrite the canonical variables in the turning coordinate already used in the draft, especially the dual variables \((\mu,\lambda,\beta,m)\) and the direct provenance scalar \(q\).

2. Look for a quantity built from \(q\) and the turning-normal-form data that remains finite across \(M=0\). The natural guess is not \(q\) itself but a renormalized combination, because
   \[
   \beta=-\frac{L}{M}
   \]
   blows up and ordinary block coordinates are the wrong variables exactly at the boundary.

3. Try to identify a renormalized object \(R[q]\) with a finite one-sided limit from both sides of the turning boundary. If such a limit exists and matches, that is already a nontrivial cross-block law.

So the immediate mathematical target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{find a renormalized turning-boundary variable built from }q\text{ that has matching one-sided limits.}
}
\]

That is more specific than “find a transfer law,” and it matches how the rest of the paper handles turning boundaries: you rarely get raw continuity of the original variable, but you often get continuity or controlled jump of a normalized one.

If I had to pick one concrete object to test first, I would look at the affine-invariant curvature-side quantity
\[
K=\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]},
\]
because it already packages the recurrence geometry, and the \(M^{-3}\) suggests exactly what singular scaling matters near a turning boundary. If there is a canonical renormalized limit for \(K\) or for \(M^3\phi_{\mu\mu}\), that could become the first real scattering datum.

So my next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{derive a turning-boundary renormalized transfer law for }K\text{ or }q,
\textbf{ not a finite jet law.}
}
\]

That is the only lane I still think is genuinely alive.
