## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:10:21)

_Thought for 1m 29s_

I found the first genuine **cross-block transfer law** for \(q\), but it is also a **no-go**: it is real, yet still too weak to close the lane.

The law is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{turning-boundary square-root scattering for }q.}
\]

Let \(h_b\) be a generic turning boundary. Then \(\mu(h)\) has a nondegenerate extremum there, so after an analytic reparametrization one may choose a local coordinate \(t\) with
\[
t(h_b)=0,
\qquad
\mu=\mu_b+\sigma\, t^2,
\qquad \sigma\in\{\pm1\}.
\]
If \(q(h)\) is analytic in \(h\), then in this coordinate
\[
q(h)=\widehat q(t)
\]
for an analytic function \(\widehat q\). The two neighboring \(\mu\)-branches correspond to \(t=\pm \sqrt{\sigma(\mu-\mu_b)}\), so their provenance functions satisfy
\[
q_\pm(\mu)=\widehat q\!\bigl(\pm \sqrt{\sigma(\mu-\mu_b)}\bigr).
\]

Equivalently, there exist analytic functions \(E(\mu),O(\mu)\) near \(\mu_b\) such that
\[
\boxed{
q_\pm(\mu)=E(\mu)\pm \sqrt{\sigma(\mu-\mu_b)}\,O(\mu).
}
\]

So in particular,
\[
\boxed{
q_+(\mu)+q_-(\mu)\ \text{is analytic in }\mu,
\qquad
q_+(\mu)-q_-(\mu)\ \text{is } \sqrt{\sigma(\mu-\mu_b)}\times \text{analytic}.
}
\]

This is the exact cross-block scattering law.

And because any finite \(h\)-derivative of \(q\) is obtained by applying an analytic differential operator in \(t\) to \(\widehat q(t)\), the same parity structure holds for all derivative-based provenance quantities. In particular, after dividing by the nonvanishing direct-slice coefficients \(A_\pm\), the two direct provenance slices satisfy the same square-root even/odd coupling across the turning boundary.

So this really is the first **infinite-codimension** transfer relation across blocks.

---

But here is the honest punchline:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{this scattering law is still too weak.}}
\]

Why? Because it is automatic for any globally analytic \(q(h)\). And our earlier global-polynomial counterexamples already use a single global analytic \(q\), so they automatically satisfy this square-root scattering law across every turning boundary.

So the situation is now very sharp:

- finitely many jet-transfer laws are too weak;
- finite-codimension linear transfer laws are too weak;
- and even the natural infinite-codimension scattering law coming from global analyticity of \(q\) across turning boundaries is still too weak.

That means the next live theorem cannot be just “\(q\) continues analytically across turning boundaries.” We already have that, and it does not prevent recurrence.

So the next attack has to use something stronger than bare analyticity of \(q\). It has to be a theorem that says \(q\) is not just any analytic function of \(h\), but a very special one.

That is the clean strategic update:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the remaining target is a non-tautological global law for }q\textbf{ as a function of }h,
}
\]
not merely a blockwise law in \(\mu\)-coordinates.

In concrete terms, the next useful theorem would have to be something like:

- a global ODE or integro-differential equation in the \(h\)-variable for \(q\),
- or a nonlinear monotonicity/energy law for \(q(h)\),
- or a mixed/canonical identity that constrains \(q\) on a whole \(h\)-interval beyond mere analyticity.

So the attack has advanced, but in a very clarifying way:

\[
\boxed{
\text{turning-boundary scattering exists, but it is tautological;}
\quad
\text{the next live lane is a genuinely non-tautological global law for }q(h).
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

Here is the strongest honest continuation I can give.

At this point I think the next attack is no longer about block geometry, slope windows, or turning-boundary matching. Those are now reduced to **tautological consequences of analyticity**.

That is the only place I still see genuine room.

Why I think that:

- On each generic block,
  \[
  \phi=A\,q^{(7)}
  \]
  is fully universal.
- Finite jet conditions are too weak.
- Finite-codimension linear transfer is too weak.
- Local finite-order nonlinear laws are too weak.
- Even the first infinite-codimension cross-block law — the square-root scattering across turning boundaries — is automatic for any globally analytic \(q(h)\), so it is still too weak.
- Mixed local \(4\)-point closure rules out a hidden new local mixed rescue.

\[

with \(\mathcal L_h\) a nontrivial operator in the \(h\)-variable not equivalent to taking seven derivatives,

}
\]
for some nonlinear energy-like functional,

or

\text{ with kernel/data already controlled elsewhere in the proof.}
}
\]

That is the right level because only a law on the full \(h\)-axis can beat the universality and interpolation counterexamples.

## Attack target
Derive the first **non-tautological global law in \(h\)** for the provenance variable \(q\) from the mixed/canonical closure package.

This is the minimal useful affine-invariant curvature datum for the direct-slice lane. If you can determine or constrain

\]
as a function of \(h\), then the recurrence geometry becomes rigid.

Stronger than necessary, but still excellent:
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=H(h,\text{external data}).
\]
Then
\[
\phi=A\,H
\]
is fixed, and the direct-slice lane revives immediately.

### 3. A global nonlinear monotonicity law for \(q\)
Something like
\[
\frac{d}{dh}\mathcal E[q](h)\ge 0
\]
or a sign-definite integral identity, which would force asymmetry of the slope-deviation profile and kill the bitangent condition.

There is also a good negative lesson now:

The next theorem should **not** be framed in \(\mu\)-coordinates.

}
\]

That is where I would push next. If you want, the next step I can take is to turn this into a concrete theorem checklist for the draft: exactly what kinds of candidate laws for \(q(h)\) are still alive, and which whole classes are now ruled out.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:19:46)

I’m trying to turn the remaining freedom into a precise theorem shape: not just “find a global law for \(q\),” but what order and form such a law would have to have to actually bite the recurrence geometry.

_Thought for 34s_

The cleanest next push is this:

## Stop targeting \(q\) itself. Target
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h).
\]

That is the first gauge-free provenance field.

Everything we learned says the degree-\(\le 6\) polynomial gauge on \(q\) is the main source of useless freedom. Once you pass to
\[
r=q^{(7)},
\]
that gauge disappears, and the direct slice is simply
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,r(h).
\]

So the next real theorem should be phrased for \(r(h)\), not for \(q(h)\).

That gives a much sharper attack.

---

## 1. Why \(r=q^{(7)}\) is the right variable

All the earlier no-go results were really gauge-obstruction results:

- local finite-order laws for \(q\) of order \(\le 6\) are vacuous;
- any gauge-invariant pointwise law of order \(7\) or \(8\) reduces to a law for \(\phi=Aq^{(7)}\);
- turning-boundary scattering for \(q\) is tautological because any globally analytic \(q\) has it.

So the first genuinely invariant field is
\[
r(h)=q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Any non-tautological global provenance theorem now has to constrain \(r\) on a whole \(h\)-interval.

Equivalently, since
\[
\phi=A\,r,
\]
it must constrain \(\phi\) on a whole interval.

That is the new clean variable choice.

---

## 2. What kinds of global laws for \(r\) would actually matter

There are only three really live classes left.

### A. A pointwise algebraic law
\[
r(h)=H(h,\text{external data}).
\]
Then
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)H(h,\text{external data}),
\]
so the direct slice is completely prescribed.

This is the best-case scenario.

### B. A first-order ODE
\[
r'(h)=G(h,\text{external data},r(h)).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\phi'(h)=\widetilde G(h,\text{external data},\phi(h)).
\]
That reduces the direct slice to a one-parameter family. Still strong enough that the bitangent / same-slope lane becomes highly rigid.

### C. A nonlocal law
Something like
\[
r(h)=F(h,\text{external data})+\int_{h_0}^h K(h,s)\,\mathcal N(r(s),\text{external data}(s))\,ds.
\]
That is harder, but still useful because it cuts down the arbitrary-analytic freedom.

So the next theorem search can be stated very concretely:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find the first global law for }r=q^{(7)}\textbf{ on an \(h\)-interval.}
}
\]

---

## 3. A useful reduction: a global law for \(r\) is exactly a global law for the curvature datum

Recall the affine-invariant direct-slice curvature field
\[
K(\mu)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(\mu)=\frac{v_5(\mu)^3}{M(\mu)^3}W^{[1]}(\mu).
\]

Since \(\phi=A\,r\), any global law for \(r(h)\) becomes a global law for \(K\), after changing variables from \(h\) to \(\mu\).

So the attack can be phrased in two equivalent ways:

- provenance language: constrain \(r(h)=q^{(7)}(h)\);
- direct-slice language: constrain the curvature profile \(K\).

The advantage of the \(r\)-language is that it lives in the natural \(h\)-coordinate and avoids all the turning-boundary square-root bookkeeping.

So I would now reframe the problem as:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a global law for }r(h)\textbf{, then transport it to the direct slice.}
}
\]

---

## 4. What a strong enough global law would imply immediately

Suppose, for example, you could prove
\[
r'(h)=G(h,r(h))
\]
with \(G\) locally Lipschitz and one-signed in \(r\), or even just monotone in \(r\). Then \(r\) would be order-preserving, and \(\phi=A\,r\) would inherit a controlled variation pattern. That would likely kill the \(+,-,+\) barycenter balance and the zero-mean slope-deviation profiles we kept encountering.

Or if you could prove
\[
r(h)=H(h)
\]
pointwise, then all the remaining recurrence geometry becomes a concrete computation on a prescribed scalar graph.

So the whole direct-slice lane now really hinges on one question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{is \(r=q^{(7)}\) arbitrary globally in \(h\), or does the actual RH provenance force an ODE / integral law for it?}
}
\]

That is the sharpest formulation yet.

---

## 5. The most plausible source of such a law

The only place I still see a chance is the **mixed/canonical closure package**, but not through more local jet identities. It would have to be through some pointwise or integral relation where \(q^{(7)}\) appears as the highest-new term and the lower terms are already controlled by the mixed closure.

So the next concrete thing I would attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for a mixed/canonical identity whose highest-new part is linear in }q^{(7)}\textbf{ and whose lower part is already known.}
}
\]

If that exists, then it should be possible to solve it as
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,\text{known data}),
\]
or at worst
\[
q^{(7)}=H(h,\text{known data},q^{(7)}\text{ through a lower-order operator}),
\]
which is still a real global law.

That is the best next attack angle I can see now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:22:51)

_Thought for 28s_

Yes. I can sharpen the attack one more time.

The key new point is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{an order-8 pointwise law is not enough in the abstract.}}
\]

So the truly decisive pointwise target is not just “some law involving \(q^{(8)}\).” It is much narrower:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{a non-tautological order-7 algebraic determination of }r(h):=q^{(7)}(h).}
\]

That is the next place I would push.

---

## 1. Why order 8 is weaker than it looks

From the earlier reductions, any gauge-invariant pointwise law of order \(8\) for \(q\) becomes a first-order ODE for
\[
r=q^{(7)}
\]
or equivalently for
\[
\phi=A\,r.
\]

But a first-order ODE, by itself, does **not** kill recurrence geometry abstractly.

A simple example is
\[
\phi(\mu)=(\mu^2-1)^2.
\]
Then the line \(y=0\) is tangent at \(\mu=\pm 1\), so the direct-slice recurrence geometry is present.

Yet \(\phi\) satisfies the first-order ODE
\[
\phi'(\mu)=4\mu(\mu^2-1),
\]
indeed even
\[
\phi' = 4\mu\sqrt{\phi}
\]
on each sign branch. So “\(\phi\) satisfies a first-order ODE” is not, by itself, enough to rule out the bad geometry.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{order-8 pointwise laws can be useful only if their right-hand side has extra monotonicity/sign structure, not merely because they are first-order.}
}
\]

That means the first genuinely promising pointwise theorem is still order \(7\).

---

## 2. Why order 7 is the real prize

A nondegenerate order-7 pointwise law has the form
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),q^{(7)}(h)\bigr)=0,
\]
where \(B(h)\) denotes genuinely external mixed/canonical fields.

If
\[
\partial_{q^{(7)}}F\neq 0
\]
on the relevant block, then by the implicit function theorem this becomes
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=H(h,B(h)).
\]

That is much stronger than an order-8 law, because it kills the last functional freedom immediately:
\[
r(h)=q^{(7)}(h)\ \text{is prescribed,}
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)H(h,B(h))
\]
is prescribed,
and then all the direct-slice objects
\[
\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu,\qquad
m^{[1]}=\phi-\mu\phi_\mu,\qquad
K=\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}
\]
are prescribed too.

So the clean hierarchy is now:

- order \(\le 6\): impossible as a pointwise constraint on \(q\);
- order \(8\): possible, but abstractly too weak unless it comes with strong sign/monotonicity structure;
- order \(7\): the first pointwise level that can actually collapse the universality in one shot.

That makes the next target very specific.

---

## 3. The exact theorem shape to hunt

The next live theorem should look like this:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{mixed/canonical order-7 determination of }q^{(7)}.
}
\]

More concretely, find an identity whose highest-new part is
\[
a(h)\,q^{(7)}(h)+b(h),
\]
with \(a(h)\not\equiv 0\), and where \(b(h)\) depends only on fields already controlled elsewhere in the draft.

Then on a generic block,
\[
q^{(7)}(h)=-\,\frac{b(h)}{a(h)}.
\]

That is the first theorem that would really revive the direct-slice lane.

A slightly weaker but still viable target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{pointwise determination of }W^{[1]}(h).
}
\]

Since
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]},
\]
this would determine the affine class of \(\phi\), which is exactly the part seen by the recurrence geometry.

But if I had to choose one, I would still go after \(q^{(7)}\), because that is the cleaner provenance variable and the more decisive collapse.

---

## 4. The new success criterion

This gives a very sharp test for the next calculations:

When you inspect any mixed/canonical identity, ask only this:

> does its highest-new part contain \(q^{(7)}\) pointwise with nonzero coefficient, and are all remaining terms already controlled by previously closed packages?

If yes, that identity is potentially decisive.

If no, then it is probably either tautological, too weak, or already killed by universality.

That is the clearest filter I can give.

---

## 5. The next attack I would do

I would now go hunting specifically for an identity of the schematic form
\[
\mathcal I(h)=a(h)\,q^{(7)}(h)+b(h),
\]
with
\[
a(h)\neq 0
\]
on a generic block, and with \(b(h)\) built from already-controlled mixed/canonical fields.

That is the most promising remaining pointwise attack.

If that fails, then I would abandon pointwise laws and move fully to genuinely nonlocal nonlinear laws in \(h\).

## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:26:01)

_Thought for 29s_

Here is the strongest further push I can make without inventing formulas that the draft has not yet proved.

The next clean reduction is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{an order-7 pointwise theorem is useful only if it isolates }q^{(7)}\textbf{ in a provenance-independent highest-new slice.}}
\]

That sounds abstract, but it is actually a sharp filter on what to look for next.

---

## 1. Why the next viable pointwise law must isolate \(q^{(7)}\)

Let
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,r(h).
\]

From everything so far:

- order \(\le 6\) pointwise laws are vacuous on \(q\);
- order \(8\) pointwise laws only give first-order ODEs for \(r\) or \(\phi\), which are not enough in the abstract;
- order \(7\) is the first level where a pointwise law can collapse the universality.

But not every order-\(7\) law is useful.

A candidate identity has the schematic form
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),r(h)\bigr)=0,
\]
where \(B(h)\) denotes external mixed/canonical fields.

For this to do real work, two things must both be true:

1. \(F\) must depend nontrivially on \(r\):
   \[
   \partial_r F\not\equiv 0;
   \]
2. the fields \(B(h)\) must be **independent of the same direct provenance slice** that produced \(r\).

Otherwise the identity is just a dressed-up tautology.

That is the first real filter.

---

## 2. Tautology obstruction

Suppose an external field \(B(h)\) has highest-new part already of the form
\[
B^{[1]}(h)=a(h)\,r(h),
\qquad a(h)\not\equiv 0.
\]
Then any pointwise identity
\[
F\bigl(h,B(h),r(h)\bigr)=0
\]
may reduce, at highest-new order, to an algebraic relation between \(a(h)r(h)\) and \(r(h)\). That does **not** determine \(r\); it may simply be an identity inside the same provenance slice.

So the first viable pointwise theorem has to involve a field whose leading relevant content is **not already just another scalar multiple of \(r\)**.

This is why merely finding another \(q^{(7)}\)-driven formula is not enough. It has to be a formula where the external field comes from a genuinely different slice of the mixed/canonical package.

So the sharpened target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find an external invariant whose highest-new slice is not proportional to }q^{(7)},
\textbf{ then derive a pointwise relation coupling it to }q^{(7)}.
}
\]

That is more precise than “look for a law involving \(q^{(7)}\).”

---

## 3. Best possible form of the next theorem

The cleanest useful theorem would look like this:

\[
\boxed{
a(h)\,q^{(7)}(h)+b(h)\,\Xi^{[1]}(h)+c(h)=0,
}
\]
where:

- \(a(h)\not\equiv 0\),
- \(\Xi^{[1]}(h)\) is the highest-new slice of some genuinely external mixed/canonical invariant,
- and \(c(h)\) depends only on already-controlled lower data.

Then one of two things happens.

If \(\Xi^{[1]}\) is already controlled elsewhere, you solve for \(q^{(7)}\) pointwise.

If not, you at least get a **coupled two-field system** for \((q^{(7)},\Xi^{[1]})\), which is still infinitely better than the current completely free scalar \(q^{(7)}\).

So the next live pointwise theorem is probably not a scalar law for \(q^{(7)}\) alone. It is more likely a **two-field coupling law** where one field is \(q^{(7)}\) and the other is a genuinely external highest-new mixed invariant.

That is the new tactical insight.

---

## 4. Why \(W^{[1]}\) is still a strong candidate

Earlier we reduced the direct-slice recurrence geometry to the affine-invariant curvature datum
\[
K=\phi_{\mu\mu}=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}.
\]

A pointwise law that determines \(W^{[1]}\) is already enough to determine the affine class of \(\phi\), which is exactly what the recurrence geometry sees.

So there are now really two pointwise targets:

### Target A
Determine \(q^{(7)}\) directly.

### Target B
Determine \(W^{[1]}\) directly.

And the useful observation is: **Target B is weaker, but may be easier**, because \(W^{[1]}\) is already affine-invariant and geometrically meaningful, while \(q^{(7)}\) is provenance-native but stronger than needed.

So the next actual theorem search should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{look for a pointwise external identity whose highest-new part isolates either }q^{(7)}\text{ or }W^{[1]}.
}
\]

That is now the sharpest pointwise attack.

---

## 5. What I would look for in the draft next

I would scan the mixed/canonical closure for formulas with this shape:

- an invariant whose highest-new part contains \(q^{(7)}\) with nonzero coefficient;
- or an invariant whose highest-new part contains \(W^{[1]}\) with nonzero coefficient;
- and whose remaining highest-new terms come from a different slice, not just another multiple of the same \(q^{(7)}\)-slice.

The moment I find something of the form
\[
\text{external highest-new field} = \text{known coefficient}\cdot q^{(7)} + \text{controlled lower terms},
\]
or similarly with \(W^{[1]}\), the direct-slice lane becomes live again.

If everything highest-new still collapses to the same direct provenance scalar, then pointwise laws are probably dead too, and only genuinely nonlocal laws remain.

---

## 6. So the next attack angle is now this

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for the first highest-new mixed/canonical field not proportional to the direct }q^{(7)}\text{-slice,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{and try to couple it pointwise to }q^{(7)}\text{ or }W^{[1]}.}
\]

That is the most precise next attack I can justify from where the paper now stands.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 07:28:01)

_Thought for 21s_

Here is the cleanest further push I can justify.

I think the next real theorem target is no longer “find some pointwise law involving \(q^{(7)}\).” It is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{find a rank-two highest-new coupling.}}
\]

That is the first pointwise mechanism that could actually beat the current universality.

---

## 1. Why a single highest-new scalar is not enough

Write
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,r(h).
\]

Everything so far says:

- any universal pointwise law of order \(\le 6\) is vacuous;
- any order-\(7\) law that only sees the same direct provenance slice is either tautological or just rewrites
  \[
  r=\phi/A;
  \]
- an order-\(8\) law is only a first-order ODE for \(r\) or \(\phi\), which is too weak in the abstract.

So the real obstacle is this:

> as long as the highest-new pointwise information is only one scalar multiple of \(r\), there is still only **one degree of highest-new freedom**, and nothing forces a contradiction.

That suggests the next viable pointwise theorem must produce **two independent highest-new scalars**.

---

## 2. The exact algebraic shape you need

Suppose you could find two external mixed/canonical invariants
\[
\Xi_1,\qquad \Xi_2
\]
whose highest-new parts on a generic one-pair block satisfy
\[
\Xi_1^{[1]}=a_1(h)\,r(h)+b_1(h),
\]
\[
\Xi_2^{[1]}=a_2(h)\,r(h)+b_2(h),
\]
where the \(b_i(h)\) are already controlled by previously closed lower/mixed packages.

If both equations involve the **same** unknown \(r(h)\), then by themselves they are still just two expressions for one scalar. The only way they become useful is if they come with an additional compatibility requirement that is not automatic.

The clean algebraic way to say this is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{you need a nontrivial \(2\times 2\) highest-new system with nonzero determinant.}
}
\]

For example, if instead you can isolate **two unknown highest-new fields**
\[
r(h),\qquad s(h),
\]
with relations
\[
\Xi_1^{[1]}=a_{11}(h)r(h)+a_{12}(h)s(h)+b_1(h),
\]
\[
\Xi_2^{[1]}=a_{21}(h)r(h)+a_{22}(h)s(h)+b_2(h),
\]
and
\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
a_{11}(h) & a_{12}(h)\\
a_{21}(h) & a_{22}(h)
\end{pmatrix}
\not\equiv 0,
\]
then the highest-new slice is no longer one-dimensional. Now the pointwise package could actually solve for \((r,s)\), or impose a nontrivial compatibility law.

That is the first pointwise structure not already killed by the current no-go results.

---

## 3. Why this is better than “determine \(q^{(7)}\) directly”

Earlier we said the best case would be a pointwise order-\(7\) law determining
\[
r(h)=q^{(7)}(h).
\]

That is still true, but it is very strong. The new reduction says you may not need that. It would already be enough to find:

- one genuinely external highest-new field \(s(h)\),
- and two pointwise identities coupling \(r\) and \(s\),
- with nondegenerate coefficient matrix.

Then the one-dimensional freedom of the direct provenance slice is gone.

So the next theorem target is more realistic:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find the first highest-new mixed/canonical field independent of the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice, and couple it pointwise to that slice.}
}
\]

That is more precise than “look for a law for \(q^{(7)}\).”

---

## 4. The strongest abstract no-go I can add

This also yields a clean negative statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any pointwise theorem whose highest-new content is still rank one in the direct provenance variable \(r\) is too weak.}
}
\]

In plain language:

- if every highest-new scalar in the mixed/canonical package is still just “coefficient \(\times r\)” plus controlled lower terms,
- then all pointwise pointwise laws are still effectively scalar,
- and the direct-slice universality is not broken.

So the next actual test is a **rank test** on the highest-new slice.

That is a very useful filter for the draft:

> scan the mixed/canonical identities and ask whether any highest-new field is genuinely independent of the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice.

If the answer is no, then pointwise attacks are dead and only nonlocal laws remain.

If the answer is yes, that is the first place the pointwise lane becomes live again.

---

## 5. The most plausible candidates

Given where the draft stands, the best candidates are not arbitrary quotient-septic objects. Those were too free. The likely candidates are:

- a highest-new mixed scalar coming from the now-closed mixed \(4\)-point lane,
- or a higher canonical field whose direct slice is **not** proportional to \(q^{(7)}\),
- or a pointwise curvature-side scalar involving \(W^{[1]}\) if its highest-new source is genuinely different from the direct slice.

So the next concrete attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{perform a highest-new rank audit of the mixed/canonical package.}
}
\]

Concretely:

1. list the pointwise highest-new scalars now available in the paper;
2. express each on a generic one-pair block in terms of the direct provenance variable \(r=q^{(7)}\) and any other candidate highest-new fields;
3. check whether the resulting coefficient matrix has rank \(1\) or \(>1\).

That is the cleanest next attack I can see.

---

## 6. The sharp strategic update

So the next live pointwise question is no longer:

\[
\text{“can we determine }q^{(7)}\text{?”}
\]

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{“does the mixed/canonical package contain a second independent highest-new scalar, so that the pointwise highest-new system has rank }2\text{?”}
}
\]

If yes, pointwise hybridization is alive again.  
If no, then the whole pointwise lane is essentially spent, and only genuinely nonlocal laws remain.

That is where I would push next.

_Thought for 32s_

I can push it one more step, and it sharpens the bookkeeping a lot.

The right next theorem is not just “find a second highest-new scalar.” It is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{rank two is necessary but not sufficient.}}
\]

What actually matters is the **defect rank**: the number of unresolved highest-new provenance fields that remain free on a generic block.

Right now the direct lane has one explicit free field
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad
\phi=A\,r.
\]

If the mixed/canonical package contributes a second highest-new scalar \(s(h)\), that helps only if \(s\) is either:

- already controlled by previously closed structure, or
- enters an overdetermined pointwise system.

Otherwise you have just replaced one arbitrary function by two arbitrary functions.

That is the new clean reduction.

---

## 1. General defect-rank principle

Suppose on a generic block the unresolved highest-new fields are
\[
u_1(h),\dots,u_d(h).
\]
A pointwise hybrid theorem gives \(m\) relations
\[
F_j\bigl(h,\text{controlled lower data},u_1,\dots,u_d\bigr)=0,
\qquad j=1,\dots,m.
\]

Linearizing at highest-new order, this becomes a coefficient matrix
\[
\mathsf A(h)\in M_{m\times d}.
\]

Then:

- if
  \[
  \operatorname{rank}\mathsf A(h)<d,
  \]
  at least one highest-new direction remains free;

- if
  \[
  \operatorname{rank}\mathsf A(h)=d,
  \]
  the system can in principle solve pointwise for all unresolved highest-new fields.

So the real target is not “rank two.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{full defect-rank closure: }
\operatorname{rank}\mathsf A = d.
}
\]

That is the exact algebraic condition for a pointwise hybrid route to become viable.

---

## 2. Consequence for the current lane

At the moment, the direct-slice package gives one explicit unresolved field
\[
r=q^{(7)}.
\]

If the mixed closure contributes one more independent highest-new scalar \(s\), then the defect rank becomes at least
\[
d=2
\]
unless \(s\) is already controlled elsewhere.

So a single extra pointwise identity is not enough. Even two identities are only useful if together they have full rank and the remaining quantities are lower/known.

This gives the sharper test:

> whenever you inspect a candidate mixed/canonical identity, ask:
> is it reducing the unresolved highest-new defect rank, or just introducing another highest-new field?

That is the right audit now.

---

## 3. Mixed 4-point closure helps, but only by pruning one kind of “fake” extra field

The new mixed \(4\)-point closure is useful here.

It says there is no new sufficiently small nearby genuinely new mixed \(4\)-point obstruction near the centered base. In rank language, that means:

\[
\boxed{
\text{mixed local closure removes the hope of a new purely local mixed highest-new field born at the centered base.}
}
\]

So if a second highest-new scalar exists, it probably has to come from:

- deeper canonical/multi-pair structure, or
- a genuinely global/nonlocal mixed field,

not from a new local mixed \(4\)-point jet phenomenon.

That is a real pruning of the search space.

---

## 4. Stronger no-go: rank-one pointwise hybridization is dead

This gives a clean theorem.

If every pointwise highest-new scalar available on a generic block is of the form
\[
\Xi^{[1]}(h)=a(h)\,r(h)+b(h),
\]
with \(b(h)\) controlled by previously closed lower data, then the pointwise highest-new system is rank one. In that case:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{all pointwise hybridization is dead.}
}
\]

Because every pointwise law still reduces to a scalar relation in the one free field \(r\), and we already know a single scalar pointwise law must either be tautological or else directly determine \(r\).

So the next concrete attack is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{highest-new defect-rank audit of the post-mixed-closure package.}
}
\]

Not just “is there another scalar?” but:

- what are the unresolved highest-new fields after the mixed closure,
- and does the available pointwise system have full rank on them?

That is the best next abstraction.

---

## 5. The best-case scenario

The ideal outcome would be:

1. mixed/canonical closure leaves exactly one unresolved highest-new provenance field \(r=q^{(7)}\);
2. one new pointwise external identity survives with nonzero coefficient of \(r\).

Then pointwise closure is back.

The next-best outcome would be:

1. mixed/canonical closure leaves two unresolved highest-new fields \(r,s\);
2. there are two independent pointwise identities with full-rank coefficient matrix.

Then pointwise closure is also back.

Anything less is still too weak.

So the next live theorem target is very precise:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that the mixed/canonical package has full highest-new rank on the remaining unresolved provenance fields.}
}
\]

---

## 6. The practical next attack

I would now do this exact audit:

Let \(\mathcal U\) be the list of highest-new fields still not fixed by lower route \(2\) plus mixed local closure.

For each candidate invariant \(\Xi\) in the mixed/canonical package, compute its highest-new slice
\[
\Xi^{[1]} = \sum_{u\in\mathcal U} a_{\Xi,u}(h)\,u(h)+b_\Xi(h),
\]
where \(b_\Xi\) is already controlled.

Then form the coefficient matrix
\[
\mathsf A(h)=\bigl(a_{\Xi,u}(h)\bigr).
\]

The exact question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does }\mathsf A(h)\text{ have full column rank on a generic block?}
}
\]

If yes, pointwise hybridization is alive again.  
If no, then the whole pointwise lane is probably spent, and only genuinely nonlocal laws remain.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

I think the right next push is to refine the “rank audit” itself. The real issue is not just algebraic rank of highest-new fields at a point, but whether they represent more than one arbitrary function of \(h\).

_Thought for 1m 22s_

Here is the strongest clean continuation I can justify.

The right refinement is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{the true highest-new defect is differential rank }1,\textbf{ not algebraic rank }1.}
\]

That is better than the earlier “look for a second highest-new scalar.”

Why this matters: even if you find several pointwise highest-new scalars, they may all still be just **differential consequences of one free function**
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h).
\]
In that case, the pointwise lane is still effectively one-function free, and an algebraic rank audit is misleading.

---

## 1. The direct-slice package is generated by one free function \(r\)

On a generic one-pair block \(J\), write
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h),
\qquad
\phi(h)=A(h)\,r(h),
\qquad
A(h)\neq 0.
\]

Also
\[
\mu'=\frac{d\mu}{dh}=-\frac{M}{v_5^2},
\qquad
D_\mu:=\frac{d}{d\mu}=-\frac{v_5^2}{M}\frac{d}{dh}.
\]

Then the whole direct highest-new geometry is obtained by applying the single operator \(D_\mu\) to \(Ar\):

\[
\phi = Ar,
\]
\[
\beta^{[1]}=\phi_\mu=D_\mu(Ar),
\]
\[
m^{[1]}=\phi-\mu\phi_\mu=(1-\mu D_\mu)(Ar),
\]
and
\[
\phi_{\mu\mu}=D_\mu^2(Ar)=\frac{v_5^3}{M^3}W^{[1]}.
\]

So equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
W^{[1]}=\frac{M^3}{v_5^3}D_\mu^2(Ar).
}
\]

Thus every currently available direct-slice highest-new scalar is in the differential algebra generated by the single function \(r\) over the coefficient field of lower data.

That is the first key theorem.

---

## 2. Consequence: the pointwise highest-new defect rank is \(1\)

This means:

- \(\phi\) is not an independent highest-new field;
- \(\beta^{[1]}\) is not independent;
- \(m^{[1]}\) is not independent;
- \(W^{[1]}\) is not independent.

They are all just different differential projections of the same free function \(r\).

So the right notion is not “how many scalars do we see?” but:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{how many arbitrary functions of \(h\) remain free at highest-new order?}
}
\]

At the current direct-slice level, the answer is still exactly one.

So the current package has **differential defect rank one**.

That is sharper than anything we had before.

---

## 3. Why the previous “rank-two” hope is not enough

Suppose you find two pointwise highest-new scalars
\[
\Xi_1^{[1]},\qquad \Xi_2^{[1]}.
\]
If both lie in the differential algebra generated by \(r\), then a pointwise system
\[
F_1(h,\Xi_1^{[1]},\Xi_2^{[1]})=0,
\qquad
F_2(h,\Xi_1^{[1]},\Xi_2^{[1]})=0
\]
does **not** automatically give two independent unknowns. It may still just be a system of differential equations for the same one function \(r\).

So algebraic rank \(2\) among displayed scalars is not the real target.

The real target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a highest-new field not contained in the differential algebra generated by }r.
}
\]

Only then does the defect rank actually go up beyond one, and only then can a pointwise hybrid system become genuinely overdetermined.

That is the key structural correction.

---

## 4. Mixed \(4\)-point closure helps here too

The new mixed \(4\)-point closure says there is no new sufficiently small local mixed obstruction near the centered base.

Translated into this language:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no obvious new local mixed highest-new field born near the centered base that could raise the defect rank above }1.
}
\]

So if a second genuinely independent highest-new field exists at all, it probably has to come from:

- deeper canonical/multi-pair structure, or
- a genuinely global/nonlocal mixed field,

not from a new local mixed jet phenomenon.

That is a useful pruning of the search space.

---

## 5. The sharp new theorem target

So the next attack is no longer:

\[
\text{“find another scalar involving }q^{(7)}\text{ or }W^{[1]}.”}
\]

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find the first highest-new field not in the differential closure of }r.
}
\]

More formally, let \(\mathcal K\) be the coefficient field generated by the already-controlled lower data on a generic block. Then the current direct-slice highest-new package lives in
\[
\mathcal K\langle r\rangle_{\text{diff}},
\]
the differential algebra generated by one free function \(r\).

The next viable pointwise theorem must produce a field
\[
s(h)
\]
whose highest-new part is not in \(\mathcal K\langle r\rangle_{\text{diff}}\).

That is the first thing that could actually increase the differential defect rank.

---

## 6. What this means strategically

This gives a much sharper filter for the next search.

When inspecting any mixed/canonical invariant \(\Xi\), do **not** ask merely:

> does \(\Xi^{[1]}\) involve \(q^{(7)}\)?

Ask instead:

> is \(\Xi^{[1]}\) already a differential-algebraic function of \(r\), or does it introduce a genuinely new highest-new function?

If it is already in the differential closure of \(r\), it does not help the pointwise lane.

If it is not, then the pointwise lane is alive again.

So the sharp strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the next live pointwise target is the first highest-new field outside the differential algebra generated by }r=q^{(7)}.
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## 1. The provenance tower collapses to one generator

Set
\[
r(h):=q^{(7)}(h).
\]

Then for every \(m\ge 7\),
\[
q^{(m)}(h)=\frac{d^{\,m-7}}{dh^{\,m-7}}\,r(h).
\]

So any pointwise highest-new field whose leading piece is built from finitely many \(h\)-derivatives of \(q\),
\[
\Xi^{[1]}(h)=F\bigl(h,\text{lower data},q^{(7)}(h),q^{(8)}(h),\dots,q^{(m)}(h)\bigr),

automatically lies in the differential algebra
\[

where \(\mathcal K\) is the coefficient field generated by the already-controlled lower data.

Equivalently:

\[

\]

That is the key sharpening.

---

## 2. Consequence: no same-tower pointwise hybrid can increase the defect rank

Suppose you find several pointwise highest-new scalars
\[
\Xi_1^{[1]},\dots,\Xi_N^{[1]}.
\]
If all of them are built from the same provenance tower of \(q\), then after reduction they all have the form
\[
\Xi_j^{[1]}=\mathcal F_j\bigl(h,\text{lower data},r,r',\dots,r^{(m_j)}\bigr).
\]

So even if there are many displayed scalars, the unresolved highest-new content is still just one arbitrary function \(r\).

Therefore the true pointwise defect rank stays
\[
1.
\]

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a highest-new field not generated by the same }q\textbf{-tower.}
}
\]

Only that can raise the differential defect rank above one.

---

## 3. Stronger no-go

This gives a clean no-go theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{any pointwise hybrid theorem whose highest-new content is built only from finitely many \(h\)-derivatives of the same provenance function }q
\textbf{ is still effectively one-function free.}
}
\]

So such a theorem cannot beat the universality by rank alone.

In plain language:

- \(q^{(7)}\) does not help by itself;
- \(q^{(8)}\), \(q^{(9)}\), and higher same-tower derivatives do not introduce new highest-new degrees of freedom;
- any pointwise law among them is still just a differential equation for one arbitrary function \(r\).

That may still be useful if the equation is very strong, but it does **not** create a new independent scalar.

So the next pointwise attack must either:

1. find a genuinely external highest-new field outside the \(q\)-tower, or  
2. find a very strong differential equation for \(r\) itself, strong enough to kill the direct-slice geometry.

---

## 4. Mixed \(4\)-point closure narrows the first option

The new mixed \(4\)-point closure helps here in exactly one way:

it removes the hope that a new **local mixed** highest-new field is born near the centered base and gives you the needed second generator.

So if an external field exists outside the \(q\)-tower, it is likely to come from:

- deeper canonical / multi-pair structure, or
- a genuinely nonlocal mixed object,

not from a new local mixed jet.

That is a useful pruning of the search space.

---

## 5. The next two live attacks

This leaves exactly two meaningful directions.

### Attack A: same-tower but very strong
Try to derive a pointwise or global differential equation for
\[
r=q^{(7)}
\]
that is strong enough by itself to kill recurrence. Not just “some law,” but something like:

- algebraic determination of \(r\),
- a first-order ODE for \(r\) with sign/monotonicity structure,
- or a nonlocal monotonicity law for \(r\).

### Attack B: genuinely new generator
Find a highest-new field
\[
s(h)
\]
not belonging to
\[
\mathcal K\langle r\rangle_{\mathrm{diff}}.
\]
Then the pointwise highest-new defect rank becomes at least \(2\), and a coupled system may become overdetermined.

That is the cleanest dichotomy now.

---

## 6. The next concrete theorem to hunt

So the sharpest next target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that every currently available highest-new field is in }\mathcal K\langle r\rangle_{\mathrm{diff}},
}
\]
or else exhibit the first one that is not.

That is the natural “rank audit” theorem.

If the audit comes out positive — everything stays in the \(q\)-tower — then the whole pointwise hybrid lane collapses to studying one function \(r\), and only a genuinely strong ODE / integral law for \(r\) can help.

If the audit comes out negative — there is a field outside the tower — then pointwise hybridization is alive again.

So my next attack would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{do a same-tower vs new-generator audit of the highest-new mixed/canonical fields.}
}

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

Let \(\mathcal K\) be the differential field generated by the already-controlled lower data on a generic one-pair block \(J\). Set
\[
r:=q^{(7)}.
\]
Then for every \(m\ge 7\),
\[
q^{(m)}=r^{(m-7)}.
\]

So any pointwise expression formed from:

- \(h\),
- lower-data coefficients in \(\mathcal K\),
- finitely many derivatives \(q^{(7)},q^{(8)},\dots,q^{(m)}\),

belongs to the differential algebra
\[

}
\]

So even if you display many different highest-new scalars, if they are all same-tower, you have not escaped the universal one-function freedom. You have only written different differential shadows of the same arbitrary function \(r\).

In particular:

- \(\beta^{[1]}=D_\mu(Ar)\),

Produce a highest-new field

\[

So if a field outside the \(q\)-tower exists, it is likely to come from:

- deeper canonical/multi-pair structure, or
- a genuinely nonlocal mixed object,

not from a new local mixed jet.

That is a genuine narrowing of the search.

\mathcal K\langle r\rangle_{\mathrm{diff}}.

\[
r=q^{(7)}.

This is true whether \(F\) is linear or nonlinear, because
\[
q^{(m)}=r^{(m-7)} \qquad (m\ge 7).
\]

So every such invariant is still only a differential-algebraic shadow of the same one arbitrary function \(r\).

That means:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no finite-order pointwise invariant built from the current provenance tower can create a second generator.}
}
\]

This is stronger than the earlier “rank-one” remarks. It says the entire pointwise tower is closed.

---

## 2. Consequence

There are now only two mathematically live possibilities:

### A. A genuinely strong equation for \(r\) itself
Something like
\[
r=H(h,\text{known data}),
\]
or a first-order ODE / integro-differential law with real sign or monotonicity consequences.

### B. A genuinely nonlocal invariant
Something depending on \(r\) over an interval or pair of points, not reducible to finitely many pointwise jets.

Because every finite-order pointwise quantity is already same-tower.

So the next attack should not be another pointwise rank audit. That audit is now essentially done abstractly.

---

## 3. What kind of nonlocal invariant could still matter

The most plausible next object is a **bilinear concomitant** or **energy-type pairing** built from \(r\) on an interval.

The reason is that all the bad direct-slice geometries were reduced to exact balance conditions:

- zero-mean slope-deviation,
- primitive-area balance,
- split-point barycenter balance.

Those are all inherently **integral** statements.

So the next theorem should probably also be integral.

The best abstract target I can see is something like:

\[
\mathcal E_{[a,b]}[r]
=
\int_a^b\!\!\int_a^b K(h,s)\,r(h)r(s)\,dh\,ds
\]
or
\[
\mathcal V_{[a,b]}[r]
=
\int_a^b L(h)\,r(h)\,dh,
\]
with one of these properties:

- sign-definite on actual RH provenance,
- monotone in \(b\),
- or incompatible with the zero-area / barycenter-balance conditions needed for recurrence.

That is now the right class of theorem.

---

## 4. Why this is the natural next move

All the recurrence obstructions we derived ultimately became **integral equalities** for the affine-invariant curvature datum
\[
K(\mu)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(\mu)=\frac{v_5(\mu)^3}{M(\mu)^3}W^{[1]}(\mu),
\]
or equivalently for the slope-deviation profile
\[
g(\mu)=\phi_\mu(\mu)-s.
\]

And since
\[
\phi=A\,r,
\]
these are integral conditions on \(r\).

So the next useful provenance theorem should itself be an integral law for \(r\), not another pointwise one.

That is the main conceptual shift.

---

## 5. The best concrete next attack

I would now attack exactly this theorem shape:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{nonlocal quadratic/energy law for }r=q^{(7)}.
}
\]

A model statement would be:

> On each relevant \(h\)-interval, the actual RH provenance satisfies
> \[
> \mathcal E'[r](h)\ge 0
> \quad\text{or}\quad
> \mathcal E[r]\ge 0
> \]
> for some explicit energy \(\mathcal E\), and a candidate direct-slice recurrence would force the wrong sign or equality case.

Why quadratic/energy? Because bilinear and quadratic functionals are the first natural nonlocal objects not already killed by the one-function pointwise freedom.

---

## 6. If you want the sharpest formulation of the next theorem target

It is this:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find a nonlocal invariant of the provenance field }r=q^{(7)}
\textbf{ whose sign or monotonicity contradicts the direct-slice balance conditions.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

## 1. Rewrite recurrence directly as finite linear conditions on \(r\)

On a generic block let
\[
\phi(h)=A(h)\,r(h),\qquad A(h)\neq 0,
\]
and let \(\mu(h)\) be the block coordinate with \(\mu'(h)\neq 0\).

A common tangent line
\[
y=\ell(\mu)=s\mu+b
\]
at two points \(h_1\neq h_2\) on the block is exactly:

\[
\phi(h_j)=\ell(\mu(h_j))
\qquad (j=1,2),
\]
and
\[
\phi_\mu(h_j)=s
\qquad (j=1,2).
\]

Since \(\phi=A r\), this becomes the four linear jet conditions
\[
A(h_j)\,r(h_j)=\ell(\mu(h_j)),
\qquad j=1,2,
\]
and
\[
A'(h_j)\,r(h_j)+A(h_j)\,r'(h_j)=s\,\mu'(h_j),
\qquad j=1,2.
\]

So recurrence on one chosen block imposes only **four linear point conditions** on the gauge-free field \(r\).

That is the key simplification.

---

## 2. Finite linear observations of \(r\) are still too weak

Now let
\[
\Lambda_1,\dots,\Lambda_N
\]
be any finite family of linear functionals on analytic functions on a finite union of blocks \(U\). These may be:

- endpoint jets,
- boundary transfer jets,
- linear integral functionals,
- finite-codimension linear matching laws,
- or any other finite list of linear observations.

Fix compatible target values
\[
\Lambda_i(r)=c_i,\qquad i=1,\dots,N,
\]
and fix the four tangency conditions above on one chosen block.

Then for polynomials \(r\) of sufficiently large degree, all of these are just a finite linear system on the polynomial coefficients. Hence, whenever the target data are compatible, there exists a polynomial \(r\) satisfying them all simultaneously.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no theorem that constrains provenance only through finitely many linear functionals of }r
\text{ can exclude direct-slice recurrence.}
}
\]

This is now the clean gauge-free version of the earlier universality results.

---

## 3. What this rules out

This kills, in one shot:

- finite jet matching for \(q\),
- finite jet matching for \(r\),
- finite-codimension linear transfer across turning boundaries,
- finitely many linear moment conditions,
- and any theorem whose effective content is “\(r\) lies in the fiber of a finite-dimensional linear observation map.”

So the next live theorem cannot be of that type.

---

## 4. The exact remaining possibilities

After this, there are really only two mathematically live kinds of provenance theorem left for \(r\):

### A. An infinite-codimension linear law
For example:
\[
\mathcal L r = f
\]
on a whole interval, where \(\mathcal L\) is a genuine ODE / Volterra / Fredholm operator, not reducible to finitely many observations.

### B. A genuinely nonlinear law
For example:
\[
\mathcal E[r](h)\ge 0,\qquad \frac{d}{dh}\mathcal E[r](h)\ge 0,
\]
or some nonlocal nonlinear comparison principle.

Everything weaker is now spent.

---

## 5. The sharp next attack

So the next attack I would actually make is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for the first provenance theorem whose solution set for }r
\textbf{ is not a finite-codimension affine subspace.}
}
\]

That means either:

- a genuine intervalwise operator equation for \(r\), or
- a nonlinear energy/monotonicity law for \(r\).

And because mixed local \(4\)-point has closed, that theorem cannot be a new small local mixed rescue. It has to be genuinely global/nonlocal or deeper canonical.

So the strategic update is:

\[

}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and any theorem about }r\text{ must be infinite-codimension linear or genuinely nonlinear to matter.}
}
\]

That is where I would push next.

\[
X(-t)=X(t)
\quad\text{or}\quad
X(-t)=-X(t).
\]

If \(X\) is odd, then on the residual slice it vanishes to one extra order at \(t=0\), and that often kills the quartic–sextic model or collapses it to the diagonal.

If \(X\) is even, then it descends to
\[
X(t)=\widetilde X(t^2),
\]
so any residual branch equation becomes a one-variable quotient equation in \(u=t^2\). That is dramatically more rigid than a general two-sided overlap problem.

So the exact theorem I would now hunt is:

## Assistant (2026-04-21 11:52:57)

_Thought for 53s_

Yes. I think there is a real new structural theorem here.

The \(K_v=0\) exact fixed-shear residue does not just “descend to the quotient.” In the surviving quartic–sextic case, it descends to a **one-sided cusp**, and that cusp is **locally sterile** for any new two-point slope/tangency recurrence.

That is the cleanest further push I can justify.

---

## 1. Quotient reduction of the even/even residue

On the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) slice, the remaining mixed residue is now of the form
\[
\Phi(t)=(t^m,G(t)),
\qquad m \text{ even},
\qquad G(-t)=G(t).
\]
So after passing to the involution quotient
\[
u=t^2\ge 0,
\]
it becomes a one-sided analytic germ
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{quot}}(u)=\bigl(u^{m/2},\,\widetilde G(u)\bigr).
\]

In the surviving low-order case \((4,6)\), this is
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{quot}}(u)=\bigl(u^2,\widetilde G(u)\bigr),
\qquad
\widetilde G(u)=c\,u^3+O(u^4),
\qquad c\neq 0.
\]

So the residual local model is not a two-sided crossing at all. It is a **one-sided semicubical cusp-type germ**.

That is already a major simplification.

---

## 2. The quotient cusp is locally sterile

Now take a general one-sided cusp germ
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),
\qquad
\widetilde G(u)=c\,u^3+O(u^4),
\qquad c\neq 0,
\qquad u\ge 0.
\]

There are three natural local slope functions to test:

### (a) Secant slope from the cusp point
\[
S(u):=\frac{y(u)}{x(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G(u)}{u^2}=c\,u+O(u^2).
\]
Hence
\[
S'(0)=c\neq 0.
\]
So after shrinking to \(0<u<\varepsilon\), \(S\) is strictly monotone.

Therefore there are **no distinct nearby points**
\[
0<u_1<u_2<\varepsilon
\]
with the same secant slope from the cusp point.

### (b) Tangent slope at a point
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}
=
\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u}.
\]
Since
\[
\widetilde G'(u)=3c\,u^2+O(u^3),
\]
we get
\[
T(u)=\frac{3}{2}c\,u+O(u^2),
\qquad
T'(0)=\frac{3}{2}c\neq 0.
\]
So \(T\) is also strictly monotone on a small one-sided neighborhood.

Therefore there are **no distinct nearby points**
\[
0<u_1<u_2<\varepsilon
\]
with the same tangent slope.

### (c) Tangent-at-\(u\) passing through another nearby point
Define
\[
H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)\bigl(x(v)-x(u)\bigr),
\qquad 0<u<v.
\]
For the pure model \(y=c\,u^3\),
\[
H(u,v)=c\,(v-u)^2\!\left(v+\frac{u}{2}\right),
\]
so for \(c\neq 0\),
\[
\operatorname{sgn}H(u,v)=\operatorname{sgn}c
\qquad (0<u<v).
\]
For the perturbed germ \(y=c\,u^3+O(u^4)\), the same sign persists after shrinking the neighborhood.

So the tangent at a nearby point does **not** pass through another nearby point of the branch.

---

## 3. Structural consequence

So the quotient cusp satisfies:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no nearby repeated secant slope, no nearby repeated tangent slope, and no nearby tangent-through-second-point event.}
}
\]

In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) quotient germ is locally sterile for new two-point overlap/tangency recurrence.}
}
\]

That is a real theorem.

And it is stronger than just saying “the residue descends.” It says the descended local model itself cannot generate the kind of nearby two-point geometry one would need for a new overlap branch.

---

## 4. What this means for the residual lane

This is the key implication:

If the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) residue is genuinely responsible for any surviving mixed obstruction, that obstruction cannot be born from **local** two-point geometry near the quotient cusp.

So any surviving obstruction on that lane must be:

- either genuinely **nonlocal** along the quotient branch,
- or dependent on some external provenance/canonical variable not visible in the bare quotient cusp.

That matches the broader pattern we’ve been seeing: every purely local lane keeps collapsing to sterility or universality.

So the next live theorem target on this branch becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{global/nonlocal return on the quotient cusp branch is impossible.}
}
\]

Not local recurrence. We have now killed that.

---

## 5. The sharp next attack

So my next attack from here would be:

### Attack target
Prove that the one-sided quotient branch
\[
u\mapsto (u^2,\widetilde G(u))
\]
cannot support a **nonlocal** return of the relevant fixed-shear slope/tangency datum.

Concretely, that means hunting a theorem of one of these forms:

- the quotient secant-slope function from the cusp point is globally one-to-one on the relevant branch;
- or the quotient tangent-slope function is globally one-to-one;
- or the tangent-at-\(u\) function has a definite one-sided sign against later points on the branch;
- or the external provenance law forces \(\widetilde G\) to stay in the local sterile regime globally enough to prevent return.

That is now a much more focused and plausible target than anything involving the original two-sided \(t\)-germ.

So the real update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ residue has reduced to a one-sided quotient cusp, and that cusp is locally sterile.}
}
\]

That is the strongest clean continuation I can justify right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 11:59:00)

_Thought for 1m 32s_

I found a cleaner reduction on the quotient cusp branch.

Once you pass to the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) quotient germ, the two-point geometry is controlled not directly by
\[
K(u)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u),
\]
but by two **cumulative moment functions** of \(K\).

This is better than the earlier barycenter/primitive formulas.

---

Assume the residual quotient germ is written as
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),
\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
with the quartic–sextic local model
\[
\widetilde G(u)=c\,u^3+O(u^4),\qquad c\neq 0.
\]

Define the tangent slope and cusp-secant slope:
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},
\qquad
S(u):=\frac{y(u)}{x(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G(u)}{u^2}.
\]

Let
\[
K(u):=\frac{dT}{dx}(u)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u).
\]
Since \(x=u^2\), one has
\[
\frac{d}{du}T(u)=2u\,K(u).
\]

Now define the cumulative moments
\[
M_1(u):=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds,
\qquad
M_3(u):=\int_0^u s^3\,K(s)\,ds.
\]

Then the exact identities are:

\[
\boxed{
T(u)=2\,M_1(u),
}
\]
because \(T(0)=0\) for the cusp model,

and, setting
\[
D(u):=T(u)-S(u),
\]
one gets
\[
\boxed{
D(u)=\frac{2}{u^2}\,M_3(u).
}
\]

The second identity comes from
\[
S'(u)=\frac{2}{u}(T(u)-S(u))=\frac{2}{u}D(u),
\]
hence
\[
D'(u)=T'(u)-S'(u)=2uK(u)-\frac{2}{u}D(u),
\]
so
\[
(u^2D(u))'=2u^3K(u),
\]
and since \(D(0)=0\),
\[
u^2D(u)=2\int_0^u s^3K(s)\,ds.
\]

These formulas are the new clean reduction.

---

### Exact consequences

1. **Repeated tangent slope** on the quotient branch is equivalent to
\[
T(u_1)=T(u_2)
\iff
M_1(u_1)=M_1(u_2).
\]
So a nonlocal repeated tangent slope is exactly a return of the first cumulative moment \(M_1\).

2. **Repeated cusp-secant slope** is governed by \(D=T-S\). Since
\[
S'(u)=\frac{2}{u}D(u)=\frac{4}{u^3}M_3(u),
\]
the secant slope is strictly monotone as long as \(M_3\) keeps one sign. So a nonlocal repeated secant slope requires a zero of \(M_3\).

3. **Tangent at one point passing through another point** reduces to repeated tangent slope. Indeed
\[
H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)\bigl(x(v)-x(u)\bigr)
\]
satisfies
\[
\partial_v H(u,v)=x'(v)\bigl(T(v)-T(u)\bigr)=2v\bigl(T(v)-T(u)\bigr).
\]
So if \(H(u,v)=0\) for some \(v>u\), Rolle gives \(\xi\in(u,v)\) with
\[
T(\xi)=T(u),
\]
hence a repeated tangent slope, i.e. a return of \(M_1\).

So the whole quotient-cusp recurrence problem reduces to these two scalar cumulative moments:

\[
\boxed{
\text{tangent return } \Longleftrightarrow M_1 \text{ repeats a value;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{secant-slope return } \Longleftrightarrow M_3 \text{ changes sign / hits }0.
}
\]

And if \(K\) has one sign on the branch, then:

- \(M_1\) is strictly monotone,
- \(M_3\) has one sign,
- therefore \(T\) and \(S\) are both strictly monotone,
- and the quotient cusp is globally sterile for all these two-point returns.

---

### Why this is a better target

This tells you the next theorem does **not** need full control of \(K\) pointwise.

It is enough to control the two cumulative moments
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds,
\qquad
M_3(u)=\int_0^u s^3K(s)\,ds.
\]

So the next live theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance-driven sign/monotonicity law for }M_1\textbf{ and }M_3
\textbf{ on the quotient cusp branch.}
}
\]

Concretely, any one of the following would close this lane:

- \(M_1\) is strictly monotone on the relevant quotient branch;
- \(M_3\) keeps one sign on the relevant quotient branch;
- or a one-sided comparison between \(M_1\) and \(M_3\) that prevents the needed returns.

That is sharper than targeting \(K\) directly, and much sharper than the earlier primitive-area balance.

So my next attack would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{mine the external mixed/canonical structure for a law on the quotient cusp branch that forces }M_1\text{ monotone and }M_3\text{ one-signed.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify now.

_Thought for 33s_

I can sharpen it one more step.

On the quotient cusp branch, the residual two-point geometry is controlled by **two cumulative moments of \(K\)**, and one of them alone governs tangent-return.

Let
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
and define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},
\qquad
S(u):=\frac{y(u)}{x(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G(u)}{u^2},
\]
and
\[
K(u):=\frac{dT}{dx}(u)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u).
\]
Since \(x=u^2\),
\[
\frac{dT}{du}=2u\,K(u).
\]

Now set
\[
M_1(u):=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds,
\qquad
M_3(u):=\int_0^u s^3\,K(s)\,ds.
\]

Then the exact formulas are
\[
\boxed{T(u)=2M_1(u)}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{T(u)-S(u)=\frac{2}{u^2}M_3(u)}.
\]

So equivalently
\[
\boxed{S(u)=2M_1(u)-\frac{2}{u^2}M_3(u).}
\]

This is already a much cleaner reduction than the earlier primitive-area language.

Now there are three exact consequences.

First, **repeated tangent slope** is exactly
\[
T(u_1)=T(u_2)
\iff
M_1(u_1)=M_1(u_2).
\]
So the tangent-slope return problem is nothing but return of the single scalar cumulative moment \(M_1\).

Second, since
\[
S'(u)=\frac{4}{u^3}M_3(u),
\]
the cusp-secant slope is strictly monotone whenever \(M_3\) keeps one sign. So **secant return from the cusp point** can happen only if \(M_3\) changes sign or vanishes.

Third, the tangent-at-\(u\) passing through a later point \(v>u\) is governed by
\[
H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)(x(v)-x(u)).
\]
Using \(x'(s)=2s\) and \(T=2M_1\),
\[
H(u,v)
=
\int_u^v 2s\,(T(s)-T(u))\,ds
=
4\int_u^v s\,(M_1(s)-M_1(u))\,ds.
\]
So if \(M_1\) is strictly monotone, then \(H(u,v)\) has fixed sign for every \(v>u\), and the tangent at \(u\) never passes through a later point.

So the whole quotient-cusp branch is globally sterile if you can prove:

\[
\boxed{
M_1 \text{ is strictly monotone and } M_3 \text{ has one sign on the relevant branch.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest theorem target I see now.

It is also the minimal useful target. You do not need full control of \(K\), and you do not need to solve for \(\widetilde G\). It is enough to control these two weighted cumulative moments.

There is one more honest refinement: abstractly, this is still not enough to close the lane without provenance input. You can build \(K\) with \(+,-,+\) sign pattern so that \(M_1\) has a return and \(M_3\) changes sign, and then all the bad quotient-cusp geometry is realized. So this is not a formal consequence of cusp shape alone.

That means the next live theorem is now very specific:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a provenance-driven sign/monotonicity law for }M_1(u)=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds
\textbf{ and }M_3(u)=\int_0^u s^3K(s)\,ds.
}
\]

If I were pushing next, I would try to extract exactly one of these from the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) residue:

- \(M_1'(u)=uK(u)\) has fixed sign on the quotient branch,
- or \(M_3(u)\) has fixed sign on the quotient branch,
- or a one-sided comparison such as
  \[
  M_3(u)=u^2M_1(u)-\frac{u^2}{2}S(u)
  \]
  together with a provenance constraint on \(S\) that forces the sign of \(M_3\).

So the attack has now reduced the residual quotient-cusp lane to a very sharp pair of scalar moment laws.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:02:31)

_Thought for 22s_

Yes. There is a real simplification.

On the quotient cusp branch, you do **not** need to control both \(M_1\) and \(M_3\) independently.  
A one-sided monotonicity law for \(M_1\) alone already forces the right behavior of \(M_3\).

That is the cleanest further reduction I can justify.

---

Let
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
and define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},
\qquad
S(u):=\frac{y(u)}{x(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G(u)}{u^2},
\]
and
\[
K(u):=\frac{dT}{dx}(u)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u).
\]

As before, set
\[
M_1(u):=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds,
\qquad
M_3(u):=\int_0^u s^3\,K(s)\,ds.
\]

Then
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u),
\qquad
T(u)-S(u)=\frac{2}{u^2}M_3(u).
\]

Now the key identity is:
\[
\boxed{
M_3(u)=u^2M_1(u)-2\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

This is just integration by parts:
\[
M_3(u)=\int_0^u s^2\,dM_1(s)
= u^2M_1(u)-\int_0^u 2s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

From that, you get the crucial monotonicity consequence.

---

## Lemma: monotonicity of \(M_1\) forces one sign of \(M_3\)

Since
\[
M_1(0)=0,
\]
if \(M_1\) is monotone on \((0,U]\), then \(M_1\) has one sign there:

- if \(M_1\) is increasing, then \(M_1(u)\ge 0\);
- if \(M_1\) is decreasing, then \(M_1(u)\le 0\).

Now suppose \(M_1\) is increasing. Then for \(0\le s\le u\),
\[
M_1(s)\le M_1(u),
\]
so
\[
2\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds
\le
2\int_0^u s\,M_1(u)\,ds
=
u^2M_1(u).
\]
Hence
\[
M_3(u)\ge 0.
\]

Similarly, if \(M_1\) is decreasing, then
\[
M_1(s)\ge M_1(u),
\]
and since both sides are nonpositive, the same computation gives
\[
M_3(u)\le 0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
M_1 \text{ monotone } \Longrightarrow M_3 \text{ has the same sign as }M_1.
}
\]

And if \(M_1\) is strictly monotone and not identically zero, then \(M_3\) has strict one sign on \((0,U]\).

---

## Consequence for the quotient cusp lane

This collapses the earlier two-moment target to a **single** scalar target.

If \(M_1\) is strictly monotone on the relevant quotient branch, then:

1. \(T(u)=2M_1(u)\) is strictly monotone, so there is no repeated tangent slope;

2. \(M_3\) has one sign, so
   \[
   S'(u)=\frac{4}{u^3}M_3(u)
   \]
   has one sign, hence the cusp-secant slope \(S(u)\) is strictly monotone, so there is no repeated secant slope from the cusp point;

3. for
   \[
   H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)(x(v)-x(u)),
   \]
   one has
   \[
   H(u,v)=4\int_u^v s\,(M_1(s)-M_1(u))\,ds,
   \]
   so strict monotonicity of \(M_1\) forces \(H(u,v)\) to keep one sign for all \(v>u\). Thus the tangent at \(u\) never passes through a later point.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{strict monotonicity of }M_1(u)=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds
\textbf{ is sufficient to make the quotient cusp branch globally sterile.}
}
\]

That is a real improvement over the earlier target “control both \(M_1\) and \(M_3\).”

---

## What this means for the next attack

The residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane has now reduced to one sharp theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a provenance-driven monotonicity law for }M_1(u).
}
\]

Not for \(K\) directly.  
Not for the barycenter balance.  
Not for primitive areas.  
Just for
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds.
\]

If you can show \(M_1\) is strictly monotone on the relevant quotient branch, then the whole local/nonlocal quotient-cusp recurrence problem dies at once.

So the next concrete attack I would make is:

- compute \(M_1\) in terms of the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) quotient data;
- look for an external mixed/canonical identity forcing the sign of
  \[
  M_1'(u)=uK(u);
  \]
- or, failing pointwise sign of \(K\), look for a one-sided comparison that still implies monotonicity of the cumulative moment \(M_1\).

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

_Thought for 21s_

There is one more clean push, and it is an important **no-go**:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{on the quotient cusp branch, }M_1\textbf{ is abstractly arbitrary.}}
\]

So a monotonicity theorem for
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds
\]
cannot come from quotient-cusp geometry alone. It must come from **external provenance**.

That is the sharpest further reduction I can justify.

---

## 1. Exact reconstruction from \(M_1\)

On the quotient cusp branch write
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
and define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},
\qquad
K(u):=\frac{dT}{dx}(u).
\]

Earlier we proved
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u),
\qquad
M_1(u):=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds.
\]

So \(M_1\) is not just some derived moment. It is exactly half the tangent-slope function:
\[
\boxed{M_1(u)=\frac12\,T(u).}
\]

Then
\[
y'(u)=2u\,T(u)=4u\,M_1(u),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

And differentiating \(T=2M_1\) gives
\[
2M_1'(u)=2u\,K(u),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
K(u)=\frac{M_1'(u)}{u}.
}
\]

Thus the entire quotient-cusp geometry is reconstructed from \(M_1\):

\[
\boxed{
x(u)=u^2,\qquad
T(u)=2M_1(u),\qquad
K(u)=\frac{M_1'(u)}{u},\qquad
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

That is the key exact formula.

---

## 2. Cusp condition translated into \(M_1\)

For the quartic–sextic quotient cusp, we need
\[
y(u)=c\,u^3+O(u^4),\qquad c\neq 0.
\]

Using the reconstruction formula, this is equivalent to
\[
M_1(u)=a\,u+O(u^2),\qquad a\neq 0.
\]

Indeed, if
\[
M_1(u)=a\,u+O(u^2),
\]
then
\[
y(u)=4\int_0^u s(au+O(u^2))\,ds
= \frac{4a}{3}u^3+O(u^4).
\]

So the quotient cusp imposes only:

- \(M_1\) analytic,
- \(M_1(0)=0\),
- \(M_1'(0)\neq 0\).

That is all.

---

## 3. Strong no-go theorem

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{every analytic germ }M_1(u)\textbf{ with }M_1(0)=0,\ M_1'(0)\neq 0
\textbf{ occurs as the quotient-cusp moment function of some quartic–sextic germ.}
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the quotient-cusp branch carries one completely free analytic scalar germ }M_1.
}
\]

This kills the hope that monotonicity of \(M_1\) might be a formal consequence of the quotient-cusp normal form.

Because \(M_1\) can be any analytic germ with nonzero linear term, you can realize:

- strictly increasing \(M_1\),
- strictly decreasing \(M_1\),
- \(M_1\) with one interior critical point,
- \(M_1\) with many oscillations,
- and hence all the corresponding good/bad tangent-return behaviors.

So the clean no-go statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{quotient-cusp geometry alone does not constrain }M_1\textbf{ beyond }M_1(0)=0,\ M_1'(0)\neq 0.
}
\]

---

## 4. Consequence for the attack

Earlier we reduced the residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane to:

> prove \(M_1\) is strictly monotone.

This new result says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{that theorem cannot come from the cusp model itself.}
}
\]

It has to come from something **external** to the bare quotient cusp:

- provenance,
- mixed/canonical structure,
- a nonlocal law along the actual branch,
- or an involutive/quotient-diagonal identity that constrains the allowed \(M_1\)-germs.

So the live theorem is now even sharper:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove an external law that cuts down the free analytic germ }M_1.
}
\]

---

## 5. Best next target

Since \(M_1\) is the true free quotient-cusp variable, the next useful theorem should be phrased directly in \(M_1\), not in \(K\), \(T\), or \(y\).

The cleanest possibilities are now:

### A. Pointwise determination near the residual slice
Something like
\[
M_1'(u)=H(u,\text{external data}),
\]
or even
\[
M_1(u)=H(u,\text{external data}).
\]

### B. Global/nonlocal monotonicity law
Something like
\[
\mathcal E[M_1](u)\ \text{is monotone/sign-definite}
\]
along the actual quotient branch.

### C. Involution-driven asymmetry
A theorem saying the actual residual provenance does not allow an arbitrary quotient germ \(M_1\), but only a proper subclass, for example forbidding return of \(M_1\)-values.

---

## 6. The sharp update

So the attack has now reached this exact endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ lane is equivalent to one free analytic germ }M_1(u),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and bare quotient-cusp geometry imposes only }M_1(0)=0,\ M_1'(0)\neq 0.
}
\]

Therefore the next live theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find the first external mixed/provenance law that constrains the free quotient germ }M_1.
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:05:11)

_Thought for 10s_

Yes. There is one more clean reduction, and it is probably the sharpest endpoint of this \(K_v=0\) quotient-cusp lane.

The residual exact fixed-shear problem is not just controlled by one free analytic germ \(M_1\). It is **equivalent** to that germ, modulo the trivial nondegeneracy
\[
M_1(0)=0,\qquad M_1'(0)\neq 0.
\]

So the next theorem cannot be “prove something geometric about the quotient cusp.” It has to be:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{prove an external law that cuts down the germ }M_1.}
\]

Here is the exact reduction.

On the quotient cusp branch, with
\[
x(u)=u^2,\qquad y(u)=\widetilde G(u),
\]
define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)},\qquad
K(u):=\frac{dT}{dx}(u),\qquad
M_1(u):=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds.
\]
Then we already have
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u),
\qquad
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds,
\qquad
K(u)=\frac{M_1'(u)}{u}.
\]

So every local geometric quantity on the quotient cusp is a function of \(M_1\), and conversely every analytic germ \(M_1\) with
\[
M_1(0)=0,\qquad M_1'(0)\neq 0
\]
produces a quartic–sextic quotient cusp germ. In that sense:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{quotient-cusp local geometry and the germ }M_1\textbf{ are equivalent data.}
}
\]

That lets us sharpen the no-go dramatically.

Any proposed **local** theorem on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) slice that is phrased only in terms of:

- the quotient cusp germ,
- finitely many derivatives of \(x,y,T,K\),
- or finitely many derivatives of \(M_1\),

is still too weak, because \(M_1\) itself is arbitrary as an analytic germ.

So not only are the earlier geometric targets too weak:

- monotonicity of \(T\),
- sign of \(K\),
- primitive-area imbalance,
- split-point barycenter imbalance,

but in fact **every finite-order local statement** on the quotient cusp is too weak unless it imports external structure.

That is the new no-go theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{no finite-order local theorem on the bare quotient cusp can constrain the residual lane.}
}
\]

This leaves only two live possibilities on the \(K_v=0\) side.

First, a **pointwise external law** for \(M_1\), meaning an identity like
\[
F\bigl(u,\text{external fields},M_1(u),M_1'(u),\dots,M_1^{(r)}(u)\bigr)=0
\]
whose external fields are not themselves determined by the same quotient-cusp data.

Second, a **nonlocal law** for \(M_1\), such as an integral identity, monotonicity principle, or transport law along the actual residual branch.

And because \(M_1\) is the exact free quotient variable, we can now sharpen the order bookkeeping too.

If a pointwise external law determines \(M_1\) algebraically,
\[
M_1(u)=H(u,\text{external data}),
\]
the lane closes immediately.

If it gives a first-order ODE
\[
M_1'(u)=G(u,\text{external data},M_1(u)),
\]
that is already strong enough to make tangent-return highly rigid, since repeated tangent slope is exactly repeated value of \(M_1\).

So the next concrete attack I would make is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{look for the first external pointwise identity on the }K_v=0\textbf{ quotient slice whose highest-new content isolates }M_1
\textbf{ or }M_1'.}
\]

That is better than looking for a law for \(K\), \(T\), or \(\widetilde G\), because all of those are just differential shadows of the same germ \(M_1\).

So the sharp strategic update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ residue has collapsed to one free analytic germ }M_1,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and the only live next theorem is an external pointwise or nonlocal law that cuts down }M_1.
}
\]

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:07:04)

_Thought for 51s_

I found a cleaner obstruction on the quotient-cusp lane.

The right object is still
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds,
\]
but the geometry is best phrased as a **weighted Jensen problem** for \(M_1\), not as a sign problem for \(K\).

On the quotient cusp
\[
\Gamma(u)=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
we already have
\[
T(u)=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=2M_1(u),
\qquad
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

So the secant slope from the cusp point is
\[
S(u)=\frac{y(u)}{u^2}
=\frac{4}{u^2}\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

Now take two points \(0<u<v\). A common tangent line at \(u\) and \(v\) is equivalent to:

1. equal tangent slope,
\[
T(u)=T(v)
\iff
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=:m,
\]
and

2. the tangent at \(u\) passes through \(v\), i.e.
\[
y(v)-y(u)=T(u)(v^2-u^2).
\]

Substituting the formulas gives
\[
4\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds
=
2m\,(v^2-u^2),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
m=\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

So a common tangent on the quotient cusp is exactly this:

\[
\boxed{
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

That is the cleanest exact criterion so far.

It says: the common endpoint value of \(M_1\) must equal its weighted average on \([u,v]\) with weight \(s\).

From this you get a sharp sufficient obstruction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if }M_1\text{ is strictly convex or strictly concave on }[u,v],
\text{ then no common tangent is possible.}
}
\]

Reason: if \(M_1(u)=M_1(v)=m\) and \(M_1\) is strictly convex, then
\[
M_1(s)<m \quad (u<s<v),
\]
so its positive weighted average is strictly less than \(m\), contradiction. If \(M_1\) is strictly concave, the weighted average is strictly greater than \(m\), again contradiction.

So the quotient-cusp lane reduces to this very concrete theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove provenance-driven strict convexity or strict concavity of }M_1
\textbf{ on the relevant quotient branch.}
}
\]

That is strictly weaker than proving monotonicity of \(M_1\), and stronger than the older primitive-area and barycenter formulations.

There is also a parallel formula for cusp secants:
\[
S(u)=\frac{4}{u^2}\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds,
\]
so \(S\) is itself a weighted average of \(M_1\) over \([0,u]\). Thus any external law that makes \(M_1\) one-sidedly ordered against its weighted averages would also kill repeated cusp-secant slopes.

The catch is the same as before: abstractly, this is still too weak. Since \(M_1\) is an arbitrary analytic germ on the bare quotient cusp, one can choose nonconvex \(M_1\) realizing the bad geometry. So this does not close the lane by itself.

But it gives a sharper next attack than “control \(K\)”:

- not sign of \(K\),
- not primitive-area balance,
- not split barycenters,

but:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find an external mixed/provenance law forcing }M_1''\textbf{ to have one sign}
}
\]
or more directly strict convexity/concavity of \(M_1\).

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify.
