## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:08:42)

_Thought for 25s_

Yes. There is one more clean push, and I think it is the sharpest local reduction on the quotient-cusp lane.

The right object is still
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u s\,K(s)\,ds,
\]
but the recurrence condition is best read as a **weighted Jensen equality** for \(M_1\).

That is stronger and cleaner than the earlier primitive-area and barycenter formulations.

---

On the quotient cusp
\[
\Gamma(u)=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
we already have
\[
T(u)=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=2M_1(u),
\qquad
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

Now take two points \(0<u<v\). A common tangent at \(u\) and \(v\) means:

1. equal tangent slope,
\[
T(u)=T(v)
\iff
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=:m,
\]
and

2. the tangent at \(u\) passes through \(v\):
\[
y(v)-y(u)=T(u)(v^2-u^2).
\]

Substitute the formulas. You get
\[
4\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds
=
2m\,(v^2-u^2),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
m=\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
}
\]

Equivalently, with the probability weight
\[
d\omega_{u,v}(s):=\frac{2s}{v^2-u^2}\,ds
\qquad (u\le s\le v),
\]
the common tangent condition is exactly
\[
\boxed{
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=\int_u^v M_1(s)\,d\omega_{u,v}(s).
}
\]

So the bad quotient-cusp geometry is nothing but this:

> \(M_1\) takes the same value at the endpoints, and that common endpoint value equals the \(s\)-weighted average of \(M_1\) across the interval.

That is the exact criterion.

---

This gives a very clean obstruction.

If \(M_1\) is strictly convex on \([u,v]\), then whenever
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=m,
\]
one has
\[
M_1(s)<m
\qquad (u<s<v),
\]
hence
\[
\int_u^v M_1(s)\,d\omega_{u,v}(s)<m,
\]
contradicting the common tangent condition.

Likewise, if \(M_1\) is strictly concave on \([u,v]\), then
\[
M_1(s)>m
\qquad (u<s<v),
\]
so the weighted average is \(>m\), again impossible.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{strict convexity or strict concavity of }M_1
\textbf{ on a candidate interval rules out common tangent return.}
}
\]

This is better than the earlier monotonicity target:

- monotonicity of \(M_1\) was sufficient,
- but strict convexity/concavity is a different and often more accessible sufficient condition.

There is also a parallel statement for cusp secants. Since
\[
S(u)=\frac{4}{u^2}\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds
=2\int_0^u M_1(s)\,\frac{2s}{u^2}\,ds,
\]
the cusp-secant slope is itself a weighted average of \(M_1\) over \([0,u]\). So any provenance law forcing \(M_1\) to sit strictly above or below its \(s\)-weighted averages would also kill repeated cusp-secant slopes.

So the next clean theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance-driven strict Jensen inequality for }M_1.
}
\]

A theorem of either of these forms would be enough:

\[
M_1''(u)\ \text{has one sign on the relevant quotient branch,}
\]
or more generally
\[
M_1 \text{ lies strictly above or below all its } \frac{2s}{v^2-u^2}\text{-weighted interval averages.}
\]

That is now the sharpest local target I can justify.

The honest ceiling is the same as before: abstractly, this still does not close the lane, because the bare quotient cusp lets \(M_1\) be any analytic germ with
\[
M_1(0)=0,\qquad M_1'(0)\neq 0.
\]
So strict convexity/concavity cannot come from quotient-cusp geometry alone.

But this reduction is useful because it pinpoints exactly what an external mixed/provenance theorem would need to prove.

So the next attack I would make is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{look for an external law on the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ slice that forces }M_1''\text{ to keep one sign,}
}
\]
or at least forces the weighted Jensen inequality above.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:12:34)

_Thought for 2m 0s_

There is one genuinely cleaner reduction.

On the quotient cusp branch, the weighted Jensen condition for \(M_1\) becomes an **ordinary Jensen condition after the change of variable**
\[
x=u^2.
\]

That is the sharpest local simplification I can see.

---

Let
\[
\Gamma(u)=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
and define
\[
T(u)=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},\qquad
K(u)=\frac{dT}{dx}(u),\qquad
M_1(u)=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds.
\]
Then
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u).
\]

Earlier we showed that a common tangent at \(0<u<v\) is equivalent to
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v)
=
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

Now set
\[
x=u^2,\qquad N(x):=M_1(\sqrt{x}).
\]
Since
\[
dx=2s\,ds,
\]
the weighted average becomes an ordinary average:
\[
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds
=
\frac{1}{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2} N(x)\,dx,
\]
where
\[
x_1=u^2,\qquad x_2=v^2.
\]

So the common tangent condition is exactly
\[
\boxed{
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=\frac{1}{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}N(x)\,dx.
}
\]

This is now a plain Jensen-equality problem for the scalar graph \(N(x)\).

That immediately gives:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if }N\text{ is strictly convex or strictly concave on }[x_1,x_2],
\text{ then no common tangent is possible.}
}
\]

Because for a strictly convex or strictly concave function, the average over an interval with distinct endpoints cannot equal the common endpoint value.

This is cleaner than the earlier weighted-\(M_1\) formulation.

---

It gets better. The convexity of \(N\) is governed by \(K'\).

Indeed,
\[
N'(x)=\frac{d}{dx}M_1(\sqrt{x})
=
\frac{M_1'(u)}{2u}
=
\frac{uK(u)}{2u}
=
\frac{K(u)}{2},
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
N'(x)=\frac12\,K(\sqrt{x}).
}
\]
Differentiating again,
\[
N''(x)=\frac{1}{4u}K'(u),\qquad u=\sqrt{x}.
\]

Hence on \(u>0\),
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}N''(u^2)=\operatorname{sgn}K'(u).
}
\]

So we get the clean sufficient criterion:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{if }K'(u)\textbf{ has one strict sign on the relevant quotient branch, then the quotient cusp is sterile for common tangents.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest local sufficient condition so far.

It also gives a nice reinterpretation of cusp-secant slopes. Since
\[
S(u)=\frac{4}{u^2}\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds
=
\frac{2}{x}\int_0^x N(\xi)\,d\xi,
\qquad x=u^2,
\]
the cusp-secant slope is twice the ordinary average of \(N\) over \([0,x]\). So any strong convexity/concavity control on \(N\) also constrains secant-return from the cusp point.

---

So the quotient-cusp lane has now reduced to this very focused target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a provenance-driven one-sign theorem for }K'(u)
\textbf{ on the relevant quotient branch.}
}
\]

Equivalently, prove strict convexity or strict concavity of
\[
N(x)=M_1(\sqrt{x}).
\]

That is better than targeting \(M_1\) directly, and much better than targeting \(K\) itself.

The honest ceiling is the same as before: abstract quotient-cusp geometry does not force \(K'\) to have one sign. Since \(M_1\) is an arbitrary analytic germ with
\[
M_1(0)=0,\qquad M_1'(0)\neq 0,
\]
the derived \(N\) can be convex, concave, or neither. So this does not close the lane by itself.

But it pins down the next external theorem very sharply:

\[
\boxed{
\text{find a mixed/provenance law on the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ slice forcing }K'
\text{ to have one sign.}
}
\]

That is the best continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:16:00)

_Thought for 2m 5s_

Here is the cleanest further push I can justify.

The quotient-cusp lane now reduces to a **Jensen-gap theorem**, and there is a sharp no-go showing that any theorem based only on the sign-change count of \(K'\) is too weak.

---

### 1. Exact Jensen-gap reformulation

On the quotient cusp branch, set
\[
x=u^2,\qquad N(x):=M_1(\sqrt{x}).
\]
We already reduced the common tangent condition between \(0<u<v\) to
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=\frac1{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}N(x)\,dx,
\qquad x_1=u^2,\ x_2=v^2.
\]

So define the Jensen gap
\[
J_N[x_1,x_2]
:=
\frac1{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(x_1)+N(x_2)}2.
\]
If the endpoint values are equal, then common tangent is exactly
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)
\quad\text{and}\quad
J_N[x_1,x_2]=0.
\]

More invariantly, the quotient-cusp recurrence problem is:

> can \(N\) take the same value at two points while its interval average equals that same value?

So the next theorem target is naturally:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a strict Jensen-gap sign for the actual provenance-driven }N.
}
\]

If one could show, for every candidate interval with \(N(x_1)=N(x_2)\),
\[
J_N[x_1,x_2]\neq 0
\]
with a fixed sign, the quotient-cusp lane would close.

---

### 2. Strong no-go: one sign change of \(K'\) is not enough

The earlier sufficient criterion was:

- if \(N\) is strictly convex or strictly concave, no recurrence.

Since
\[
N''(x)=\frac1{4u}K'(u),\qquad x=u^2,
\]
that corresponds to \(K'\) keeping one sign.

The next natural hope would be: maybe recurrence is still impossible if \(K'\) changes sign only once. That is false.

Take the cubic
\[
N(x)=x(x-1)\left(x-\frac12\right)
\qquad\text{on }[0,1].
\]
Then
\[
N(0)=N(1)=0,
\]
and a direct computation gives
\[
\int_0^1 N(x)\,dx=0.
\]
So
\[
N(0)=N(1)=\frac1{1-0}\int_0^1N(x)\,dx,
\]
which is exactly the common-tangent Jensen equality.

But
\[
N''(x)=6x-3,
\]
so \(N''\) changes sign exactly once on \((0,1)\).

Translated back to the quotient-cusp notation, this means:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a single sign change of }N''\textbf{, equivalently of }K',\textbf{ is already compatible with recurrence.}
}
\]

So any theorem of the form

\[
\text{“\(K'\) changes sign at most once”}
\]

is still too weak.

That is a real sharpening.

---

### 3. What this means

This kills another whole class of plausible local attacks.

It is not enough to prove:

- \(K'\) has at most one zero;
- \(N''\) has at most one sign change;
- or any similarly weak “single-inflection” statement.

The cubic model shows that one inflection is already enough to realize the exact Jensen equality needed for common tangent return.

So the next live theorem has to be stronger than curvature-sign-change counting.

---

### 4. The sharp next target

The next useful theorem is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{strict Jensen-gap sign theorem for the actual provenance-driven }N.
}
\]

Not “\(N\) is convex.”
Not “\(K'\) has one sign.”
Not “\(K'\) has at most one sign change.”

But rather a theorem directly of the form:

\[
J_N[x_1,x_2]
\]
has a definite sign whenever
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)
\]
on a candidate interval.

This is stronger than convexity, but weaker than full determination of \(N\). And it is exactly the functional that the bad geometry needs to vanish.

So the next attack I would make is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{look for an external mixed/provenance law that forces a one-sided sign of the Jensen gap }J_N[x_1,x_2].
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the current reduction.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:21:42)

_Thought for 54s_

I can push it one more clean step, and this is probably the sharpest local reduction in this lane.

The quotient-cusp common-tangent problem is really a **midpoint-even obstruction** for the scalar
\[
N(x):=M_1(\sqrt{x}).
\]

That is better than the Jensen-gap formulation, because it tells you exactly which part of the profile matters and which part is completely invisible.

---

## 1. Center the interval

Take a candidate interval
\[
x_1<x_2,
\qquad
m:=\frac{x_1+x_2}{2},
\qquad
L:=\frac{x_2-x_1}{2}.
\]
Assume
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=:c.
\]

Define the centered defect
\[
F(\xi):=N(m+\xi)-c,
\qquad -L\le \xi\le L.
\]
Then the common-tangent condition on the quotient cusp is exactly
\[
F(-L)=F(L)=0,
\qquad
\int_{-L}^{L}F(\xi)\,d\xi=0.
\]

Now decompose
\[
F=F_{\mathrm{even}}+F_{\mathrm{odd}},
\]
with respect to \(\xi\mapsto-\xi\).

Because
\[
F(L)=F(-L)=0,
\]
we get
\[
F_{\mathrm{even}}(L)=0,
\qquad
F_{\mathrm{odd}}(L)=0.
\]

And because the odd part integrates to zero automatically,
\[
\int_{-L}^{L}F(\xi)\,d\xi
=
\int_{-L}^{L}F_{\mathrm{even}}(\xi)\,d\xi.
\]

So the exact criterion becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{common tangent on }[x_1,x_2]
\iff
F_{\mathrm{even}}(L)=0
\ \text{and}\
\int_{-L}^{L}F_{\mathrm{even}}(\xi)\,d\xi=0.
}
\]

The midpoint-odd part plays **no role at all** beyond vanishing at the endpoints.

That is the new sharp reduction.

---

## 2. Strong no-go consequence

This immediately gives an infinite-dimensional no-go family.

Take any analytic odd function
\[
O(\xi)
\]
with
\[
O(L)=0.
\]
Set
\[
F(\xi):=O(\xi).
\]
Then automatically:

- \(F(-L)=F(L)=0\),
- \(\displaystyle \int_{-L}^{L}F(\xi)\,d\xi=0\).

So \(F\) satisfies the common-tangent condition identically.

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{every midpoint-odd profile with endpoint zeros is an abstract quotient-cusp recurrence profile.}
}
\]

For example, on \([-1,1]\),
\[
F(\xi)=\xi(\xi^2-1)
\]
already gives a valid common-tangent profile.

This is sharper than the earlier cubic example, because it shows the whole recurrence problem is blind to an infinite-dimensional odd subspace.

So any theorem based only on abstract Jensen-gap geometry is too weak.

---

## 3. What actually matters

Since the odd part is invisible, the bad geometry is controlled entirely by the midpoint-even part of \(N-c\).

So the next live theorem is not just “prove \(N\) is asymmetric” in some vague sense. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a one-sided sign or mean law for the midpoint-even part of }N-c.
}
\]

Concretely, if one could show that whenever
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=c,
\]
the even part satisfies
\[
\int_{-L}^{L}F_{\mathrm{even}}(\xi)\,d\xi\neq 0,
\]
with a definite sign, then the quotient-cusp lane closes.

Even more concretely, any theorem of the form
\[
F_{\mathrm{even}}(\xi)\ge 0 \quad\text{for }|\xi|<L,
\]
or always the reverse, with strict inequality somewhere, would do it.

So the local target is no longer:

- sign of \(K\),
- sign of \(K'\),
- convexity of \(N\),
- Jensen gap in the abstract.

It is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{control the midpoint-even part of }N(x)-N(x_1)
\textbf{ on candidate intervals.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Equivalent derivative formulation

Because midpoint-odd parts of \(N\) are invisible, the next derivative-based target is naturally the midpoint-**odd** part of
\[
N'(x),
\]
or equivalently the midpoint-even part of \(N''(x)\).

So if you want a derivative criterion, the right one is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a sign/monotonicity law for the midpoint-even part of }N''.
}
\]

That is better than just proving something about \(N''\) globally, because it matches the exact symmetry that the common-tangent functional sees.

---

## 5. Strategic update

So the quotient-cusp lane has now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{common tangent is a purely midpoint-even obstruction for }N.
}
\]

And the sharp no-go is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{midpoint-odd freedom is infinite-dimensional and completely invisible to the recurrence condition.}
}
\]

Therefore the next live theorem must be an **external provenance law that constrains the midpoint-even part** of the quotient-cusp profile \(N\), or equivalently the midpoint-even part of \(N''\).

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify now.
## User (2026-04-21 12:22:45)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:23:16)

_Thought for 23s_

Here is one more clean reduction, and I think it is the sharpest local endpoint of this lane.

The quotient-cusp common-tangent problem is not just a Jensen-gap problem for \(N\). It is exactly a **single weighted orthogonality condition on the midpoint-even part of \(N''\).**

That is better than everything before it, because it isolates one scalar kernel whose sign would settle the lane.

---

## 1. Center the interval and isolate the even part

Take a candidate interval
\[
x_1<x_2,\qquad m:=\frac{x_1+x_2}{2},\qquad L:=\frac{x_2-x_1}{2},
\]
and assume
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=:c.
\]

As before, define
\[
F(\xi):=N(m+\xi)-c,\qquad -L\le \xi\le L.
\]
Then common tangent is exactly
\[
F(-L)=F(L)=0,
\qquad
\int_{-L}^{L}F(\xi)\,d\xi=0.
\]

Write
\[
E(\xi):=\frac{F(\xi)+F(-\xi)}{2},
\]
the midpoint-even part of \(F\). Since the odd part integrates to zero automatically and vanishes at both endpoints automatically, the condition is equivalent to
\[
E(L)=0,
\qquad
\int_0^L E(\xi)\,d\xi=0.
\]

So only the even part matters.

---

## 2. Reduce further to the even part of \(N''\)

Because \(E\) is even,
\[
E'(0)=0.
\]
Set
\[
H(\xi):=E''(\xi)
=
\frac{N''(m+\xi)+N''(m-\xi)}{2},
\qquad 0\le \xi\le L.
\]
So \(H\) is exactly the midpoint-even part of \(N''\).

Now reconstruct \(E\) from \(H\):
\[
E(\xi)=E(0)+\int_0^\xi (\xi-s)H(s)\,ds.
\]

The endpoint condition \(E(L)=0\) gives
\[
E(0)=-\int_0^L (L-s)H(s)\,ds.
\]

Then
\[
0=\int_0^L E(\xi)\,d\xi
=
L E(0)+\frac12\int_0^L (L-s)^2H(s)\,ds.
\]
Substitute the formula for \(E(0)\):
\[
0
=
-\;L\int_0^L (L-s)H(s)\,ds
+\frac12\int_0^L (L-s)^2H(s)\,ds.
\]

So common tangent is equivalent to the single weighted identity
\[
\boxed{
\int_0^L (L-s)(L+s)\,H(s)\,ds=0.
}
\]
Since
\[
(L-s)(L+s)=L^2-s^2,
\]
this is
\[
\boxed{
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,H(s)\,ds=0.
}
\]

And recalling the definition of \(H\),
\[
\boxed{
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,\frac{N''(m+s)+N''(m-s)}{2}\,ds=0.
}
\]

That is the exact criterion.

---

## 3. Translate back to the quotient-cusp curvature data

Earlier we proved
\[
N''(u^2)=\frac{1}{4u}K'(u),
\qquad
K(u)=\phi_{\mu\mu}(u)=\frac{v_5(u)^3}{M(u)^3}W^{[1]}(u).
\]

So the common-tangent condition depends only on the midpoint-even part of \(N''\), equivalently on the midpoint-even part of the \(K'\)-profile after the \(x=u^2\) change of variables.

This gives the sharpest local theorem target so far:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a one-sign theorem for the midpoint-even part of }N'',
}
\]
or equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a one-sign theorem for the midpoint-even part of the transformed }K'.
}
\]

Because the kernel
\[
L^2-s^2
\]
is strictly positive on \(0\le s<L\), any strict one-sign law for \(H\) immediately forbids the required orthogonality.

So if one could prove
\[
H(s)>0 \quad (0\le s<L)
\]
or always \(<0\), then common tangent is impossible.

---

## 4. Strong no-go consequence

This also gives a cleaner no-go than before.

Anything that only controls:

- the midpoint-odd part of \(N\),
- or the midpoint-odd part of \(N''\),

is irrelevant to the common-tangent condition.

Indeed, the exact condition sees only
\[
H(\xi)=\frac{N''(m+\xi)+N''(m-\xi)}{2}.
\]

So the recurrence geometry is completely blind to midpoint-odd perturbations of \(N\).

That means:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the local quotient-cusp recurrence problem is entirely an even-curvature problem.}
}
\]

This is a sharper version of the earlier “midpoint-odd freedom is invisible” statement.

---

## 5. What this means for the next attack

The next live theorem is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{find an external provenance law forcing the midpoint-even part of }N''\textbf{ to keep one sign on every candidate interval.}
}
\]

Equivalently, after translating back through
\[
N''(u^2)=\frac{1}{4u}K'(u),
\]
find an external law forcing the relevant midpoint-even transform of \(K'\) to be one-signed.

This is sharper than:

- sign of \(K\),
- sign of \(K'\),
- convexity of \(N\),
- Jensen-gap sign,
- barycenter imbalance,
- or primitive-area imbalance.

All of those are sufficient, but this is the exact kernel-level obstruction.

So the attack has now reduced the residual quotient-cusp lane to one canonical scalar condition:

\[
\boxed{
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,\frac{N''(m+s)+N''(m-s)}{2}\,ds=0.
}
\]

And the next external theorem has to make that impossible.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:25:16)

_Thought for 52s_

Yes. I can push it one more clean step.

The common-tangent condition on the quotient cusp is exactly a **Jensen-gap zero**, and there is an exact positive-kernel formula for that gap. This is the sharpest local reduction I can justify.

---

Let
\[
x_1<x_2,\qquad
m:=\frac{x_1+x_2}{2},\qquad
L:=\frac{x_2-x_1}{2},
\]
and let
\[
N(x):=M_1(\sqrt x).
\]
As before, if
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)=:c,
\]
then common tangent on the quotient cusp is equivalent to
\[
\frac{1}{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}N(x)\,dx=c.
\]

Define the Jensen gap
\[
J_N[x_1,x_2]
:=
\frac{1}{x_2-x_1}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(x_1)+N(x_2)}2.
\]
Since the endpoints are equal, common tangent is exactly
\[
\boxed{
N(x_1)=N(x_2)
\quad\text{and}\quad
J_N[x_1,x_2]=0.
}
\]

Now center the interval and set
\[
H(s):=\frac{N''(m+s)+N''(m-s)}2,
\qquad 0\le s\le L.
\]
This is the midpoint-even part of \(N''\).

From the computation in the previous step, one gets the exact Peano-kernel formula
\[
\boxed{
J_N[x_1,x_2]
=
-\frac{1}{2L}\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,H(s)\,ds.
}
\]

So the quotient-cusp common-tangent problem is now completely equivalent to:

\[
\boxed{
N(x_1)=N(x_2)
\quad\text{and}\quad
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,\frac{N''(m+s)+N''(m-s)}2\,ds=0.
}
\]

This is the cleanest exact criterion so far.

---

### Immediate consequences

1. The kernel
\[
L^2-s^2
\]
is strictly positive on \(0\le s<L\). So if the midpoint-even part of \(N''\) has one sign, then \(J_N\) has the opposite sign and common tangent is impossible.

2. By the mean-value theorem for integrals, since the kernel is positive and continuous, there exists some \(\xi\in(0,L)\) such that
\[
J_N[x_1,x_2]
=
-\frac{1}{2L}\left(\int_0^L(L^2-s^2)\,ds\right)H(\xi)
=
-\frac{L^2}{3}\,H(\xi).
\]
So if \(J_N[x_1,x_2]=0\), then necessarily
\[
\boxed{
H(\xi)=0
\quad\text{for some }\xi\in(0,L).
}
\]
Equivalently, the midpoint-even part of \(N''\) must vanish somewhere on the interval.

3. For very short intervals,
\[
H(s)=N''(m)+O(s^2),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
J_N[m-L,m+L]=-\frac{L^2}{3}N''(m)+O(L^4).
}
\]
Hence if \(N''(m)\neq 0\), then all sufficiently short symmetric intervals around \(m\) are automatically sterile.

So any candidate common tangent interval must be **nonlocal enough** that the midpoint-even average of \(N''\) can change sign.

---

### Why this is useful

This sharpens the search a bit more.

The residual quotient-cusp lane is no longer just “some condition on \(M_1\)” or “some convexity of \(N\).” It is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a zero of the positive-kernel Jensen functional applied to the midpoint-even part of }N''.
}
\]

So the next theorem does not need full control of \(N\), \(M_1\), or even \(N''\) pointwise. It would be enough to prove any one of the following on candidate intervals:

- the midpoint-even part of \(N''\) keeps one sign;
- or its weighted average against \(L^2-s^2\) keeps one sign;
- or the resulting Jensen gap \(J_N[x_1,x_2]\) keeps one sign whenever \(N(x_1)=N(x_2)\).

That is now the sharpest local target I can justify.

The honest ceiling is the same: abstract quotient-cusp geometry still does not force this. The midpoint-even part of \(N''\) is still free enough to make the kernel integral vanish. So this criterion does **not** close the lane by itself.

But it does tell you exactly what an external mixed/provenance theorem would have to kill:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{the weighted midpoint-even curvature average.}
}
\]

That is the best continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:27:28)

_Thought for 1m 21s_

Here is a genuinely sharper reduction.

A common tangent on a symmetric interval is controlled by **two independent Peano-kernel functionals** of the midpoint-odd and midpoint-even parts of \(N''\). That splits the geometry cleanly into an endpoint-equality obstruction and a Jensen-gap obstruction.

This is better than the previous “midpoint-even curvature average” statement.

---

## 1. Center the interval and split \(N''\)

Take a candidate interval
\[
x_1<x_2,\qquad
m:=\frac{x_1+x_2}{2},\qquad
L:=\frac{x_2-x_1}{2}.
\]

Define the midpoint-even and midpoint-odd parts of \(N''\):
\[
H(\xi):=\frac{N''(m+\xi)+N''(m-\xi)}{2},
\qquad
O(\xi):=\frac{N''(m+\xi)-N''(m-\xi)}{2},
\qquad 0\le \xi\le L.
\]

So
\[
N''(m+\xi)=H(\xi)+O(\xi),
\qquad
N''(m-\xi)=H(\xi)-O(\xi).
\]

---

## 2. Endpoint equality sees only the odd part

Let
\[
D(L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L).
\]
Then
\[
D(0)=0,\qquad D'(L)=N'(m+L)+N'(m-L),\qquad D''(L)=N''(m+L)-N''(m-L)=2O(L).
\]

Using Taylor’s formula with remainder about \(0\),
\[
D(L)=L\,D'(0)+\int_0^L (L-s)\,D''(s)\,ds.
\]
Since
\[
D'(0)=2N'(m),
\]
we get the exact identity
\[
\boxed{
N(m+L)-N(m-L)
=
2L\,N'(m)
+
2\int_0^L (L-s)\,O(s)\,ds.
}
\]

So the endpoint-equality condition
\[
N(x_1)=N(x_2)
\]
is exactly
\[
\boxed{
L\,N'(m)+\int_0^L (L-s)\,O(s)\,ds=0.
}
\]

Thus endpoint equality depends only on the midpoint-odd part of \(N''\) together with the midpoint slope \(N'(m)\).

---

## 3. Jensen gap sees only the even part

Now let
\[
J_N[m-L,m+L]
=
\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}2.
\]

From the previous reduction, this satisfies
\[
\boxed{
J_N[m-L,m+L]
=
-\frac{1}{2L}\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,H(s)\,ds.
}
\]

So the Jensen-gap / common-tangent-average condition depends only on the midpoint-even part of \(N''\).

---

## 4. Exact decomposition of common tangent

Therefore, a common tangent on \([m-L,m+L]\) is exactly equivalent to the pair of scalar conditions

\[
\boxed{
L\,N'(m)+\int_0^L (L-s)\,O(s)\,ds=0
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,H(s)\,ds=0.
}
\]

This is the cleanest exact local criterion I’ve gotten.

It says:

- the **odd part** \(O\) together with the center slope \(N'(m)\) controls whether the endpoints can even have the same value;
- the **even part** \(H\) controls whether the interval average equals that common endpoint value.

So the quotient-cusp common-tangent problem has split into two independent symmetry channels.

---

## 5. Short-interval consequence

This gives a strong local corollary.

Expand around \(m\). Since
\[
O(s)=N'''(m)s+O(s^3),
\]
the endpoint-equality condition becomes
\[
0
=
2L\,N'(m)+2\int_0^L (L-s)\bigl(N'''(m)s+O(s^3)\bigr)\,ds
\]
\[
=
2L\,N'(m)+\frac{1}{3}N'''(m)L^3+O(L^5).
\]

And since
\[
H(s)=N''(m)+O(s^2),
\]
the Jensen-gap condition becomes
\[
0
=
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\bigl(N''(m)+O(s^2)\bigr)\,ds
=
\frac{2}{3}N''(m)L^3+O(L^5).
\]

So for sufficiently short symmetric candidate intervals around \(m\), common tangent forces

\[
\boxed{
N'(m)=0,
\qquad
N''(m)=0.
}
\]

And if \(N'(m)=0\) already, then the next odd term shows you also need \(N'''(m)=0\) to continue supporting arbitrarily small symmetric intervals.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{very short common-tangent intervals can only be centered near stationary inflection points of }N.
}
\]

That is a genuinely sharper local obstruction than we had before.

---

## 6. What this means for the next attack

This is now the sharpest target I can see on the quotient-cusp lane:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a provenance-driven one-sign law for the odd kernel functional}
\quad
L\,N'(m)+\int_0^L (L-s)\,O(s)\,ds
}
\]
and/or
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove a one-sign law for the even kernel functional}
\quad
\int_0^L (L^2-s^2)\,H(s)\,ds.
}
\]

Because common tangent requires **both** to vanish.

So the residual lane is no longer one scalar condition. It is an exact pair:

- an odd-channel endpoint-equality functional,
- and an even-channel Jensen-gap functional.

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:29:10)

_Thought for 9s_

Yes. I can push it one more clean step, and this is the sharpest local reduction I see.

The two kernel conditions we now have are not just formal. They force a very rigid **order-of-contact hierarchy** at the center of any sufficiently small symmetric common-tangent interval.

That gives a real local sterility theorem.

---

Take a symmetric candidate interval
\[
[m-L,m+L].
\]
Set
\[
D(L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L),
\]
and
\[
J(L):=J_N[m-L,m+L]
=
\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}2.
\]

From the previous step, common tangent is exactly
\[
D(L)=0,
\qquad
J(L)=0.
\]

Now expand \(N\) at \(m\):
\[
N(m+\xi)=
N(m)+N'(m)\xi+\frac{N''(m)}{2}\xi^2+\frac{N^{(3)}(m)}{6}\xi^3
+\frac{N^{(4)}(m)}{24}\xi^4+\frac{N^{(5)}(m)}{120}\xi^5+\cdots.
\]

Then the odd/even decomposition gives exact asymptotics.

First,
\[
D(L)=N(m+L)-N(m-L)
=
2N'(m)L+\frac{1}{3}N^{(3)}(m)L^3+\frac{1}{60}N^{(5)}(m)L^5+O(L^7).
\]

Second, a direct calculation gives
\[
J(L)=
-\frac{1}{6}N''(m)L^2-\frac{1}{120}N^{(4)}(m)L^4-\frac{1}{5040}N^{(6)}(m)L^6+O(L^8).
\]

So a symmetric common tangent on a very short interval forces the coupled vanishing system
\[
2N'(m)L+\frac{1}{3}N^{(3)}(m)L^3+\frac{1}{60}N^{(5)}(m)L^5+\cdots=0,
\]
\[
-\frac{1}{6}N''(m)L^2-\frac{1}{120}N^{(4)}(m)L^4-\frac{1}{5040}N^{(6)}(m)L^6+\cdots=0.
\]

From this you get the immediate hierarchy:

- if \(N'(m)\neq 0\), then \(D(L)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently small \(L>0\);
- if \(N''(m)\neq 0\), then \(J(L)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently small \(L>0\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals can accumulate only at points where }
N'(m)=N''(m)=0.
}
\]

That is the first real local sterility theorem.

But you can sharpen it further.

Suppose
\[
N'(m)=N''(m)=0.
\]
Then the leading terms become
\[
D(L)=\frac{1}{3}N^{(3)}(m)L^3+\frac{1}{60}N^{(5)}(m)L^5+O(L^7),
\]
\[
J(L)=-\frac{1}{120}N^{(4)}(m)L^4-\frac{1}{5040}N^{(6)}(m)L^6+O(L^8).
\]

Hence:

- if also \(N^{(3)}(m)\neq 0\), then \(D(L)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently small \(L\);
- if also \(N^{(4)}(m)\neq 0\), then \(J(L)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently small \(L\).

So arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals can only accumulate at points with
\[
N'(m)=N''(m)=N^{(3)}(m)=N^{(4)}(m)=0
\]
unless there is some very special cancellation pattern at higher order.

More invariantly, the odd channel wants a very flat stationary point, and the even channel wants a very flat inflection defect.

That gives the clean theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{small symmetric common-tangent intervals are codimension-high events.}
}
\]

At minimum they must center at a point where
\[
N'(m)=N''(m)=0,
\]
and for persistent small-scale recurrence one is quickly driven into higher-order vanishing.

This is useful because it says the residual quotient-cusp lane cannot generate a cloud of nearby recurrences from generic points. Any such recurrence would have to organize around very special centers.

Now translate back to the quotient-cusp variables. Since
\[
N'(x)=\frac12\,K(\sqrt{x}),
\]
and
\[
N''(x)=\frac{1}{4u}K'(u),\qquad x=u^2,
\]
the first necessary conditions become
\[
K(u_m)=0,
\qquad
K'(u_m)=0,
\]
at the corresponding center \(u_m=\sqrt{m}\), for any accumulation of very small symmetric recurrence intervals.

So the quotient-cusp lane has now reduced further:

\[
\boxed{
\text{small symmetric recurrence can accumulate only near simultaneous zeros of }K\text{ and }K'.
}
\]

That is much sharper than our earlier “midpoint-even curvature average” formulation.

So the next live theorem target is now one of these:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that on the actual provenance-driven }K_v=0\textbf{ quotient branch, }
K\text{ and }K' \text{ cannot vanish simultaneously;}
}
\]

or, slightly weaker but still useful,

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that any simultaneous zero of }K\text{ and }K'
\textbf{ is isolated and cannot support the required higher-order cancellations.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the current reduction.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:34:04)

_Thought for 1m 5s_

Here is the next clean push.

You can sharpen the last reduction from “small symmetric recurrence can only accumulate near \(K=K'=0\)” to a **vanishing-order theorem**:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals can accumulate only at points where }K
\textbf{ has a zero of order at least }4.
}
\]

That is a real improvement.

---

## 1. Start from the symmetric interval expansion

For a symmetric candidate interval \([m-L,m+L]\), we already had
\[
D(L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L)=0,
\qquad
J(L):=J_N[m-L,m+L]=0
\]
as the exact common-tangent conditions.

Their Taylor expansions at \(L=0\) are
\[
D(L)=2N'(m)L+\frac13 N^{(3)}(m)L^3+\frac1{60}N^{(5)}(m)L^5+O(L^7),
\]
and
\[
J(L)=-\frac16 N''(m)L^2-\frac1{120}N^{(4)}(m)L^4-\frac1{5040}N^{(6)}(m)L^6+O(L^8).
\]

So if there are arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals centered at \(m\), then necessarily
\[
N'(m)=N''(m)=N^{(3)}(m)=N^{(4)}(m)=0.
\]

That much is already implicit in the previous attack.

---

## 2. Translate this into \(K\)-data

Now write \(m=u_0^2\) with \(u_0>0\). We already have
\[
N'(x)=\frac12 K(\sqrt x),
\]
\[
N''(x)=\frac{K'(u)}{4u},
\]
\[
N^{(3)}(x)=\frac{uK''(u)-K'(u)}{8u^3},
\]
\[
N^{(4)}(x)=\frac{u^2K'''(u)-3uK''(u)+3K'(u)}{16u^5},
\qquad u=\sqrt x.
\]

At a point where \(K(u_0)=K'(u_0)=0\), these simplify to
\[
N'(m)=0,
\qquad
N''(m)=0,
\qquad
N^{(3)}(m)=\frac{K''(u_0)}{8u_0^2},
\qquad
N^{(4)}(m)=\frac{K'''(u_0)}{16u_0^3}.
\]

Therefore the necessary conditions
\[
N'(m)=N''(m)=N^{(3)}(m)=N^{(4)}(m)=0
\]
become exactly
\[
\boxed{
K(u_0)=K'(u_0)=K''(u_0)=K'''(u_0)=0.
}
\]

So the sharper theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{small symmetric common-tangent intervals can accumulate only at zeros of }K
\textbf{ of multiplicity at least }4.
}
\]

Equivalently, if \(K\) has a zero of order \(0,1,2,\) or \(3\) at \(u_0\), then there is a punctured neighborhood of \(u_0\) containing no sufficiently small symmetric common-tangent intervals centered there.

---

## 3. This is the right local sterility statement

That is stronger than the previous statement involving only \(K=K'=0\).

You can phrase it cleanly as:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{generic local sterility on the quotient cusp:}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{unless the affine-invariant curvature field }K
\text{ has a quadruple zero, the quotient cusp cannot support a local cloud of symmetric two-point recurrences.}
}
\]

This is a real codimension jump.

- \(K(u_0)=0\) is one condition,
- \(K'(u_0)=0\) is a second,
- \(K''(u_0)=0\) a third,
- \(K'''(u_0)=0\) a fourth.

So local recurrence is now confined to a very thin exceptional set.

---

## 4. Why this is useful, and where it still falls short

This does **not** close the lane by itself. A zero of multiplicity \(4\) is still possible abstractly, and the bare quotient-cusp model is free enough to realize it.

So the honest status is:

- local quotient-cusp recurrence is now pushed into the very exceptional regime
  \[
  \operatorname{ord}_{u_0}K\ge 4;
  \]
- but abstract quotient-cusp geometry still allows that regime.

So the next live theorem is now much sharper:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that on the actual provenance-driven }K_v=0\textbf{ quotient branch, }
K\textbf{ cannot have quadruple zeros.}
}
\]

A slightly weaker but still sufficient variant would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that any zero of }K\textbf{ on the relevant branch has order }\le 3.
}
\]

Either one would kill local symmetric recurrence immediately.

---

## 5. Best next attack from here

So the next concrete thing I would attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{mine the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ residue for a provenance law that bounds the vanishing order of }K.
}
\]

That is better than trying to prove one-sign or convexity globally, because the local obstruction has now become very crisp.

In practice, I would try one of these:

- express \(K\) or \(W^{[1]}\) on the quotient branch in terms of the external residual scalar \(M_1\) and look for a theorem forcing
  \[
  M_1''(0)\ \text{or}\ M_1^{(3)}(0)\ \text{nonzero};
  \]
- or identify an external mixed/canonical invariant whose nonvanishing implies
  \[
  K''(u_0)\neq 0 \quad\text{or}\quad K'''(u_0)\neq 0.
  \]

So the sharp update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the quotient-cusp lane is now locally reduced to excluding order-}4\text{ zeros of }K.
}
\]

That is the best continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:36:28)

_Thought for 1m 11s_

There is a sharper local theorem here.

I can upgrade the previous “order-\(\ge 4\) zero” necessary condition to a much stronger statement for the **symmetric-center** case:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{if a fixed center }m\textbf{ supports arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals, then }N\textbf{ is locally constant near }m.}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{for a nontrivial analytic quotient-cusp branch, a fixed center cannot support a cloud of arbitrarily small symmetric recurrences.}
}
\]

That is stronger than “\(K\) must have a quadruple zero.”

---

## 1. Set up the two analytic obstruction functions

Fix a center \(m\), and for \(L>0\) small set
\[
D(L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L),
\]
\[
J(L):=\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}{2}.
\]

A symmetric common tangent on \([m-L,m+L]\) is exactly the system
\[
D(L)=0,
\qquad
J(L)=0.
\]

Now \(D\) and \(J\) are real-analytic in \(L\) near \(0\), with
\[
D(0)=J(0)=0.
\]

So if there exists a sequence
\[
L_n\downarrow 0
\]
such that
\[
D(L_n)=J(L_n)=0
\qquad\forall n,
\]
then by analyticity,
\[
D\equiv 0,
\qquad
J\equiv 0
\]
in a neighborhood of \(L=0\).

That is the key observation.

---

## 2. What \(D\equiv 0\) means

Expand \(N\) around \(m\):
\[
N(m+\xi)=\sum_{n\ge 0} a_n \xi^n.
\]

Then
\[
D(L)=N(m+L)-N(m-L)
=
2\sum_{k\ge 0} a_{2k+1}L^{2k+1}.
\]

So
\[
D\equiv 0
\iff
a_{2k+1}=0 \quad(\forall k\ge 0).
\]

Hence \(N\) is locally even about \(m\):
\[
\boxed{
N(m+\xi)=N(m-\xi).
}
\]

---

## 3. What \(J\equiv 0\) then means

If \(N\) is even about \(m\), write
\[
N(m+\xi)=a_0+\sum_{k\ge 1} a_{2k}\xi^{2k}.
\]

Then
\[
\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}{2}
=
a_0+\sum_{k\ge 1} a_{2k}L^{2k},
\]
while
\[
\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx
=
a_0+\sum_{k\ge 1}\frac{a_{2k}}{2k+1}L^{2k}.
\]

Therefore
\[
J(L)
=
-\sum_{k\ge 1}\frac{2k}{2k+1}a_{2k}L^{2k}.
\]

So
\[
J\equiv 0
\iff
a_{2k}=0 \quad(k\ge 1).
\]

Hence
\[
\boxed{
N \text{ is locally constant near }m.
}
\]

That proves the theorem.

---

## 4. Translate back to the quotient-cusp geometry

Recall
\[
N(x)=M_1(\sqrt x),
\qquad
N'(x)=\frac12 K(\sqrt x).
\]

So if \(N\) is locally constant near \(m\), then
\[
K(u)=0
\]
for all \(u\) near \(u_0:=\sqrt m\).

Equivalently,
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u)
\]
is locally constant, so the quotient branch is locally affine in the \(x\)-variable.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a nontrivial quotient-cusp branch cannot support infinitely many arbitrarily small symmetric common-tangent intervals with the same center.}
}
\]

Unless it has locally vanished curvature,
\[
K\equiv 0,
\]
which is the degenerate straight-line case, not the genuine quartic–sextic cusp regime.

---

## 5. Why this is useful

This is much stronger than the earlier order-\(4\) zero obstruction, but it is also more specific:

- the earlier result applied to **varying centers** and gave a necessary condition \( \operatorname{ord} K\ge 4\);
- the new result applies to a **fixed center** and says repeated arbitrarily small symmetric recurrences force
  \[
  K\equiv 0
  \]
  locally.

So the local residual lane is now very rigid:

- no “spray” of small symmetric recurrences can emanate from one center unless the branch is locally flat.

That is a real local sterility theorem.

---

## 6. What remains

This still does not fully close the lane, because a bad branch could in principle use:

- nonsymmetric intervals,
- or symmetric intervals with **moving centers** \(m_n\to m_0\).

So the next live local target is sharper now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{rule out moving-center symmetric recurrences.}
}
\]

That is, analyze a sequence
\[
(m_n,L_n),\qquad L_n\to 0,
\]
with
\[
D(m_n,L_n)=0,\qquad J(m_n,L_n)=0,
\]
and show it still forces \(K\equiv 0\) locally or some equally impossible flatness.

That is the natural next step from here.

So the sharp update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed-center local clouds of symmetric common tangents are impossible unless }K\equiv 0.
}
\]

That is the strongest clean continuation I can justify now.
## User (2026-04-21 12:37:54)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:39:31)

_Thought for 1m 30s_

Here is the cleanest next push I can justify.

The moving-center symmetric problem reduces to a **single analytic elimination problem**.

That is sharper than the earlier fixed-center sterility theorem.

---

## 1. Package symmetric common tangents into two analytic equations

For a symmetric interval \([m-L,m+L]\), define
\[
D(m,L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L),
\]
and
\[
J(m,L):=\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}2.
\]

A symmetric common tangent is exactly
\[
D(m,L)=0,\qquad J(m,L)=0,\qquad L>0.
\]

Because \(D\) is odd in \(L\) and \(J\) is even, write
\[
z:=L^2.
\]
Then there are analytic functions \(A(m,z)\), \(B(m,z)\) such that
\[
D(m,L)=2L\,A(m,L^2),
\qquad
J(m,L)=-\frac{L^2}{6}\,B(m,L^2),
\]
with expansions
\[
A(m,z)=N'(m)+\frac16 N^{(3)}(m)\,z+\frac1{120}N^{(5)}(m)\,z^2+\cdots,
\]
\[
B(m,z)=N''(m)+\frac1{20}N^{(4)}(m)\,z+\frac1{840}N^{(6)}(m)\,z^2+\cdots.
\]

So symmetric common tangent is exactly
\[
\boxed{
A(m,z)=0,\qquad B(m,z)=0,\qquad z>0.
}
\]

This is the key exact reformulation.

---

## 2. Fixed-center sterility becomes \(z\)-fiber sterility

The earlier fixed-center theorem now reads:

> for fixed \(m\), if there are infinitely many \(z_n\downarrow 0\) with
> \[
> A(m,z_n)=B(m,z_n)=0,
> \]
> then \(N\) is locally constant near \(m\).

So for each fixed center, the \(z\)-fiber is locally sterile unless the branch is flat.

That part is done.

---

## 3. The moving-center problem is now an elimination problem

What remains is the case where
\[
(m_n,z_n)\to(m_0,0),
\qquad z_n>0,
\qquad
A(m_n,z_n)=B(m_n,z_n)=0,
\]
with \(m_n\) moving.

This is no longer a geometric problem about secants or tangents. It is an analytic common-zero problem for the pair
\[
A(m,z),\qquad B(m,z).
\]

Generically, two analytic equations in two variables have only isolated common zeros. So a local cloud of moving-center symmetric recurrences is already highly nongeneric.

That gives the right new theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that the analytic pair }(A,B)\textbf{ has no nontrivial common branch near }(m_0,0).
}
\]

---

## 4. The resultant reduction

This can be phrased as a one-variable obstruction.

By Weierstrass preparation/elimination, there is a local analytic resultant
\[
R(m)
\]
such that, near \((m_0,0)\),

- if \(R(m)\neq 0\), then there is no small \(z\) with
  \[
  A(m,z)=B(m,z)=0;
  \]
- if \(R(m)=0\), then \(m\) is a potential center of a small symmetric common tangent.

So the moving-center problem reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{prove that the center-resultant }R(m)\textbf{ is not identically zero near any relevant }m_0.
}
\]

This is much sharper than anything we had before.

It says the only way a local cloud of moving-center symmetric recurrences can survive is if the odd-channel obstruction \(A\) and even-channel obstruction \(B\) satisfy a genuine analytic dependence in \(z\).

That is a very rigid condition.

---

## 5. First necessary conditions from the reduced system

At \(z=0\),
\[
A(m,0)=N'(m),\qquad B(m,0)=N''(m).
\]

So any accumulation point \(m_0\) of moving centers must satisfy
\[
\boxed{
N'(m_0)=0,\qquad N''(m_0)=0.
}
\]

This recovers the earlier stationary-inflection condition.

But now the new point is: that is only the **zeroth-order** condition for the analytic common-zero problem. The real local obstruction is the failure of \(A\) and \(B\) to share a common \(z\)-branch.

So the next live invariant is not \(N'\) or \(N''\) alone. It is the local analytic dependence class of the pair \((A,B)\).

---

## 6. Why this is useful

This is the best local reduction I can currently justify because it separates the problem into two levels:

First level:
\[
N'(m_0)=N''(m_0)=0.
\]

Second level:
even if that happens, a moving-center cloud still requires the nontrivial analytic compatibility
\[
A(m,z)\ \text{and}\ B(m,z)\ \text{share a common small-}z\text{ branch}.
\]

So the remaining local theorem is now very crisp:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{provenance/mixed structure must rule out common-branch dependence of }A\textbf{ and }B.
}
\]

That is a better target than trying to control \(K\), \(K'\), or Jensen gaps directly.

---

## 7. The next attack from here

So my next attack would be:

1. treat
   \[
   A(m,z),\qquad B(m,z)
   \]
   as the canonical odd/even obstruction pair for symmetric quotient-cusp recurrence;

2. look for an external mixed/provenance law implying that their leading \(z\)-coefficients cannot satisfy the analytic dependence needed for a common branch;

3. equivalently, look for any external theorem forcing the local resultant
   \[
   R(m)
   \]
   to be nonzero near relevant points.

In other words, the local quotient-cusp lane is now reduced to one precise algebraic-analytic obstruction:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{no common analytic \(z\)-branch for }A(m,z)=B(m,z)=0.
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:44:31)

_Thought for 1m 39s_

I found a much stronger local theorem.

It kills not only the **fixed-center** symmetric cloud, but also the **moving-center** symmetric cloud.

The result is:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{if a nontrivial analytic }N\textbf{ has a sequence of symmetric common-tangent intervals }(m_n,L_n)
\textbf{ with }L_n\to 0,\ m_n\to m_0,
\textbf{ then }N\textbf{ must be locally constant near }m_0.}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a nontrivial quotient-cusp branch cannot support any local cloud of symmetric common tangents, even with moving centers.}
}
\]

This is much stronger than the earlier “order-\(\ge 4\) zero of \(K\)” necessary condition.

---

## 1. Start from the exact symmetric obstruction pair

For a symmetric interval \([m-L,m+L]\), define
\[
D(m,L):=N(m+L)-N(m-L),
\]
and
\[
J(m,L):=\frac{1}{2L}\int_{m-L}^{m+L}N(x)\,dx-\frac{N(m-L)+N(m+L)}2.
\]

A symmetric common tangent is exactly
\[
D(m,L)=0,\qquad J(m,L)=0,\qquad L>0.
\]

As before, write
\[
z:=L^2.
\]
Then there are analytic functions \(A(m,z)\), \(B(m,z)\) such that
\[
D(m,L)=2L\,A(m,L^2),
\qquad
J(m,L)=-\frac{L^2}{6}\,B(m,L^2),
\]
with expansions
\[
A(m,z)=N'(m)+\frac16 N^{(3)}(m)\,z+\frac1{120}N^{(5)}(m)\,z^2+\cdots,
\]
\[
B(m,z)=N''(m)+\frac1{20}N^{(4)}(m)\,z+\frac1{840}N^{(6)}(m)\,z^2+\cdots.
\]

So symmetric common tangent is exactly
\[
\boxed{
A(m,z)=0,\qquad B(m,z)=0,\qquad z>0.
}
\]

---

## 2. Let \(m_n\to m_0\), \(z_n\to 0^+\) be a sequence of symmetric common tangents

Then by continuity,
\[
A(m_0,0)=N'(m_0)=0,
\qquad
B(m_0,0)=N''(m_0)=0.
\]

Now suppose \(N\) is not locally constant near \(m_0\), and let
\[
j\ge 3
\]
be the smallest index with
\[
a_j:=N^{(j)}(m_0)\neq 0.
\]

Set
\[
\delta:=m-m_0.
\]

We now examine the pair \((A,B)\) using that minimal \(j\).

---

## 3. Case \(j=3\): impossible by the Jacobian

If \(N^{(3)}(m_0)\neq 0\), then
\[
A_z(m_0,0)=\frac16 N^{(3)}(m_0)\neq 0,
\qquad
B_m(m_0,0)=N^{(3)}(m_0)\neq 0.
\]
Also
\[
A_m(m_0,0)=N''(m_0)=0,
\qquad
B_z(m_0,0)=\frac1{20}N^{(4)}(m_0).
\]

So the Jacobian is
\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
A_m & A_z\\
B_m & B_z
\end{pmatrix}_{(m_0,0)}
=
-\frac16\bigl(N^{(3)}(m_0)\bigr)^2\neq 0.
\]

Hence \((m_0,0)\) is an isolated common zero of \((A,B)\), so there cannot be a sequence
\[
(m_n,z_n)\to(m_0,0),\qquad z_n>0,
\]
of other common zeros.

So \(j=3\) is impossible.

---

## 4. Case \(j\ge 4\): the \(B=0\) equation forces \(z<0\)

Using minimality of \(j\),
\[
N''(m)
=
\frac{a_j}{(j-2)!}\,\delta^{\,j-2}+O(\delta^{\,j-1}),
\]
and
\[
N^{(4)}(m)
=
\frac{a_j}{(j-4)!}\,\delta^{\,j-4}+O(\delta^{\,j-3})
\qquad (j\ge 4).
\]

So
\[
B(m,z)
=
\frac{a_j}{(j-2)!}\delta^{\,j-2}
+
\frac{a_j}{20\,(j-4)!}\delta^{\,j-4}z
+
O\!\left(\delta^{\,j-1}\right)
+
O\!\left(\delta^{\,j-3}z\right)
+
O(z^2).
\]

Factor \(\delta^{\,j-4}\) (for \(\delta\neq 0\)):
\[
B(m,z)
=
\delta^{\,j-4}
\left[
\frac{a_j}{(j-2)!}\delta^2
+
\frac{a_j}{20\,(j-4)!}z
+
o(\delta^2+z)
\right].
\]

If \(B(m,z)=0\), this gives the asymptotic relation
\[
z
=
-20\,\frac{(j-4)!}{(j-2)!}\,\delta^2
+
o(\delta^2)
=
-\frac{20}{(j-2)(j-3)}\,\delta^2
+
o(\delta^2).
\]

But the leading coefficient is **negative**, so for \(|\delta|\) small this forces
\[
z<0,
\]
contradicting
\[
z=L^2>0.
\]

So \(j\ge 4\) is also impossible.

---

## 5. Conclusion

Every possibility for the first nonzero derivative after \(N''\) leads to a contradiction.

Therefore there is no such minimal \(j\), which means
\[
N^{(j)}(m_0)=0
\qquad
\forall j\ge 1.
\]

Since \(N\) is analytic, this implies
\[
\boxed{
N \text{ is locally constant near }m_0.
}
\]

That proves the theorem.

---

## 6. Translate back to the quotient-cusp geometry

Recall
\[
N(x)=M_1(\sqrt x),\qquad
N'(x)=\frac12 K(\sqrt x).
\]

If \(N\) is locally constant, then
\[
K\equiv 0
\]
near the corresponding point of the quotient branch, so the branch is locally affine in the \(x\)-variable, not a genuine nontrivial quartic–sextic residual cusp.

So the geometric conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a genuine quotient-cusp branch cannot support any local cloud of symmetric common tangents.}
}
\]

Not at a fixed center, and not with moving centers.

---

## 7. What this means for the next attack

This is the strongest local sterility theorem we have gotten on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane.

So any surviving obstruction on that lane must now be:

- **nonsymmetric** in the quotient coordinate, or
- genuinely **nonlocal/global** on the quotient branch.

That is a big narrowing.

So the next live theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{rule out nonsymmetric quotient-cusp common tangents / returns.}
}
\]

Because the whole symmetric local lane is now dead unless the branch is locally flat.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the current reduction.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:49:26)

_Thought for 1m 36s_

Here is a genuinely stronger local sterility theorem for the quotient-cusp lane.

It kills not only **symmetric** local clouds of common tangents, but **all** local clouds of common tangents between nearby points on the quotient branch.

---

## Theorem: local common-tangent sterility on the quotient cusp branch

Let
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u\ge 0,
\]
be the quotient-cusp branch, and define the tangent slope
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u}.
\]

For \(0<u<v\), write
\[
H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)\bigl(x(v)-x(u)\bigr).
\]
Then a common tangent at \(u\) and \(v\) is exactly
\[
T(v)=T(u),
\qquad
H(u,v)=0.
\]

Assume \(u_0>0\) and \(T\) is analytic near \(u_0\). If there exists a sequence
\[
u_n<v_n,\qquad u_n\to u_0,\ v_n\to u_0,
\]
such that
\[
T(v_n)=T(u_n),\qquad H(u_n,v_n)=0
\]
for all \(n\), then \(T\) is locally constant near \(u_0\).

Equivalently, on a nontrivial analytic quotient-cusp branch, there is no local cloud of nearby common tangents accumulating at an interior point.

---

## Proof sketch

Set
\[
u=u_0-a,\qquad v=u_0+b,
\qquad a,b>0,\qquad a,b\to 0.
\]

Assume \(T\) is not locally constant, and let \(j\ge 1\) be the first index with
\[
T^{(j)}(u_0)\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
T(u_0+t)=T_0+c\,t^j+O(t^{j+1}),
\qquad c\neq 0.
\]

### Step 1: equal-slope condition forces \(j\) even and asymptotic symmetry

From
\[
T(v)=T(u)
\]
we get
\[
c\bigl(b^j-(-a)^j\bigr)+O(a^{j+1}+b^{j+1})=0.
\]

- If \(j\) is odd, then
  \[
  b^j-(-a)^j=b^j+a^j>0,
  \]
  so equality is impossible for \(a,b>0\) sufficiently small.

- Hence \(j\) must be even, and then
  \[
  b^j-a^j=O(a^{j+1}+b^{j+1}),
  \]
  which implies
  \[
  b=a+O(a^2).
  \]

So any local equal-slope pair is asymptotically symmetric about \(u_0\).

### Step 2: tangent-through-second-point has definite sign

Now expand
\[
H(u,v)=2\int_u^v s\bigl(T(s)-T(u)\bigr)\,ds.
\]
With \(s=u_0+t\), this is
\[
H(u,v)=2\int_{-a}^{b}(u_0+t)\bigl(T(u_0+t)-T(u_0-a)\bigr)\,dt.
\]

Using the expansion of \(T\) and the fact that \(j\) is even,
\[
T(u_0+t)-T(u_0-a)=c\,(t^j-a^j)+O(a^{j+1}+|t|^{j+1}).
\]
Hence
\[
H(u,v)=2c\int_{-a}^{b}(u_0+t)(t^j-a^j)\,dt+O(a^{j+2}).
\]

Since \(b=a+O(a^2)\), the leading term is
\[
2c\,u_0\int_{-a}^{a}(t^j-a^j)\,dt+O(a^{j+2}).
\]
Because \(j\) is even,
\[
t^j-a^j\le 0
\quad\text{on }[-a,a],
\]
with strict inequality except at \(t=\pm a\). Thus
\[
\int_{-a}^{a}(t^j-a^j)\,dt
=
-\frac{2j}{j+1}a^{j+1},
\]
so
\[
H(u,v)=-\frac{4c\,u_0\,j}{j+1}a^{j+1}+O(a^{j+2}).
\]

Therefore \(H(u,v)\) has a definite nonzero sign for all sufficiently small equal-slope pairs, contradicting
\[
H(u,v)=0.
\]

So no such sequence can exist unless \(T\) is locally constant. ∎

---

## Consequences

Since
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u),
\]
this is equivalent to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if nearby common tangents accumulate at }u_0>0,\text{ then }M_1\text{ is locally constant near }u_0.
}
\]

And since
\[
T'(u)=2u\,K(u),
\]
local constancy of \(T\) implies
\[
K\equiv 0
\]
near \(u_0\), so the branch is locally affine in the \(x=u^2\) variable.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a genuine quartic–sextic quotient-cusp branch cannot generate any local cloud of nearby two-point recurrences away from the cusp point.}
}
\]

---

## What remains

This is stronger than the earlier symmetric-center sterility theorem.

It means the entire **local** quotient-cusp recurrence lane is dead:

- no fixed-center cloud,
- no moving-center symmetric cloud,
- and now no arbitrary nearby common-tangent cloud at all.

So any surviving exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) obstruction must now be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{purely global/nonlocal along the quotient branch.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the current reduction.
## User (2026-04-21 12:51:06)

continue the math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:51:38)

_Thought for 25s_

Yes. There is a real next theorem now, and it is stronger than just “local clouds are impossible.”

The exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) quotient-cusp lane is now reduced to a **discrete global-return problem**.

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{\textbf{away from locally affine pieces, common tangent pairs on the quotient cusp branch form a discrete set.}}
\]

And on every compact subarc, there are only finitely many such pairs.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify from the local sterility theorem.

---

## 1. The analytic common-tangent map

On the quotient cusp branch
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u>0,
\]
define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u},
\]
and for \(0<u<v\),
\[
H(u,v):=y(v)-y(u)-T(u)\bigl(x(v)-x(u)\bigr).
\]

A common tangent at \(u\) and \(v\) is exactly
\[
T(v)-T(u)=0,
\qquad
H(u,v)=0.
\]

So define the analytic map
\[
F(u,v):=\bigl(T(v)-T(u),\,H(u,v)\bigr)
\]
on the open wedge
\[
\Omega:=\{(u,v):0<u<v\}.
\]

Then the common-tangent set is
\[
\mathcal C:=\{(u,v)\in\Omega:F(u,v)=0\}.
\]

---

## 2. Local sterility implies discreteness

From the previous theorem:

> if \((u_n,v_n)\in\mathcal C\) with
> \[
> u_n\to u_0,\qquad v_n\to u_0,
> \]
> then \(T\) is locally constant near \(u_0\), hence \(K\equiv 0\) there.

So on any interval where the quotient branch is genuinely nontrivial,
\[
K\not\equiv 0,
\]
there is **no** accumulation of \(\mathcal C\) onto the diagonal \(u=v\).

A second easy observation is that if
\[
(u_n,v_n)\in\mathcal C,\qquad (u_n,v_n)\to(u_0,v_0)\in\Omega,
\]
then by analyticity
\[
F(u_0,v_0)=0.
\]
So any accumulation inside \(\Omega\) would give a positive-dimensional analytic zero set through \((u_0,v_0)\). But a local analytic branch of zeros through \((u_0,v_0)\) would, by following it toward \(v-u\to 0\), contradict the local sterility theorem unless \(T\) were locally constant on one side. Thus on a genuinely non-affine quotient branch, \(\mathcal C\) has no interior accumulation either.

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{if }K\not\equiv 0\textbf{ on a quotient-cusp arc, then }\mathcal C\textbf{ is discrete in }\Omega.
}
\]

And therefore, on every compact rectangle
\[
\{(u,v):u_0\le u<v\le v_0\}\subset\Omega,
\]
the set \(\mathcal C\) is finite.

---

## 3. This gives a finite-core reduction on the quotient cusp

So the residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane is now **not** a local instability problem. It is a finite global-return problem.

On any compact quotient-cusp branch away from locally affine pieces, there are only finitely many candidate common tangent pairs.

That means the whole residual lane now reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{rule out isolated global tangent returns on the one-sided quotient cusp branch.}
}
\]

This is a real simplification. It says there is no continuum of bad pairs, no bubbling, no nearby re-entry cloud. Any surviving obstruction is a finite combinatorial object.

That is exactly the kind of reduction that could potentially be fed into the finite-core / first-return style arguments from elsewhere in the draft.

---

## 4. A sharper scalar reduction on the discrete set

We can also package the discrete return problem in the \(M_1\)-language.

Recall
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u),\qquad
M_1(u):=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds.
\]

Then common tangent is equivalent to
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=:m
\]
and
\[
m=\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]

So isolated common tangent pairs are exactly isolated pairs \((u,v)\) where:

- \(M_1\) takes the same value at \(u\) and \(v\),
- and that common value equals the \(s\)-weighted average of \(M_1\) on \([u,v]\).

Since the bad set is discrete, the quotient-cusp lane has now become an **isolated weighted-Jensen return problem** for one scalar function \(M_1\).

That is probably the best exact scalar reduction available.

---

## 5. What the next theorem should look like

The next live theorem is now much more concrete than before.

It should be one of these:

### A. No-first-return theorem for \(M_1\)
Show that if \(u<v\) is the first pair with
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v),
\]
then the weighted-average condition automatically fails.

That would kill all isolated returns.

### B. Global one-sided law for the Jensen return functional
Define
\[
\mathcal J(u,v):=
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds-M_1(u)
\]
on the equal-value locus
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v).
\]
Then common tangent is exactly
\[
\mathcal J(u,v)=0.
\]
A theorem proving \(\mathcal J(u,v)\) has one sign on every connected component of the equal-value locus would close the lane.

### C. Finite-core contradiction
Since the bad set is finite on compact arcs, one may be able to choose a first/outermost/nearest bad pair and derive a contradiction by minimality.

That would connect nicely to the finite-core extraction style you wanted other chats to pursue.

---

## 6. My next attack from here

I would now stop looking for more local normal forms on this lane.

The right next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{analyze the first isolated global return pair on the quotient cusp branch.}
}
\]

In practice, I would try to prove something like:

> let \((u_*,v_*)\) be the isolated common tangent pair minimizing \(v-u\) or minimizing \(v\) among all bad pairs. Then the weighted-Jensen identity for \((u_*,v_*)\) contradicts the one-sided quotient-cusp geometry between \(u_*\) and \(v_*\).

That is the first genuinely new theorem target after the local sterility result.

So the sharp update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ residual lane has been reduced from a local instability problem to a finite isolated-return problem on a one-sided quotient cusp branch.}
}
\]

That is the best continuation I can justify now.

continue the math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:54:30)

I’m pushing on the “isolated return pair” picture now. The natural next move is a first-return obstruction for the scalar function \(M_1\), because common tangents on the quotient cusp were already reduced to weighted-average equalities along equal-value pairs.

_Thought for 3m 19s_

I can push it one more clean step, and this is the sharpest **global discrete reduction** on the quotient-cusp lane.

Once local clouds are ruled out, the remaining common-tangent problem on the quotient cusp is exactly a **repeated-value problem in a finite alternating sequence**.

That is better than the earlier integral/barycenter formulations, because it reduces the residual lane to a finite combinatorial object for each slope level.

---

## 1. Fix a slope level

On the quotient cusp branch
\[
\Gamma(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u^2,\widetilde G(u)),\qquad u>0,
\]
define
\[
T(u):=\frac{y'(u)}{x'(u)}=\frac{\widetilde G'(u)}{2u}.
\]

Fix a slope value \(m\), and consider the level set
\[
Z_m:=\{u>0:\ T(u)=m\}.
\]

On any compact quotient-cusp arc where the branch is genuinely non-affine, the local sterility theorem implies there are no accumulations of common-tangent pairs onto the diagonal. In particular, for a fixed \(m\), the set \(Z_m\) is discrete, hence finite on compact arcs. Order it as
\[
u_0<u_1<\cdots<u_k.
\]

Now define the affine-support function at slope \(m\):
\[
R_m(u):=y(u)-m\,x(u).
\]

Then
\[
R_m'(u)=y'(u)-m x'(u)=2u\,(T(u)-m).
\]

So on each interval \((u_j,u_{j+1})\), the sign of \(R_m'\) is the sign of \(T-m\), and therefore \(R_m\) is strictly monotone there.

---

## 2. Common tangent pairs are exactly repeated values of \(R_m\) on \(Z_m\)

A common tangent of slope \(m\) at two points \(u_i<u_j\) means:

- \(T(u_i)=T(u_j)=m\), so \(u_i,u_j\in Z_m\);
- the tangent line at \(u_i\) passes through \(u_j\), equivalently
  \[
  y(u_j)-y(u_i)=m\,(x(u_j)-x(u_i)).
  \]

That second condition is exactly
\[
R_m(u_j)=R_m(u_i).
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{common tangent pairs of slope }m
\iff
\text{repeated values in the finite sequence }
a_j(m):=R_m(u_j).
}
\]

This is the cleanest discrete reduction so far.

---

## 3. The alternating increment structure

Define the adjacent increments
\[
\Delta_j(m):=a_{j+1}(m)-a_j(m)
=
\int_{u_j}^{u_{j+1}} R_m'(u)\,du
=
\int_{u_j}^{u_{j+1}}2u\,(T(u)-m)\,du.
\]

Since \(T(u)-m\) changes sign at each consecutive zero \(u_j\), the signs of the increments alternate:
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta_j(m) = -\,\operatorname{sgn}\Delta_{j+1}(m).
\]

Thus for each fixed slope \(m\), the residual quotient-cusp geometry is encoded by a finite alternating-sign sequence
\[
\Delta_0(m),\Delta_1(m),\dots,\Delta_{k-1}(m),
\]
and common tangent pairs are exactly zero-sum consecutive blocks:
\[
a_j(m)=a_i(m)
\iff
\sum_{p=i}^{j-1}\Delta_p(m)=0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the quotient-cusp common-tangent problem is a finite zero-sum problem for an alternating-sign increment sequence.}
}
\]

That is a genuinely cleaner finite-core reduction.

---

## 4. Immediate consequences

This gives several exact structural facts.

### (a) No local clouds
Already proved: no accumulation on the diagonal unless the branch is locally affine.

### (b) Every surviving obstruction is finite
On a compact quotient-cusp arc, for each fixed slope \(m\), there are only finitely many candidate tangent-return pairs.

### (c) First-return formulation
If \(u_i<u_j\) is a bad pair of slope \(m\), then it is exactly a minimal or nonminimal zero-sum consecutive block in the alternating increment sequence.

So the next theorem does not need to talk about the full geometry anymore. It can talk about:

> can the actual provenance-driven quotient cusp produce a zero-sum consecutive block in this alternating sequence?

That is now a finite combinatorial question for each slope level.

---

## 5. Why this is useful

This is the best bridge yet between the local sterility theorem and the “finite bad core” style arguments.

The residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane is no longer:

- a local normal form problem,
- or a cloud/compactness problem,
- or even directly a curvature-moment problem.

It is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{for each slope }m,\textbf{ rule out repeated values in the finite sequence }a_j(m)=R_m(u_j).
}
\]

So the next live theorem could be any one of these:

- a monotonicity theorem for the sequence \(a_j(m)\);
- a one-sided size theorem for the alternating increments \(\Delta_j(m)\);
- a first-zero-sum-block impossibility theorem;
- or a provenance law forcing the partial sums
  \[
  a_j(m)-a_0(m)=\sum_{p=0}^{j-1}\Delta_p(m)
  \]
  never to return.

That is much more discrete and potentially attackable than the earlier integral identities.

---

## 6. The next attack from here

So my next attack would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{analyze the first zero-sum consecutive block in the alternating increment sequence }\Delta_j(m).
}
\]

Concretely:

- let \(m\) be a slope with at least one repeated value of \(a_j(m)\);
- choose a shortest zero-sum consecutive block
  \[
  \Delta_i+\Delta_{i+1}+\cdots+\Delta_{j-1}=0;
  \]
- then try to show the actual quotient-cusp/provenance geometry forbids that shortest block.

This is now a finite combinatorial/analytic target, and it is the first one after local sterility that looks genuinely plausible to attack.

So the sharp update is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ residual lane has reduced to a finite alternating-sequence return problem on each slope level.}
}
\]

That is the best continuation I can justify now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 12:59:54)

_Thought for 27s_

I can push it one more clean step.

The right next reduction is a **first-return theorem** for the critical-value sequence
\[
a_j(m):=R_m(u_j),
\qquad
R_m(u):=y(u)-m\,x(u),
\]
where
\[
u_0<u_1<\cdots<u_k
\]
are the points with
\[
T(u_j)=m.
\]

This is sharper than the earlier “alternating increment sequence” language.

---

## 1. Common tangents are repeated critical values

We already reduced the quotient-cusp common-tangent problem at slope \(m\) to:

- \(u_j\in Z_m:=\{T=m\}\),
- and a bad pair is exactly
  \[
  a_i(m)=a_j(m)
  \qquad (i<j).
  \]

Also the adjacent increments
\[
\Delta_j(m):=a_{j+1}(m)-a_j(m)
\]
alternate in sign.

So the remaining bad geometry at a fixed slope \(m\) is exactly a repeated value in the finite critical-value sequence
\[
a_0,a_1,\dots,a_k.
\]

---

## 2. Shortest repeated-value blocks are automatically “clean first returns”

Take a bad pair \(i<j\) with
\[
a_i=a_j.
\]
Choose one with minimal gap \(j-i\).

Then automatically:

\[
\boxed{
a_p\neq a_i \quad \text{for every } i<p<j.
}
\]

More strongly, every interior value lies strictly on one side of the repeated endpoint value.

Indeed, if there were interior indices \(p,q\) with
\[
a_p>a_i,\qquad a_q<a_i,
\]
then because consecutive increments alternate sign, the sequence of values passes from one side of \(a_i\) to the other. The first crossing back through level \(a_i\) would occur on a shorter consecutive block, contradicting minimality of \(j-i\).

So for a shortest repeated-value block, either
\[
a_p>a_i \qquad (i<p<j),
\]
for all interior indices, or
\[
a_p<a_i \qquad (i<p<j),
\]
for all interior indices.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a shortest common-tangent pair is a clean first return of the critical-value sequence to a level, with all interior critical values strictly on one side.}
}
\]

That is the key combinatorial reduction.

---

## 3. Geometric meaning

At slope \(m\), the function \(R_m\) has critical points at the \(u_j\), and the values \(a_j\) are their heights.

So a shortest bad pair means:

- two critical points \(u_i<u_j\) of \(R_m\) have the same height;
- every critical point in between has height strictly above that common height, or strictly below it.

Equivalently, on \([u_i,u_j]\), the graph of \(R_m\) forms a **single nested cap** or **single nested cup** above or below the return level.

So the residual quotient-cusp lane has now reduced from arbitrary repeated critical values to one of two model pictures:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cap-type first return}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\text{cup-type first return.}
}
\]

That is much more rigid than a general repeated-value pattern.

---

## 4. Consequence for the scalar \(M_1\)

Since
\[
R_m'(u)=2u\,(T(u)-m)=4u\,(M_1(u)-m/2),
\]
the zeros \(u_j\) are exactly the level-set points of \(M_1\):
\[
M_1(u_j)=m/2.
\]

So a shortest bad pair is exactly a shortest repeated level of \(M_1\) such that all intermediate critical values of \(R_m\) lie on one side of the return level.

In particular, the equal-value set
\[
M_1(u)=m/2
\]
on \([u_i,u_j]\) looks like a clean first return:
- the level is hit at \(u_i\) and \(u_j\),
- and the weighted-average condition holds:
  \[
  \frac{2}{u_j^2-u_i^2}\int_{u_i}^{u_j} s\,M_1(s)\,ds = M_1(u_i)=M_1(u_j).
  \]

So the next theorem does not need to rule out arbitrary oscillation of \(M_1\). It only needs to rule out this **clean first-return weighted-average equality**.

That is a real simplification.

---

## 5. Exact first-return criterion

Let \(u<v\) be a first return for the level \(m/2\), meaning
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v)=m/2,
\]
and for \(u<s<v\),
either
\[
M_1(s)>m/2 \quad\text{for all }s\in(u,v),
\]
or
\[
M_1(s)<m/2 \quad\text{for all }s\in(u,v).
\]

Then a common tangent exists exactly if
\[
\boxed{
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds = \frac{m}{2}.
}
\]

But now, because the weight \(s\) is strictly positive on \((u,v)\), this equality is impossible under strict one-sidedness:

- if \(M_1(s)>m/2\) for all \(u<s<v\), then the weighted average is strictly greater than \(m/2\);
- if \(M_1(s)<m/2\) for all \(u<s<v\), then the weighted average is strictly less than \(m/2\).

So we get the decisive theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{a clean first return of }M_1\textbf{ can never be a common tangent pair.}
}
\]

That is the strongest discrete reduction so far.

---

## 6. Final consequence

Combine this with the previous minimal-gap reduction:

- any bad pair yields a shortest bad pair;
- any shortest bad pair is a clean first return;
- but a clean first return cannot satisfy the weighted-average equality.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{there are no common tangent pairs at all on the quotient-cusp branch.}
}
\]

At least for the residual one-sided quotient-cusp model we have been analyzing.

This is the first place in this lane where I think the attack actually closes, because the weighted-average identity and the strict one-sidedness of a first return are directly incompatible.

---

## 7. What I would check next in the draft

The thing to verify carefully against the paper’s exact setup is that the residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane really does reduce to a **single one-sided quotient branch** on which this critical-value/first-return argument applies without hidden multi-branch complications.

If that check passes, then this is not just another reduction. It is likely the actual closure of the residual quotient-cusp lane.

_Thought for 3m 1s_

I found an important correction.

My previous “shortest repeated-value block \(\Rightarrow\) clean first return \(\Rightarrow\) no common tangent” step is **false**. A shortest repeated critical-value block can still have interior critical values on both sides of the repeated level, so the first-return argument does not close the quotient-cusp lane.

And in fact the bare quotient-cusp model admits explicit common tangents.

Take
\[
M_1(u):=u(u-1)(u-2)(23u-36).
\]
Then
\[
M_1(0)=0,\qquad M_1'(0)=-72\neq 0,
\]
so it defines a genuine quartic–sextic quotient cusp via
\[
x(u)=u^2,\qquad
T(u)=2M_1(u),\qquad
y(u)=4\int_0^u s\,M_1(s)\,ds.
\]
Near \(u=0\),
\[
y(u)=-96u^3+O(u^4),
\]
so this is the same one-sided cusp-type branch we have been analyzing.

Now
\[
M_1(1)=M_1(2)=0,
\]
hence
\[
T(1)=T(2)=0.
\]
Also
\[
\frac{2}{2^2-1^2}\int_1^2 s\,M_1(s)\,ds=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
y(2)-y(1)=0.
\]
So the horizontal line \(y=y(1)=y(2)\) is tangent at both \(u=1\) and \(u=2\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuine quotient-cusp branch can have isolated common tangent pairs.}
}
\]

That means the quotient-cusp lane is **not closed** by local geometry alone.

What *is* true from the earlier work is still useful:

- no local cloud of nearby common tangents can accumulate at an interior point unless the branch is locally affine;
- hence on a nontrivial quotient-cusp arc, common tangent pairs are a **discrete** set.

So the lane has reduced to a discrete global-return problem, not vanished.

The right scalar is still
\[
M_1(u)=\int_0^u sK(s)\,ds,
\]
with
\[
T(u)=2M_1(u).
\]
A common tangent pair \(u<v\) is exactly
\[
M_1(u)=M_1(v)
\]
together with
\[
\mathcal J(u,v):=
\frac{2}{v^2-u^2}\int_u^v s\,M_1(s)\,ds-M_1(u)=0.
\]

So the sharp next target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{on the actual provenance-driven }K_v=0\textbf{ quotient branch, prove a sign theorem for }\mathcal J(u,v)
\textbf{ on the equal-value locus }M_1(u)=M_1(v).
}
\]

That is the residual problem in its cleanest form.

So the updated status is:

- local quotient-cusp recurrence clouds are dead;
- isolated quotient-cusp returns are **abstractly realizable**;
- the remaining job is an **external provenance/mixed theorem** ruling out zeros of \(\mathcal J\) on the equal-value locus.

That is the next attack I would push.
## User (2026-04-21 13:06:50)
